Senate of the State of Cal. v. Jones


No. S083194.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


21 Cal. 4th 1142; 988 P.2d 1089; 90 Cal. Rptr. 2d 810; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 8162; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9729; 99 Daily Journal DAR 12508



December 13, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  A peremptory writ of mandate shall issue, directing respondents to refrain from taking any steps to place Proposition 24 on the March 7, 2000, election ballot or to include the measure in the ballot pamphlet.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners filed an original proceeding seeking a writ of mandate or prohibition to restrain respondents from placing an initiative measure on the election ballot or in the ballot pamphlet, maintaining that the Proposition was invalid on three grounds, including a claim that the measure violates the single-subject rule in Cal. Const. art. II, § 8(d).


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, the California Senate and the Superintendent of Public Instruction, sought a writ of mandate or prohibition to restrain respondents, Secretary of State and county Registrar of Voters, from placing an initiative measure on the election ballot or in the ballot pamphlet, maintaining that the proposition was invalid on three grounds. The court found that the challenged measure violated the single-subject rule of Cal. Const. art. II, § 8(d) because the provisions of the initiative measure were not "reasonably germane" to a single subject, but instead embraced at least two separate and unrelated subjects. The combination of provisions addressing the separate subjects and seeking to accomplish diverse objectives clearly violated the language and purpose of the single-subject limitation.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing respondents to refrain from taking any steps to place the proposition on the election ballot or to include the measure in the ballot pamphlet because the initiative measure violated the single-subject rule.

 

2 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Broughton v. Cigna Healthplans


No. S072583.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


21 Cal. 4th 1066; 988 P.2d 67; 90 Cal. Rptr. 2d 334; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 8005; 99 Daily Journal DAR 9431; 99 Daily Journal DAR 12141; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9431



December 2, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BC117680. Ronald E. Cappai, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For all of the foregoing, we affirm the judgment of the Court of Appeal in part and reverse in part, and remand the cause for proceedings consistent with this opinion.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed the order of the Court of Appeals (California), which denied arbitration of plaintiffs' medical malpractice suit seeking injunctive relief and damages under the Consumer Legal Remedies Acts (CLRA), Cal. Civ. Code § 1750 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, a minor and his mother, sued defendant health maintenance organization for damages and injunctive relief in their medical malpractice action, arising under the Consumer Legal Remedies Acts (CLRA), Cal. Civ. Code § 1750 et seq. Specifically, plaintiffs alleged defendant engaged in deceptive advertising practices. Defendant moved to compel arbitration. On appeal, the court concluded that an action for damages under the CLRA was fully arbitrable and was severable from an injunctive relief action. However, the court held that the CLRA injunctive relief action was not subject to arbitration. The court reasoned that the purpose of arbitration was to voluntarily resolve private disputes. Here, the CLRA plaintiff was functioning as a private attorney general, enjoining future deceptive practices on behalf of the general public. The court held that under such circumstances arbitration was not a suitable forum.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part; plaintiffs' action for damages under the Consumer Legal Remedies Acts, was amenable to arbitration, but her action for injunctive relief was not.

 

3 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Miller v. Superior Court


No. S073888.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


21 Cal. 4th 883; 986 P.2d 170; 89 Cal. Rptr. 2d 834; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7198; 28 Media L. Rep. 1161; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8763; 99 Daily Journal DAR 11183



November 1, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin. Super. Ct. No. 59994. William J. Murray, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: For all the foregoing reasons, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed and the cause remanded to that court with directions to cause issuance of a peremptory writ of prohibition as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed from Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District, which upheld the trial court's contempt order, denied a writ of prohibition, and lifted the stay on an order that denied petitioner's motion to quash an order that directed the unsealing of a news videotape.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner conducted a videotaped interview with a person suspected of killing another. Portions of the interview were broadcast on the television news. Respondents issued a subpoena for the entire interview. The petitioner submitted only the previously broadcast portions of the interview, invoking the shield law, Cal. Const. art. I § 2, as to the outtakes that were not broadcast. The respondents asserted that the entire videotape should have been released on the grounds that the voters enacted a constitutional amendment affirming that in criminal cases the people have the right to due process of law, Cal. Const. art. I, § 29. The court considered whether the assertion of the constitutional right by respondent served as a justification for holding a newsperson in contempt for refusing to surrender unpublished information, despite the newsperson's immunity from contempt for refusal as expressly provided by the shield law. The court concluded that a newsperson could not be held in contempt under those circumstances.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed; assertion of constitutional right to due process in criminal cases did not trump newsperson's immunity from contempt for refusal to release unpublished materials expressly provided in the shield law.

 

4 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Falsetta


No. S071521.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


21 Cal. 4th 903; 986 P.2d 182; 89 Cal. Rptr. 2d 847; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7197; 99 Daily Journal DAR 11175; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8768



November 1, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied April 24, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 2909.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County. Super. Ct. No. CH20852. Jeffrey W. Horner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We affirm the judgment of the Court of Appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from the Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District and Third Appellate District, affirming the judgment of the superior court which held that Cal. Evid. Code §1108, permitting admission in a sex offense case of defendant's other sex crimes, was constitutionally valid.


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of several crimes, including two sex crimes. On appeal, he contended that the admission of evidence of his other uncharged rapes for the purpose of showing a propensity to commit such crimes, as allowed by Cal. Evid. Code §1108, violated his due process rights. The court affirmed, concluding that although § 1108 represented a deviation from the historical practice of excluding such "propensity" evidence, it preserved trial court discretion to exclude the evidence if its prejudicial effect outweighed its probative value, and, consistent with prior state and federal case law, the section was constitutionally valid. The court noted that by their very nature, sex crimes are usually committed in seclusion without third-party witnesses or substantial corroborating evidence, and the ensuing trial often presents conflicting versions of the event and requires the trier of fact to make difficult credibility determinations.


OUTCOME: Conviction affirmed; the section survived constitutional scrutiny because it preserved trial court discretion to exclude the evidence if its prejudicial effect outweighed its probative value, and such evidence could help the trier of fact to make difficult credibility determinations.

 

5 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Frazer


No. S067443.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


21 Cal. 4th 737; 982 P.2d 180; 88 Cal. Rptr. 2d 312; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 5535; 99 Daily Journal DAR 9017; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7116



August 30, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified October 20, 1999. Rehearing Denied October 20, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7223. Certiorari Denied May 15, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 3184, 68 U.S.L. Week 3711.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Mendocino County. Super. Ct. No. CR24362. Henry K. Nelson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Section 803(g) is not unconstitutional on any ground asserted by defendant. The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Mendocino County (California) which affirmed the lower court order sustaining defendant's demurrer challenging Cal. Penal Code § 803(g). This law permitted prosecution of a crime for an additional period--within one year of the time the victim reported an independently corroborated crime to law enforcement officials, whether the crime occurred before or after section became effective.


OVERVIEW: One year after the victim reported the crime to the police, defendant was charged with violating Cal. Penal Code § 288, prohibiting lewd conduct against children. The fixed limitations period in existence when the crime occurred had run before the complaint was filed, and before section 803(g) became effective. This law permitted prosecution of a crime one year from the time the victim reported the crime to law enforcement officials. Defendant demurred on grounds the statute constituted an unconstitutional ex post facto law, and unfairly deprived him of a statute of limitations defense under the due process clauses of the United States Constitution. The appellate court affirmed the state court's order sustaining the demurrer and dismissal of the action; the state sought review. On appeal, the court reversed holding the statute was not unconstitutional. Ex post facto protection extended only to defenses bearing on the "definition" and "elements" of the crime or "excuse or justification" for its commission, not statutes of limitation. The court also found that the statute of limitations did not create a "fundamental right," therefore there was no violation of Due Process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court and held statute was not unconstitutional. The court held statutes regulating the time at which a future criminal prosecution may be filed did not implicate the manner in which criminal conduct was defined and punished at the time it occurred, which was the sole concern of the ex post facto clause. The statute of limitation did not impose a fundamental right, therefore, statute did not violate due process.

 

6 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Warden v. State Bar


No. S060702.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


21 Cal. 4th 628; 982 P.2d 154; 88 Cal. Rptr. 2d 283; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 5533; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6998; 99 Daily Journal DAR 8877



August 26, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied October 20, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7220.
Certiorari Denied March 20, 2000, 68 U.S.L. Week 3593.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County. Super. Ct. No. 734372-4. James R. Lambden and Sandra Lynn Margulies, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, and the matter is remanded to the Court of Appeal with directions to affirm the summary judgment in favor of defendants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state bar petitioned the court for review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) finding in favor of plaintiff in plaintiff's suit against defendant challenging the constitutionality of the state's mandatory continuing legal education program.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sued defendants, challenging the constitutionality of the state's mandatory continuing legal education program. Plaintiff asserted the program violated plaintiff's right to equal protection of the laws because three categories of attorneys were exempt from the program's requirements. The appellate court reversed the trial court and held the program's exceptions were invalid under the rational relationship equal protection standard. The court held because plaintiff's claim pertained to professional licensing discrimination, the rules under attack had a presumption of constitutionality and required only that the distinctions bear some rational relationship to a conceivable legitimate state purpose. Therefore, the applicable standard of review was the rational relationship standard. However, under a proper application of the rational relationship test, the exemptions could not be found unconstitutional as the justifications for the exemptions, that the attorneys in each category were less likely than other attorneys to represent clients, were realistically conceivable legislative purposes. The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: Judgment in favor of plaintiff reversed and remanded where the exemptions to the state's mandatory continuing legal education program were realistically conceivable legislative purposes.

 

7 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Woods


No. S068741.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


21 Cal. 4th 668; 981 P.2d 1019; 88 Cal. Rptr. 2d 88; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 5534; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6990; 99 Daily Journal DAR 8867



August 26, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied March 20, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County. Super. Ct. No. 9614538. Garrett J. Grant, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed. The matter is remanded to that court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff people appealed from a decision of the California Court of Appeal that affirmed a decision of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County, California that granted a motion to suppress evidence of drug activities by defendants, a probationer's co-habitants, that had been obtained as the result of a pretextual probation search.


OVERVIEW: A probationer, as a condition of her felony probation, had agreed to submit her residence to warrantless searches. During one of those warrantless searches, the police found evidence of criminal activity involving drugs against probationer's two co-habitants, defendants. Defendants successfully moved to suppress the evidence found in probationer's residence in the trial court on the ground it had been obtained as the result of a pretextual probation search. The appeals court affirmed the trial court ruling in favor of defendants. Plaintiff people appealed. The state supreme court granted review and addressed the issue of whether a warrantless search of a probationer's house was constitutionally invalid when it was undertaken to discover incriminating evidence against a third party residing in the house. The court reversed the lower courts' rulings and remanded the case for further proceedings finding the police officer's subjective intent did not invalidate the challenged search. Applying federal constitutional standards to the police search, the court determined that probationer had consented to the search of property she fully or jointly controlled, thus the search was valid.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appeals court for defendants was reversed. The matter was remanded for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion that the officer's subjective intent did not invalidate the challenged search since the circumstances, viewed objectively, justified the officer's actions.

 

8 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Hotel Employees & Restaurant Employees Internat. Union v. Davis


No. S074850, No. S074851.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


21 Cal. 4th 585; 981 P.2d 990; 88 Cal. Rptr. 2d 56; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 5529; 99 Daily Journal DAR 8671; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6813



August 23, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  We therefore discharge the orders to show cause and direct issuance of a peremptory writ of mandate compelling the Governor and the Secretary of State not to implement Proposition 5, with the exception of the final sentence of Government Code section 98005.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner union applied for a writ of mandate to compel the governor not to implement proposed legislation that authorized gaming in tribal casinos, asserting the proposition was invalid under the laws of California and the United States.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner union filed applications for a writ of mandate to compel the governor not to implement a proposition authorizing various forms of gaming in tribal casinos, asserting the measure was invalid under the law of both California and the United States. The court held that under Cal. Const. art. IV, § 19(e), the California legislature had no power to authorize, and was to prohibit, the type of casinos operating in Nevada and New Jersey at the time § 19(e) was enacted. The type included one or more buildings, rooms, or facilities, whether separate or connected, that offered gambling activities including those statutorily prohibited in California, especially banked table games and slot machines. The court held that because the proposition at issue attempted to authorize the kind of gaming expressly prohibited by § 19(e), it was invalid. The court determined, however, that the provision of the proposition dealing with the state's waiver of immunity from suit in disputes arising out of negotiations for tribal-state compacts was not invalid because it was grammatically, functionally, and volitionally separable from the invalid parts. Accordingly, a writ of mandate was issued.


OUTCOME: A writ of mandate compelling the governor not to implement proposed legislation was issued because the proposition was inconsistent with the California Constitution, which expressly prohibited the type of gaming the proposition attempted to authorize. The provision that waived the state's immunity as to certain disputes, however, was valid because it was grammatically, functionally, and volitionally separable from the invalid parts.

 

9 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Allen


No. S054125.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


21 Cal. 4th 424; 981 P.2d 525; 87 Cal. Rptr. 2d 682; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 5310; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6701; 99 Daily Journal DAR 8565



August 19, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied October 6, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7035.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. YA020812. Donald F. Pitts, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the appellate court (California) denying defendant's motion to strike defendant's prior convictions.


OVERVIEW: At defendant's sentencing hearing, defendant moved to strike prior convictions claiming not, in the prior proceeding, to have been informed of the rights defendant was forfeiting as a result of pleading guilty. The trial court denied the motion. The appellate court held, in light a recent U.S. Supreme Court case, criminal defendants could only move to strike prior convictions when claiming to have been denied the right to counsel in the prior proceedings. Because a division in the court of appeals existed on the issue, the court granted review. The court held motions to strike based upon a claim that the accused was not informed of the rights being waived as the result of a guilty plea could only be made after the court's decision in a case which held such motions could only be made where the face of the record contained direct evidence that the accused was aware of such waiver. Therefore, because defendant entered a guilty plea in the prior case before the court decided that case, defendant was not entitled to collaterally attack the voluntariness of that plea by moving to strike the conviction in the trial court, and the appellate court's denial of the motion was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Denial of defendant's motion to strike affirmed where defendant's motion to strike procedure was available to challenge only those prior felony convictions suffered prior to the court's decision in another case.

 

10 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Associated Builders & Contractors, Inc. v. San Francisco Airports Com.


No. S066747.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


21 Cal. 4th 352; 981 P.2d 499; 87 Cal. Rptr. 2d 654; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 5309; 99 Daily Journal DAR 8397; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6588; 161 L.R.R.M. 3166; 139 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P58,692



August 16, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Super. Ct. No. 979575. David A. Garcia, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed from a decision of the California Court of Appeal that affirmed denial of a writ of mandate taken from an action of the San Francisco, California Airports Commission denying plaintiffs' writ to strike project stabilization agreement (PSA) requirements from bid specifications because of their alleged unconstitutionality and alleged violation of state competitive bidding statutes.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs sought to bid on contract to expand and renovate the San Francisco airport. Plaintiffs took defendant commission to the trial court upon a writ of mandate for a decision as to whether the project stabilization agreement (PSA) executed by defendant commission and real party in interest union violated competitive bidding laws or certain other statutory or constitutional provisions. Defendant had required all contractors who worked on the project to accept the PSA terms with the union. The trial court denied plaintiffs' writ. Plaintiffs appealed unsuccessfully where the writ denial was affirmed. The state supreme court granted plaintiffs' petition to review denial of the writ. The court found the PSA, a type of prehire agreement, was allowable under the National Labor Relations Act, 29 U.S.C.S. § 151 et seq. because it fit within a congressionally recognized exception to bargaining rules as where the PSA was for short-term and occasional employment by an employer in the construction industry. Since the PSA had not excluded non-union contractors from bidding on the project, it was not unconstitutional. The court affirmed the lower court judgment against plaintiffs.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the appeals court against plaintiffs was affirmed. The court found that plaintiffs failed to establish that its members or their employees were denied equal protection under either the state or the federal charter, and that the PSA was not anticompetitive merely because certain bidders would see some of its features as less attractive or desire to deal exclusively with non-union members.

 

11 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Aguilar v. Avis Rent A Car System, Inc.


No. S054561.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


21 Cal. 4th 121; 980 P.2d 846; 87 Cal. Rptr. 2d 132; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 4850; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7877; 80 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 643; 15 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 594; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6144; 75 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P45,960



August 2, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied May 22, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 3570.
Writ of certiorari denied Avis Rent A Car Sys. v. Aguilar, 529 U.S. 1138, 146 L. Ed. 2d 971, 120 S. Ct. 2029, 2000 U.S. LEXIS 3570 (2000)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Super. Ct. No. 948597. Carlos Bea, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant employer appealed a grant of an injunction by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) after a jury found defendant engaged in employment discrimination by allowing plaintiff employees to be the target of racial epithets spoken by a fellow employee. Defendant asserted the injunction prohibiting the employee's future use of the epithets was a prior restraint that violated the right to freedom of speech.


OVERVIEW: Defendant company sought review of a judgment that issued, after a jury found that defendant had engaged in employment discrimination in part by permitting plaintiff employees to be the target of racial epithets repeatedly spoken by a fellow employee, an injunction prohibiting the employee from using such epithets in the future. Defendant asserted the injunction constituted a prior restraint that violated the constitutional right to freedom of speech. The court held that harassing speech sufficiently severe or pervasive to constitute employment discrimination was not constitutionally protected. The court held that once the lower court found that a specific pattern of speech was unlawful, an injunctive order prohibiting the repetition, perpetuation, or continuation of that practice was not a prohibited "prior restraint" of speech. The court further explained that an injunction against speech generally would not be considered an unconstitutional prior restraint if it was issued after a jury determined that the speech was not constitutionally protected. Accordingly, the issuance of the injunction was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment that granted an injunction against defendant company was affirmed because once the lower court found that the employee's specific pattern of speech was unlawful. An injunctive order prohibiting the repetition, perpetuation, or continuation of that practice was not a prohibited "prior restraint" of speech.

 

12 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Garcia


No. S059302.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


21 Cal. 4th 1; 980 P.2d 829; 87 Cal. Rptr. 2d 114; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 4846; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7715; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6033



July 29, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County. Super. Ct. No. SCD111679. William T. Low, Judge. * 


* Retired judge of the San Diego Superior Court, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed. The matter is remanded to the Court of Appeal with directions to remand to the trial court for new proceedings on sentence.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeal (California) that upheld a ruling that sentenced defendant to an enhanced term of imprisonment under the "Three Strikes" law using a prior juvenile offense as a strike pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667(e)(1) in a criminal proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had four prior juvenile adjudications for residential burglary before he was tried and convicted for residential burglary and possession of burglar's tools as an adult. The trial court rejected defendant's argument that juvenile adjudications could not be used for purposes of the Three Strikes law. The trial court sentenced defendant to an enhanced term of imprisonment under the Three Strikes law's second strike provision Cal. Penal Code § 667(e)(1). The appellate court affirmed. The court reversed, holding that the requirement that the juvenile be adjudged a ward of the juvenile court, because of a Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707(b) offense, had not been satisfied; thus, the trial court erred in sentencing defendant under § 667(e)(1). The court stated that legislation should have been construed so as to harmonize its various elements without doing violence to its language or spirit.


OUTCOME: The sentence enhancement of defendant's sentence was reversed because the requirement that a juvenile be adjudged a ward of the juvenile court, due to a California Welfare and Institutions Code offense, had not been satisfied at trial. The court found that the trial court erred in sentencing defendant under the "Three Strikes" law, and remanded for new proceedings on the sentence.

 

13 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Carrillo v. Acf Indus.


No. S072065.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


20 Cal. 4th 1158; 980 P.2d 386; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 832; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 4635; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P15,585; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5916; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7559



July 27, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied January 10, 2000, 68 U.S.L.W. 3430. Certiorari Denied January 10, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 151.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BC083711. Alban I. Niles, Judge. *


* Judge of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed and the matter remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a denial by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) of its motion for judgment notwithstanding the verdict on the ground that plaintiff's requested state tort remedies were preempted by the federal Safety Appliance Acts, 49 U.S.C.S. § 20301 et seq. and the Federal Railroad Safety Act, 49 U.S.C.S. § 20101 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, after being injured while using equipment in his employment, filed a personal injury suit for tort damages against defendant railcar manufacturer, based on strict product liability theories of design defect and failure to warn. Following trial, a jury awarded plaintiff $ 1.4 million in damages. Defendant sought a judgment notwithstanding the verdict on the ground that state tort remedies were preempted by the federal Safety Appliance Acts, 49 U.S.C.S. § 20301 et seq., and the Federal Railroad Safety Act, 49 U.S.C.S. § 20101, et seq. The trial court denied the motion. The appellate court affirmed the judgment, holding that federal law did not preempt plaintiff's action because neither statute specifically addressed the subject of guardrails on hopper cars. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment, holding that the statutes and their implementing regulations reflected a congressional intent to occupy the field regulating railroad safety appliances, thus precluding any state law directed to the same matter, including common law tort claims predicated on design defects.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of defendant's motion for judgment notwithstanding the verdict and remanded the matter for further proceedings, concluding that the federal Safety Appliance Acts precluded state regulation of guardrails on rail freight cars and thus preempted plaintiff's cause of action under state tort law.

 

14 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



NBC Subsidiary (KNBC-TV), Inc. v. Superior Court


No. S056924.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


20 Cal. 4th 1178; 980 P.2d 337; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 778; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 4634; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7575; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5922; 28 Media L. Rep. 1001



July 27, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BC129098. David M. Schacter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal issuing a writ of mandate directing the trial court to vacate its September 12, 1996, closure order, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent county appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), vacating the trial court's closure order excluding petitioner media outlets from all proceedings held outside the presence of the jury under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 124 and U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: One celebrity sued another for deceit, intentional interference with prospective economic advantage, and breach of fiduciary duty arising out of alleged promises to assist in the development of motion picture projects. After the jury was sworn, the trial court issued an oral closure order excluding petitioner media outlets from all proceedings held outside the presence of the jury in order to ensure a fair and impartial jury. Petitioners appealed, and the appellate court issued a writ of mandate directing the trial court to vacate its closure order. Respondent county appealed, and the court affirmed, holding that the closure order violated Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 124 and U.S. Const. amend. I because the trial court did not identify particular proceedings that would or did contain information justifying closure, and regular and specific cautionary jury admonitions and instructions were less restrictive means of achieving the overriding interest in a fair trial.


OUTCOME: Vacation of the trial court's closure order excluding petitioner media outlets from all proceedings held outside the presence of the jury was affirmed because there were no identified proceedings that would or did contain information justifying closure, and there were less restrictive means of achieving a fair trial.

 

15 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Bramberg v. Jones


No. S076784.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


20 Cal. 4th 1045; 978 P.2d 1240; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 319; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 4220; 99 Daily Journal DAR 6953; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5435



July 8, 1999, Decided



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing the Secretary of State not to enforce the provisions of Proposition 225.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a taxpayer, registered voter, and a candidate for public office, brought an action for a writ of mandate prohibiting respondent secretary of state from enforcing an initiative measure relating to congressional term limits.


OVERVIEW: California voters adopted a proposition that instructed federal and state legislators to propose and support a congressional term limits amendment to the United States Constitution. The proposition directed respondent secretary of state, in future elections for congressional and state legislative offices, to include on the election ballot, adjacent to the name of any candidate who failed to support or pledge support for the proposed amendment, the phrase "Disregarded Voters' Instruction on Term Limits" or "Declined to Pledge to Support Term Limits." Petitioners, a taxpayer, registered voter, and candidate for public office challenged the proposition as unconstitutional. The court found that the measure directly instructed and indirectly attempted to coerce the state's congressional and state legislators in the exercise of their federal constitutional function of proposing and ratifying amendments to the United States Constitution and the measure was therefore unconstitutional. Petitioner's request for a writ of mandate enjoining enforcement of the proposition was granted.


OUTCOME: A writ of mandate enjoining enforcement of the challenged proposition was issued because the proposition was an unconstitutional interference with the power to propose and ratify amendments to the United States Constitution which is vested only in deliberative representative bodies.

 

16 of 1449 DOCUMENTS




People v. Smithey


No. S011206.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


20 Cal. 4th 936; 978 P.2d 1171; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 243; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 3907; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5271; 99 Daily Journal DAR 6757



July 1, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied March 20, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 2018. As Modified September 15, 1999. Review Denied September 15, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 6251.
Rehearing denied by People v. Smithey, 21 Cal. 4th 845a, 1999 Cal. LEXIS 6251 (1999)
Application granted by People v. Smithey (George Hatton), 2008 Cal. LEXIS 14251 (Cal., Dec. 10, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Calaveras County. Super. Ct. No. 2639. Orrin K. Airola, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An automatic appeal was taken from a judgment of the Superior Court of Calaveras County (California), which in the guilt phase of trial and upon jury verdict, found defendant guilty of first degree murder, first degree robbery, attempted rape, and first degree burglary. The jury found the use of a deadly weapon and three special-circumstance allegations. In the penalty phase, the jury returned a verdict imposing a sentence of death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a chronic user of methamphetamine and in need of money, went to victim's trailer. They went to her bedroom, he slit her throat, hit her carotid artery, and she bled to death. Her six-year-old daughter saw them in the bedroom, saw him on top of her on the bed, and saw her dead body. He was found with a bloody knife and other incriminating evidence. He admitted killing her, but claimed he did not remember doing so. He denied intending to steal or attempting sex. Defense experts testified that defendant suffered from classic amphetamine psychosis syndrome and was totally out of control. Rebuttal expert testimony was that apparently intentional acts were in fact intentional. Defendant was convicted of first degree murder, first degree robbery, attempted rape, and first degree burglary, and he was sentenced to death. On automatic appeal, the court affirmed. Among other things, the court found that defendant was not prejudiced by one instance of prosecutorial misconduct, where the prosecutor sought to elicit an expert's inadmissible opinion regarding defendant's capacity to form the intent required for a crime.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court that convicted defendant and sentenced him to death because the superior court did not commit a prejudicial error in either the guilt phase of the trial or in the penalty phase of the trial.


 

17 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Earp


No. S025423.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


20 Cal. 4th 826; 978 P.2d 15; 85 Cal. Rptr. 2d 857; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 3901; 99 Daily Journal DAR 6457; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5002



June 24, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied March 6, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 1779. Rehearing Denied September 1, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 6109 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. A747945. Ronald S. Coen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for first degree murder, entered in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of violating Cal. Penal Code § 187 for the rape and murder of an eighteen-month-old child, and was sentenced to death. Defendant told conflicting stories as to how the victim had obtained her injuries and had made a statement to his mother that another individual was present in the home when the injuries occurred. During cross-examination and closing argument, the prosecutor asked why defendant had had not mentioned there was a witness. Defendant contended the prosecution was prohibited from impeaching his trial testimony with evidence of his silence when, after having been advised of his constitutional rights, he chose to remain silent. Defendant also claimed that the prosecutor unfairly shifted the burden of mitigation in sentencing. On appeal, the court held that defendant had failed to preserve for appeal the prosecutorial misconduct claim by timely objecting at trial. Therefore, the point was not reviewable because an admonition would have cured the harm. As to the death penalty, the prosecutor's statements when viewed as a whole did not shift the burden of proof but mirrored the jury instructions, therefore, the conviction and sentence were affirmed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and death sentence were affirmed because defendant had failed to preserve for appeal the claim of prosecutorial misconduct by timely objecting at trial and the prosecutor's statements when viewed as a whole did not shift the burden of proof of mitigation.

 

18 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Welch


No. S011323.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


20 Cal. 4th 701; 976 P.2d 754; 85 Cal. Rptr. 2d 203; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 2976; 99 Daily Journal DAR 5242; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4127



June 1, 1999, Decided  
June 1, 1999


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied February 22, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 1034. Rehearing Denied August 18, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 5545.
Rehearing denied by People v. Welch, 1999 Cal. LEXIS 5545 (Cal., Aug. 18, 1999)
Writ of certiorari denied Welch v. California, 528 U.S. 1154, 120 S. Ct. 1160, 145 L. Ed. 2d 1071, 2000 U.S. LEXIS 1034 (2000)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Welch (David Esco) on H.C., 2007 Cal. LEXIS 7189 (Cal., June 20, 2007)
Motion granted by People v. Welch (David Esco), 2007 Cal. LEXIS 7770 (Cal., July 18, 2007)
Motion granted by People v. Welch (David Esco), 2010 Cal. LEXIS 13367 (Cal., Dec. 15, 2010)
Costs and fees proceeding at People v. Welch (David Esco), 2012 Cal. LEXIS 12163 (Cal., Nov. 26, 2012)
Writ of habeas corpus denied In re Welch, 61 Cal.4th 489, 189 Cal.Rptr.3d 179, 351 P.3d 306, 2015 Cal. LEXIS 4338 (Cal., June 22, 2015)



DISPOSITION: We affirm the judgment in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought relief from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which convicted him of murder under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3). Defendant's appeal was automatic pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1239(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of six counts of first degree murder in a single incident involving the murder of six residents of the same house, including two young children. Defendant was intoxicated at the time of the incident. However, defendant had made multiple threats to one of the victims prior to the incident. Defendant had also threatened law enforcement agents after the incident and was disruptive during the pretrial proceedings. Defendant wished to represent himself but his motion to do so was denied by the court. Defendant was given the death penalty. On appeal, the court held that ample evidence supported the conviction. Because of the extent of defendant's disruptive behavior, the lower court did not abuse its discretion in denying defendant's request to represent himself. The court also found that defendant's violent past, including threats to law enforcement officers, was properly submitted as evidence supporting the death penalty. Therefore, the conviction and sentence were affirmed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's murder conviction and resulting death sentence were affirmed. The court found that because of the extent of defendant's disruptive behavior, the lower court did not abuse its discretion in denying defendant's request to represent himself.

 

19 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Morante


No. S063446.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


20 Cal. 4th 403; 975 P.2d 1071; 84 Cal. Rptr. 2d 665; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 2968; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3583; 99 Daily Journal DAR 4585



May 17, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied July 21, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 4861.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BA096050. Edward A. Ferns, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We affirm the judgment of the Court of Appeal reversing defendant's conviction of conspiracy to transport and possess cocaine for sale, based upon our conclusion that defendant's agreement and the overt acts she performed inside the state did not, as required by the law then applicable, amount to an attempt to commit the offenses. We also affirm the judgment of that court upholding defendant's conviction for transporting and possessing cocaine for sale, based upon her having aided and abetted the commission of these offenses.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeal reversing a criminal defendant's conviction of conspiracy to transport and possess cocaine for sale, and upholding defendant's conviction for transporting and possessing cocaine for sale. Defendant also sought review, contending that the Court of Appeal erred in affirming her convictions of possession for sale and transportation of cocaine.


OVERVIEW: Defendant solicited, hired, and instructed a drug courier in California to transport cocaine to another state. Defendant transported the courier to the airport, and paid for his airplane flight. After lengthy surveillance, which included multiple telephone calls between defendant and the courier, the courier was arrested. Defendant's subsequent conviction for transporting and possessing cocaine for sale was affirmed by the intermediate court, but her conviction for conspiracy to transport and possess cocaine for sale was reversed. The prosecution appealed the reversal of the conspiracy charge, and defendant appealed the affirmation of the transportation and possession charge. The court upheld defendant's conviction for transporting and possessing cocaine for sale, based upon her having aided and abetted the commission of these offenses by acts performed within the state, despite the circumstance that the offenses were not attempted within the state. However, defendant's agreement and the overt acts she performed inside the state did not amount to an attempt to commit the offenses, therefore the court affirmed the reversal of the conviction for conspiracy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the both the reversal of the conviction of conspiracy to transport and possess cocaine for sale and the conviction for aiding and abetting because once jurisdiction was established, defendant's continued assistance, resulted in her liability for those offenses. However, defendant's involvement did not amount to an attempt to commit the offenses.

 

20 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



California Teachers Assn. v. State of California


No. S067030.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


20 Cal. 4th 327; 975 P.2d 622; 84 Cal. Rptr. 2d 425; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 2560; 99 Daily Journal DAR 4349; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3376



May 10, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County. Super. Ct. No. CV376695. James Timothy Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the state of California, sought relief from decision of Superior Court of Sacramento County, (California), finding Cal. Educ. Code § 44944 unconstitutional on its face.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff teacher was notified by defendant school district of intent to dismiss him for unfitness of service for immoral conduct. Plaintiff demanded a hearing, in which it was found that he had not engaged in immoral conduct but was unfit for service, and plaintiff was dismissed. Plaintiff was charged half the cost of the administrative hearing pursuant to Cal. Educ. Code § 44944, and defendant state controller used plaintiff's income tax refund to offset against the debt. Plaintiff petitioned for a writ of mandate and the lower court found the statute unconstitutional. On appeal, the court held that § 44944 was unconstitutional because it caused a chilling effect upon litigation. Plaintiff's position was not frivolous and thus could not be discouraged by a significant cost intended to discourage hearing demands. Under the challenged statute, teachers possessing colorable arguments who exercised their right to a hearing were subjected to a penalty more severe than that typically imposed on defeated parties, therefore the court affirmed the lower court's decision finding the statute facially unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision finding Cal. Educ. Code § 44944 unconstitutional because it caused a chilling effect upon litigation.

 

21 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Cel-Tech Communications, Inc. v. Los Angeles Cellular Telephone Co.


No. S066735.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


20 Cal. 4th 163; 973 P.2d 527; 83 Cal. Rptr. 2d 548; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 1656; 99 Daily Journal DAR 3360; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2576; 1999-1 Trade Cas. (CCH) P72,495



April 8, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. VC015535. C. Robert Simpson, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed. The unfair competition law cause of action shall be retried consistently with the legal principles stated in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a communications company and others, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) holding that defendant telephone company did not violate the Unfair Practices Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17000 et seq. Defendant appealed the judgment holding that defendant might have violated the unfair competition law, Cal. Bus. Code § 17200 et seq., and remanding the issue for retrial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sold cellular telephones and services. Defendant was one of two service providers licensed by the federal government. In an effort to increase its number of subscribers, defendant sold telephones below cost. Plaintiffs were companies that sold cellular telephones, but were not licensed to sell services. Plaintiffs brought action against defendant under the Unfair Practices Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17000 et seq. and unfair competition law, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17200 et seq., alleging that defendant's below-cost sales practice harmed them. Section 17043 prohibited defendant from selling any product at less than cost for the purpose of injuring competitors or destroying competition. Because defendant did not act with the purpose of injuring plaintiffs, defendant did not violate § 17043. Section 17044 prohibited defendant from selling a product as a loss leader. Plaintiffs argued that under § 17044, defendant's particular mental state was irrelevant. The word "unfair" meant conduct that significantly threatened or harmed competition. Plaintiff was allowed to present evidence to meet the test set forth by the court to determine the extent of defendant's unfairness.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that defendant did not violate the Unfair Practices Act and affirmed the judgment that defendant might have violated the unfair competition law, holding that defendant did not act with the purpose of injuring plaintiffs and that, because the applicable test for an unfair competition law action was stated for the first time by the court, plaintiff was allowed to present additional evidence to meet that test.

 

22 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Hubbart v. Superior Court


No. S052136.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


19 Cal. 4th 1138; 969 P.2d 584; 81 Cal. Rptr. 2d 492; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 6; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 563; 99 Daily Journal DAR 671



January 21, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County. Super. Ct. No. 140294. Robert Michael Foley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The SVPA is not unconstitutional on any ground asserted by Hubbart. The judgment of the Court of Appeal is therefore affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant petitioned the court for review of the decision of the Court of Appeal (California) which upheld the trial court's decision granting the petition to civilly commit defendant under the Sexually Violent Predators Act (SVPA), Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6600 et seq. Defendant contended that the SVPA was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Prior to petitioner's release from prison, respondent state sought petitioner's civil commitment under the Sexually Violent Predators Act (Act), Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code, §6600 et seq. Petitioner demurred to the petition on grounds the Act was unconstitutional. The trial court overruled the demurrer and the appellate court denied his petition for a writ of prohibition. On appeal, petitioner claimed that the Act conflicted with due process, equal protection and ex post facto principles. The court affirmed the judgement of the appellate court finding no merit to any of petitioner's constitutional challenges. The court found that the definitions of mental impairment and dangerousness in the Act established the requisite connection between impaired volitional control and the danger posed to the public. Therefore due process was not violated. The dangerous at the time of commitment standard ensured equal protection. Because the civil commitment was non-penal in purpose and effect, no ex post facto violation occurred.


OUTCOME: Order upholding petitioner's civil commitment as violent sexual predator was affirmed and statute authorizing commitment was found to be constitutional. The court upheld the judgment finding no merit in petitioner's claim that the statutory scheme conflicted, on its face, with due process, equal protection, and ex post facto principles.

 

23 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Santa Monica Beach v. Superior Court


No. S052824.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


19 Cal. 4th 952; 968 P.2d 993; 81 Cal. Rptr. 2d 93; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 2; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 95; 99 Daily Journal DAR 131



January 4, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied May 24, 1999, Reported at: 1999 U.S. LEXIS 3468, 67 U.S.L. Week 3715.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superiro Court Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. SS005266. Alan B. Haber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For all of the foregoing, we reverse the judgment of the Court of Appeal and remand with directions to reinstate the judgment of the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner property owner sought review from a decision, by the Superior Court Los Angeles County (California), sustaining a demurrer by the real party in interest rent control board, after the petitioner brought an action for inverse condemnation seeking a petition for writ of administrative mandate. The court of appeal issued an order to show cause and set aside the trial court's judgment.


OVERVIEW: This review was taken following the lower appellate court's order setting aside the trial court's order which approved the rent control board's dismissal of petitioner's inverse condemnation complaint, finding that there was a rational basis for the rent control law. The lower appellate court found that a heightened standard of scrutiny applied to rent control law and, if petitioner's allegations were true, that the law's failure to meet its stated purposes meant that it failed to substantially advance a legitimate government purpose, and the law was a taking of property. The court held that the heightened intermediate scrutiny standard did not apply. Rather, the party challenging rent control had to show that it constituted an arbitrary regulation of property rights. The court found there was no authority for the proposition that a piece of legislation that advances legitimate goals, but not precisely those goals specified in its preamble, could be struck down by a court as unconstitutional. The court reversed the lower appellate court and held that petitioner's allegations failed to state a cause of action for inverse condemnation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower appellate court and remanded with directions to reinstate the judgment of the trial court. The court held that the trial court was correct to sustain the board's demurrer to petitioner's cause of action for inverse condemnation. The court held that petitioner's allegations failed to state a cause of action for inverse condemnation.

 

24 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Diamond Multimedia Systems, Inc. v. Superior Court


No. S058723.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


19 Cal. 4th 1036; 968 P.2d 539; 80 Cal. Rptr. 2d 828; 1999 Cal. LEXIS 1; 99 Daily Journal DAR 97; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 84



January 4, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied June 14, 1999, Reported at: 1999 U.S. LEXIS 4049, 67 U.S.L. 3756.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County. Super. Ct. Nos. CV758927, CV759012 and CV759270. Peter G. Stone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Appeal summarily denying the petition for writ of mandate is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporation challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which held that petitioner was amenable to suit under Cal. Corp. Code § 25400 and § 25500, by out-of-state stock purchasers, for manipulating its stock price by issuing false statements to the general public concerning its financial situation.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner corporation issued press releases that gave false information about its finances, artificially inflating its stock price, whereby insider officers and directors were able to make a profit. After the false disclosures were revealed, respondents, out-of-state purchases of petitioner's stock, filed suit under Cal. Corp. Code § 25400 and § 25500. Petitioner filed a motion to dismiss, claiming that the law only applied to in-state purchasers of stock, and did not reach beyond the state boundary. The trial court and the appellate court ruled against petitioner, and petitioner appealed the ruling. The court held that under the ambiguous language of the statute, the statute did not limit its reach or recovery merely to in-state purchasers of petitioner's stock. The court determined that in accordance with the rest of the statute and the underlying purpose of the law, respondents should be able to sue petitioner even though they were out-of-state purchasers. The court concluded that this interpretation did not conflict with federal securities law or the U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl 3 Commerce Clause. The court denied petitioner's writ of mandate and affirmed the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court and held that respondents, out-of-state stock purchasers, were entitled to bring a lawsuit against petitioner corporation for issuing false press releases and manipulating its stock. Further, the law permitting respondents' action did not violate federal securities law or the U.S. Constitution's Commerce Clause.

 

25 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Hernandez


No. S047306.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


19 Cal. 4th 835; 968 P.2d 465; 80 Cal. Rptr. 2d 754; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 8038; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9337; 98 Daily Journal DAR 13009



December 24, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied May 24, 1999, Reported at: 1999 U.S. LEXIS 3538.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County. Super. Ct. No. SF79942. Susan P. Finlay, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Municipal Court for the South Bay Judicial District, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the judgment of the Court of Appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state challenged the decision of the Court of Appeals (California), which held that application of a sentence enhancement under Cal. Penal Code § 667 following defendant's conviction for multiple felonies resulted in double jeopardy.


OVERVIEW: After defendant was convicted by plaintiff state for various felonies, including assault by means likely to produce great bodily injury, battery with serious bodily injury, petty theft, and corporal injury to a cohabitant, the trial court imposed a sentence enhancement under Cal. Penal Code § 667. That enhancement resulted in a longer sentence due to a prior serious felony conviction. The appellate court found that application of the statute constituted double jeopardy and was unconstitutional. On appeal of that decision, the court reversed and remanded. The state and federal prohibitions against double jeopardy did not apply to noncapital sentencing determinations. The imposition of the sentence enhancement in this case constituted a noncapital sentencing determination, not a conviction for a new crime. The disputed enhancement was not an element of the offense. Thus, the state and federal double jeopardy protections did not preclude the trial court from considering the applicability of § 667.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded, as the appellate court improperly concluded that the trial court's enhancement of defendant's sentence based on a prior felony conviction resulted in double jeopardy.

 

26 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Calvillo-Silva v. Home Grocery


No. S053418.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


19 Cal. 4th 714; 968 P.2d 65; 80 Cal. Rptr. 2d 506; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 7631; 98 Daily Journal DAR 12803; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9170



December 17, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied February 17, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 780.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County. Super. Ct. No. 366928. William H. Harrington, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed. The matter is remanded to that court with directions to remand to the superior court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant grocers sought review of a decision by the Court of Appeal (California) that overturned a trial court's grant of summary judgment to defendants pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 847(a)-(e) which provided that under certain circumstances an owner of an interest in real property would not be liable for injuries that occurred upon the property during or after the injured person committed any one of the 25 felonies listed in the statute.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff gunshot victim who was injured during an armed robbery attempt at defendant grocers' store was successful in convincing the appeals court to reverse a summary judgment granted to defendants at trial on the basis that plaintiff's action was barred pursuant statutory immunity conferred to property owners by to Cal. Civ. Code § 847. The court granted defendant's petition for review. The court affirmed the appeals court's reversal of the summary judgment because it found the evidence in the record gave rise to conflicting inferences as to whether defendants' use of force was justified under the circumstances. The court found that when the predicates of § 847 were established, an owner would be entitled to immunity unless liability otherwise existed for willful, wanton, or criminal conduct, or for willful or malicious failure to guard or warn against a dangerous condition, use, structure, or activity and also found that the immunity granted extended to both negligent conduct and intentionally injurious acts that were justifiable under the circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appeals court's reversal of a summary judgment on the grounds that the record contained conflicting inferences as to whether defendants' use of force was justified and found that statutory immunity for the intentional use of deadly force was not categorically excepted from the scope of the statute's protection. The court remanded the case to the appeals court for remand to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

27 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Attorney Discipline System


No. S073756.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


19 Cal. 4th 582; 967 P.2d 49; 79 Cal. Rptr. 2d 836; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 7413; 98 Daily Journal DAR 12247; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8815



December 3, 1998, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This court issued an order soliciting public comment in response to a letter submitted to the court by the Governor of California requesting that the court assume responsibility over the attorney discipline system. The State Bar of California also submitted a request that the court issue an order requiring active members of the Bar to pay a fee to fund the disciplinary activities.


OVERVIEW: The Governor submitted a letter to the court acknowledging that a problem with public protection had arisen because the State Bar's disciplinary system was no longer operating effectively. The Governor requested that the court assume responsibility over the attorney discipline system pending a legislative solution. A request by the State Bar asked the court to issue an order requiring active members to pay a fee for the purpose of funding the bar's disciplinary activities. The State Bar asserted that the court had the power to assess the fee under its inherent authority to regulate the admission and discipline of attorneys practicing in the state. The court issued an order soliciting public comment on the issue. After a hearing, the court concluded that it had the authority to impose a regulatory fee upon attorneys to support an attorney discipline system. The court found that it was incumbent to do so because the lack of a functioning disciplinary system placed the public, the integrity of the legal profession, and the interests of the courts at grave risk. The court appointed a special master to ensure that the funds were utilized solely to fund disciplinary functions.


OUTCOME: Reflective of its inherent and constitutional authority, the court adopted a rule imposing a special regulatory fee on attorneys actively engaged in the practice of law. The fee was to be used exclusively for attorney disciplinary purposes. The court appointed a special master charged with oversight of the funds collected pursuant to the rule in order to ensure that they were utilized by the State Bar solely to fund disciplinary functions.

People v. Massie


No. S010775.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


19 Cal. 4th 550; 967 P.2d 29; 79 Cal. Rptr. 2d 816; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 7412; 98 Daily Journal DAR 12109; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8717



November 30, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decline to Grant Rehearing on January 27, 1999. Certiorari Denied May 17, 1999, Reported at: 1999 U.S. LEXIS 3258.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Super. Ct. No. 98806. Edward Stern, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which convicted defendant and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty, against the advice of counsel, to the murder and robbery and he admitted special circumstance allegations of prior murder and robbery murder. He also pleaded guilty to robbery, assault with a deadly weapon, and four counts of possession of a concealable firearm by a convicted felon. After a penalty trial at which defendant waived his right to a jury and represented himself, the trial court set the penalty at death. The court reversed defendant's death sentence, the convictions for the robbery and murder, and the two special circumstances. At a retrial in which defendant was represented by counsel, a jury convicted defendant and found true the two special circumstance allegations, and at the penalty phase the jury returned a verdict of death. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court because the court held that defendant's conviction was not barred by the double jeopardy clause and that defendant failed to prove that there was a violation of the fair-cross-section requirement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court because the court held that defendant's conviction was not barred by the double jeopardy clause and that defendant failed to prove that there was a violation of the fair-cross-section requirement.

People v. Roybal


No. S029453.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


19 Cal. 4th 481; 966 P.2d 521; 79 Cal. Rptr. 2d 487; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 7294; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8380; 98 Daily Journal DAR 11599



November 12, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified on Denial of Rehearing January 20, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 23. Certiorari Denied October 4, 1999, Reported at: 1999 U.S. LEXIS 5213.
Rehearing denied by People v. Roybal, 1999 Cal. LEXIS 23 (Cal., Jan. 13, 1999)
Writ of certiorari denied Roybal v. California, 528 U.S. 832, 120 S. Ct. 89, 145 L. Ed. 2d 75, 1999 U.S. LEXIS 5213 (1999)
Costs and fees proceeding at People v. Roybal, 2004 Cal. LEXIS 9451 (Cal., Sept. 29, 2004)
Habeas corpus proceeding at People v. Roybal, 2004 Cal. LEXIS 9452 (Cal., Sept. 29, 2004)
Motion granted by People v. Roybal (Rudolph J.), 2005 Cal. LEXIS 7134 (Cal., June 29, 2005)
Motion denied by People v. Roybal (Rudolph J.), 2006 Cal. LEXIS 13632 (Cal., Nov. 15, 2006)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part People v. Roybal (Rudolph Jose), 2007 Cal. LEXIS 4024 (Cal., Apr. 18, 2007)
Motion granted by People v. Roybal (Rudolph Jose), 2007 Cal. LEXIS 13364 (Cal., Nov. 28, 2007)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Roybal v. Chappell, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 176359 (S.D. Cal., Dec. 16, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County. Super. Ct. No. CRN 20596. David B. Moon, Jr.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons stated above, we affirm the judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, by automatic appeal, challenged the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that convicted him of first-degree murder with special circumstances and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder. It was alleged for death eligibility that he did so under the special circumstances of (1) felony-murder robbery, and (2) felony-murder burglary. It was further alleged that, during the commission of each of these offenses, defendant personally used a deadly and dangerous weapon, a knife, and that he inflicted great bodily injury on the victim. Defendant was found guilty of first-degree murder with special circumstances and sentenced to death. After reviewing all of defendant's claims of error, the court concluded that his motions for a continuance, suppression, and dismissal were properly denied. Defendant was not deprived of a speedy trial, the prosecutor did not improperly comment on his failure to testify, evidence was properly admitted, the jury instructions were proper, a juror was properly excluded for cause, the prosecutor's reference to the bible in his closing argument was not prejudicial, and the objection to defendant's attempt, in its closing argument, to compare defendant's crime to the facts of other well known murders was properly sustained. The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court that convicted defendant of first-degree murder and sentenced him to death was affirmed because none of defendant's claims attacking the judgment of guilt or penalty were meritorious. There was no cumulative prejudice of prosecutorial misconduct or court errors at the guilt and penalty phases of the trial that required reversal.

People v. Ochoa


No. S009522.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


19 Cal. 4th 353; 966 P.2d 442; 79 Cal. Rptr. 2d 408; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 6882; 98 Daily Journal DAR 11407; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8228



November 5, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified on Denial of Rehearing January 27, 1999; Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 387. Certiorari Denied October 4, 1999, Reported at: 1999 U.S. LEXIS 5672.
Time for Granting or Denying Rehearing Extended, 11/19/1998
Rehearing denied by People v. Ochoa, 19 Cal. 4th 1231b, 1999 Cal. LEXIS 387 (1999)
Writ of certiorari denied Ochoa v. California, 528 U.S. 862, 120 S. Ct. 152, 145 L. Ed. 2d 130, 1999 U.S. LEXIS 5672 (1999)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Judgment entered by, Certificate of appealability denied, Stay lifted by, Request denied by Ochoa v. Davis, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 85532 (C.D. Cal., June 30, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Courtof Los angeles County. Super. Ct. Nos. A885934 and A886206. Alfonso M. Bazan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his convictions by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for murder, two counts of kidnapping, two counts of forcible rape, forcible rape in concert, forcible oral copulation, forcible oral copulation in concert, rape by means of a foreign object, attempted forcible sodomy, attempted forcible sodomy in concert, assault with a deadly weapon, and residential burglary.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of several charges, including murder, for his attacks on three women. Defendant appealed and the court affirmed defendant's convictions. The court held that defendant was not in custody when he voluntarily confessed and the appointment of Keenan counsel was unnecessary. Thus, there was no abuse of discretion in failing to appoint a particular attorney. The requirements for joinder of these offenses were met because each involved sexually assaultive conduct and evidence of the commission of each offense was admissible as to the others. There was no error in the jury instructions on special circumstances, and manslaughter instructions were not warranted. The court found that there was no prosecutorial misconduct, and defendant received effective assistance of counsel. Evidence of prior violent crimes was admissible during the penalty phase. The jury was not entitled to consider sympathy for defendant's family in mitigation. Furthermore, the instructions on aggravating and mitigating factors were sufficient. It was unlikely that a different result would occur on retrial. Accordingly, there was no error in denying defendant's motion for new trial.


OUTCOME: Having found no error and that defendant received effective assistance of counsel, the court affirmed defendant's convictions of murder, kidnapping, forcible rape, forcible rape in concert, forcible oral copulation, forcible oral copulation in concert, rape by means of a foreign object, attempted forcible sodomy, attempted forcible sodomy in concert, assault with a deadly weapon, and residential burglary.


 

31 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Reyes


No. S058825.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


19 Cal. 4th 743; 968 P.2d 445; 80 Cal. Rptr. 2d 734; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 6006; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7304; 98 Daily Journal DAR 10095



September 21, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified December 2, 1998. Rehearing Denied December 2, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 8045. Certiorari Denied April 26, 1999, Reported at: 1999 U. S. LEXIS 2934.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County. Super. Ct. No. 35867. Kenneth E. Conn and Joseph A. Kalashian, Judges.

Original Opinion Previously Reported at: 19 Cal. 4th 229, 961 P.2d 984, 78 Cal. Rptr. 2d 295.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed. To the extent it is inconsistent with this opinion, Burgener is disapproved.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a judgment from the Court of Appeal (California), which reversed defendant's conviction for a drug-related parole violation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's parole agent received an anonymous tip that defendant was using drugs. Officers searched defendant's shed and discovered methamphetamine. The trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress, and defendant pled guilty. The court of appeal reversed defendant's conviction, holding that the search was not supported by reasonable suspicion. The court reversed the court of appeal's judgment. A search conducted pursuant to a proper parole search condition did not violate a parolee's expectation of privacy even where the search was not supported by reasonable suspicion. A parolee's expectation of privacy was already reduced by the absence of a warrant requirement. The threat of a suspicionless search was consistent with the deterrent purposes of the search condition. As long as a suspicionless search of a parolee was not arbitrary, capricious, or harassing, the search was reasonable within the meaning of U.S. Const. amend. IV. The court held that its ruling was retroactive and that no deterrent effect would be achieved from excluding evidence from past suspicionless searches of parolees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeal. A parolee's expectation of privacy was not violated where a search was not supported by reasonable suspicion, as long as the search was not arbitrary or harassing and conducted pursuant to a proper parole search condition.
