People v. Breverman


No. S058721.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


19 Cal. 4th 142; 960 P.2d 1094; 77 Cal. Rptr. 2d 870; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 5589; 98 Daily Journal DAR 9358; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6812



August 31, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published August 31, 1998.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superiro Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. PA015748. Charles L. Peven, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed insofar as it holds that defendant's murder conviction must be reversed because the heat of passion issue erroneously omitted from instructions on the lesser included offense of voluntary manslaughter was not necessarily resolved by the jury in another context. The cause is remanded to the Court of Appeal for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed from a decision of the Court of Appeals, Los Angeles County (California), which reversed defendant's conviction for murder on the ground that the trial court erred in failing to give a lesser included jury instruction on a "heat of passion" theory of voluntary manslaughter.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed a man who was among a group of men destroying defendant's car, claiming fear for his safety. Defendant was charged with murder, justifiable homicide, and the lesser necessarily included offenses of voluntary and involuntary manslaughter. The voluntary manslaughter instructions were premised entirely on the theory of unreasonable self-defense. Defendant was convicted of murder and challenged his conviction, claiming that the trial court erred by failing to instruct, sua sponte, on a heat of passion theory of voluntary manslaughter, Cal. Penal Code § 192(a), which was also supported by the evidence. The appellate court agreed with defendant, reversed his conviction, and remanded for a new trial. Upon further review, the court held that defendant was entitled to a jury instruction on every theory that was rationally supported by the evidence but that an automatic reversal was not required. Instead, a determination should be made as to whether the entire cause, including the evidence disclosing the error, produced a miscarriage of justice; accordingly, the court remanded the case to the appellate court for review under the court's standard for reversal.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment insofar as it held that defendant's murder conviction must be reversed because the heat of passion issue erroneously omitted from the instructions on the lesser included offense of voluntary manslaughter was not necessarily resolved by the jury in another context. The court remanded the cause to the appellate court for further proceedings.

 

33 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Birks


No. S057191.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


19 Cal. 4th 108; 960 P.2d 1073; 77 Cal. Rptr. 2d 848; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 5591; 98 Daily Journal DAR 9346; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6803



August 31, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published August 31, 1998.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County. Super. Ct. No. FSB05628. Duane M. Lloyd, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed insofar as it holds that defendant's conviction must be reversed for Geiger error. The cause is remanded to the Court of Appeal for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent state challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeals, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two (California), which held that the trial court committed reversible error in refusing to give a lesser included offense of trespassing during appellant's trial for burglary.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was arrested and charged with burglary after he was caught in a closed and locked restaurant. During the trial, appellant requested a lesser included offense charge of trespassing. The trial court refused, and appellant was found guilty of burglary. Appellant challenged his conviction, and the appellate court reversed, stating that under the Geiger precedent, the trial court was required to give the lesser included offense when appellant requested it, and it bore some relationship to the offense charged. Respondent state appealed the ruling, and the court held that Geiger was no longer good law, and was overruled. The court stated that Geiger was premised on a faulty foundation, and even under stare decisis, its premise was wrong. The court determined that Geiger was unfair to the prosecution, and violated separation of powers, by intruding on the executive branch's duties. The court concluded that appellant was not entitled to a lesser included offense, when he requested it, and it bore some conceptual and evidentiary relationship to the offense charged. The court remanded back to the appellate court for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court, and overruled the Geiger precedent, holding that appellant was not entitled to a jury instruction on a lesser included offense that bore some conceptual and evidentiary relationship to the offense alleged, when it was not charged by the prosecutor, and only requested by appellant. The court remanded for the lower court to consider appellant's other issues on appeal.

 

34 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Cortez


No. S055733.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


18 Cal. 4th 1223; 960 P.2d 537; 77 Cal. Rptr. 2d 733; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 5420; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6666; 98 Daily Journal DAR 9220



August 27, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied October 14, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 6654.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BA067687. Jacqueline A. Connor, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicting him of conspiracy to commit murder and sentencing him in accordance with a first degree murder charge.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was involved in a street gang-related shooting. The evidence at appellant's trial showed that appellant and a companion went to a location in order to retaliate against members of another gang by shooting one or more of their members. After a confrontation, appellant's companion was killed. Appellant was charged and convicted of conspiracy to commit murder in the death of his companion. On review, the court of appeals affirmed the conviction. On further review to this court, the conviction was also affirmed. The court held that the crime of conspiracy to commit murder was a unitary offense for which the punishment should be that prescribed for murder in the first degree in every instance. The court further held that, given this holding, there was no occasion or requirement for the jury in this case to further determine the degree of the underlying target offense of murder, and thus no need for specific instruction on premeditation and deliberation respecting the conspiracy charge. Accordingly, the judgment of the lower court was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Appellant's conviction for conspiracy to commit murder was affirmed because the court concluded that all conspiracy to commit murder was necessarily conspiracy to commit premeditated first degree murder and therefore punishable in the same manner as first degree murder.

Broadman v. Commission on Judicial Performance


No. S055684.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


18 Cal. 4th 1079; 959 P.2d 715; 77 Cal. Rptr. 2d 408; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 4830; 98 Daily Journal DAR 8633; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6247



August 10, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified September 2, 1998. Certiorari Denied January 11, 1999, Reported at: 1999 U.S. LEXIS 150.
Writ of certiorari denied Broadman v. California Comm'n on Judicial Performance, 525 U.S. 1070, 119 S. Ct. 801, 142 L. Ed. 2d 662, 1999 U.S. LEXIS 150 (1999)
Later proceeding at Inquiry concerning Broadman, 1999 Cal. Comm. Jud. Perform. LEXIS 2 (1999)



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, and by this order, Judge Broadman is publicly censured.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner judge sought modification or rejection of the recommendation of the respondent Commission on Judicial Performance that petitioner be publicly censured for conduct prejudicial to the administration of justice that brought the judicial office into disrepute.


OVERVIEW: Based on evidence presented at a public hearing before special masters appointed by the court, respondent commission determined that petitioner judge had engaged in prejudicial conduct and recommended public censure. Petitioner applied to the court to modify or reject respondent's recommendation. The court first determined that the scope of its authority allowed it to conclude that petitioner engaged in willful misconduct, even when respondent made a finding of prejudicial conduct and, further, that it could increase or decrease the discipline recommended by respondent. The court concluded that, when petitioner used deception to obtain a time waiver in a sentencing, there was clear and convincing evidence that his actions were willful misconduct, rather than prejudicial misconduct as found by respondent. Using a balancing test for the right of free speech, the court concluded petitioner's public comments about cases were prejudicial misconduct. Petitioner's actions in trying to influence a case against a personal enemy were private, not in his judicial capacity, and were therefore prejudicial conduct. The court agreed that the appropriate discipline was public censure.


OUTCOME: The court agreed with the recommendation of respondent commission and accordingly entered an order publicly censuring petitioner judge, based on the court's independent review of the evidence and a determination that petitioner's actions amounted to three instances of prejudicial conduct and one instance of willful misconduct.

In re Robbins


No. S048929.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


18 Cal. 4th 770; 959 P.2d 311; 77 Cal. Rptr. 2d 153; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 4691; 98 Daily Journal DAR 8368; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6042



August 3, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied September 23, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 6227.
Rehearing denied by In re Robbins, 1998 Cal. LEXIS 6227 (Cal., Sept. 23, 1998)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Robinson v. Walker, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 72625 (C.D. Cal., May 27, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County. Super. Ct. No. 140615. Ronald C. Stevens, Judge.
In re Gallego, 18 Cal. 4th 825, 77 Cal. Rptr. 2d 132, 959 P.2d 290, 1998 Cal. LEXIS 4690 (1998)
In re Robbins, 1998 Cal. LEXIS 5143 (Cal., Aug. 3, 1998)



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus to review a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California) that convicted him of murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of murder and sentenced to death after confessing to the rape and murder of a young boy. At trial, the prosecutor admitted evidence that petitioner had also confessed to a similar crime committed several months prior to the crime for which he was on trial. Several years later, as facts allegedly became known to petitioner, he applied for a writ of habeas corpus, asserting claims of misrepresentation, perjury, and improper suppression of evidence. The court issued an order to show cause and, after reviewing the claims, denied the relief as to all claims except three claims and three subclaims on the ground of timeliness. To avoid the bar of untimeliness with respect to his claims, petitioner had the burden of establishing the absence of substantial delay, good cause for the delay, or that the claim fell within an exception to the bar of untimeliness, none of which was shown by petitioner. Substantial delay was measured from the time petitioner or his counsel knew, or reasonably should have known, of the information offered in support of the claim and the legal basis for the claim. Recognized exceptions included constitutional error or actual innocence.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner relief on all claims except three claims and three subclaims.

Jordache Enterprises, Inc. v. Brobeck, Phleger & Harrison


No. S056954.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


18 Cal. 4th 739; 958 P.2d 1062; 76 Cal. Rptr. 2d 749; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 4687; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5893; 98 Daily Journal DAR 8219



July 30, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied September 30, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 6392.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BC021437. Aurelio Munoz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: This rule conforms to the language of the statute and the Legislature's intention to codify Budd, supra, 6 Cal. 3d 195. The rule recognizes that the diverse tasks and responsibilities attorneys undertake can create many occasions for error and injury. Thus, only the facts and circumstances of each case, analyzed in light of the alleged negligence and its consequences as revealed by the evidence, can establish when the plaintiff sustained actual injury under section 340.6. When the material facts are undisputed, the trial court can resolve the question as a matter of law according to the principles governing summary judgment. (Adams, supra, 11 Cal. 4th at p. 592 (lead opn. of Arabian, J.); Budd, supra, 6 Cal. 3d at p. 202; see § 437c.) Here, the trial court concluded the undisputed facts established that Jordache sustained actual injury after it discovered Brobeck's alleged neglect and more than one year before its suit was deemed filed. The Court of Appeal reversed that decision. We conclude the Court of Appeal erred in holding Jordache did not sustain actual injury before settlement of the insurance coverage litigation. The injurious consequences for Jordache of Brobeck's alleged omissions existed more than one year before Jordache sued. The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed. Because the Court of Appeal did not reach the other issues Jordache raised on appeal from the trial court's judgment, we remand the case for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent lawyer sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (California) which reversed the summary judgment awarded by the trial court against appellant client. The trial court's summary judgment was based on the grounds that appellant's cause of action was barred by the limitations period of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340.6.


OVERVIEW: More than a year before it filed a malpractice action, appellant learned of respondent attorney's alleged negligence in failing to timely claim liability insurance benefits covering a third party lawsuit against appellant and incurred defense costs in that suit. Appellant contended that it did not sustain actual injury until it settled its action against its insurer for less than full benefits. The trial court granted summary judgment to respondent, on the basis that the action was barred by the limitations period of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340.6. The court of appeals reversed that decision. This court held that the court of appeals erred. Appellant sustained actual injury more than a year before suit was filed, in unpaid defense costs in the third party suit. The damages were sufficiently mature that appellant tried to recover them in its insurance coverage suits. The alleged failure to advise appellant on insurance was not at issue in the coverage lawsuits and did not arise for the first time when appellant settled with the insurer. That litigation did not determine whether respondent breached its duty to appellant. The actual injuries ended tolling of limitations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals judgment that reversed the trial court grant of summary judgment for respondent lawyer. The court held that the court of appeals erred in holding that appellant did not sustain actual injury before settlement of the insurance coverage litigation because the injurious consequences of respondent's alleged omissions existed more than one year before appellant sued. The case was remanded.

Writ of habeas corpus denied Frye (Jerry Grant) on H.C., 2001 Cal. LEXIS 9205 (Cal., Jan. 24, 2001)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Frye v. Stokes, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 97368 (E.D. Cal., Dec. 1, 2006)
Related proceeding at Frye v. California, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 5188 (N.D. Cal., Jan. 14, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Amador County. Super. Ct. No. 14145. William T. Low, Judge. * 


* Retired judge of the San Diego Superior Court, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons stated herein, the judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Amador County (California) that convicted him of first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187, first degree robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211, residential burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 459, and the unlawful driving and taking of a vehicle in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 10851 and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a former co-worker, and defendant's girlfriend set up a campsite for the purpose of growing marijuana. An elderly couple occupied a cabin located near the site. After his girlfriend befriended the couple, defendant entered their cabin, shot both of them, and then fled the scene in their car. Appellant was convicted of first degree murder, first degree robbery, residential burglary, and the unlawful driving and taking of a vehicle and was sentenced to death. He appealed, and the court affirmed. Appellant raised several errors in his appeal. One was the trial court's denial of his motion to dismiss the special circumstances allegations on the ground the state failed to preserve exculpatory evidence. A defendant claiming a due process violation based on the failure to preserve evidence had to show the exculpatory value of the evidence at issue was apparent before it was destroyed and that comparable evidence could not be obtained by other reasonable means. He also had to show bad faith on the part of police in failing to preserve potentially useful evidence. Defendant made no showing that the uncollected evidence would have been exculpatory.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for first degree murder, first degree robbery, residential burglary, and the unlawful driving and taking of a vehicle was affirmed.

 

39 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Guiuan


No. S063097.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


18 Cal. 4th 558; 957 P.2d 928; 76 Cal. Rptr. 2d 239; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 4034; 98 Daily Journal DAR 7431; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5314



July 6, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County. Super. Ct. No. SC34790. H. James Ellis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, we affirm the judgment of the Court of Appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a decision of the Court of Appeal (California), which affirmed appellant's convictions despite her claim that the superior court erred in its instruction to the jury regarding accomplice testimony.


OVERVIEW: In appellant's trial for kidnapping and attempted first degree murder, three accomplice witnesses testified. The trial court gave the standard jury instruction that accomplice testimony should be "viewed with distrust." In response to appellant's argument that the trial court should have sua sponte tailored the instruction to state that it did not apply to testimony favorable to defendant, the court of appeal found error but affirmed the conviction on the ground the error was harmless. On further review, the court affirmed, finding that the trial court had, without objection from appellant, given the standard instruction relating to testimony of accomplice witnesses called by the prosecution; without objection by appellant, the trial court did not have a sua sponte obligation to parse the witness testimony to see if any of it was favorable to defendant. On a prospective basis, however, the supreme court set forth a refined jury instruction for accomplice testimony that substituted "with caution" language for the "with distrust" language and that applied to any and all testimony from accomplices, whether called by a criminal defendant or the state.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed. Appellant's convictions for kidnapping and attempted first degree murder were upheld, because the superior court did not have to sua sponte tailor its jury instruction on accomplice testimony as requested by appellant. The jury instruction concerning accomplice testimony was refined for future clarity.

People v. Flood


No. S059454.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


18 Cal. 4th 470; 957 P.2d 869; 76 Cal. Rptr. 2d 180; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 4033; 98 Daily Journal DAR 7349; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5266



July 2, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County. Super. Ct. No. 9506403. James J. Marchiano, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We conclude that the trial court's error in instructing the jury that Bridgeman and Gurney were peace officers was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. Accordingly, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals (California), which affirmed his conviction for evading a vehicle operated by a pursuing peace officer resulting in serious bodily injury and held that the trial court's error in instructing the jury that police officers were peace officers was harmless.


OVERVIEW: After police officers had stopped defendant's car for making an illegal U-turn, defendant sped off. During the pursuit, defendant's Cadillac hit a van, which flipped over and ejected its four occupants. The trial judge instructed the jury that the police officers involved in the chase were peace officers. Defendant was found guilty of evading a vehicle operated by a pursuing peace officer resulting in serious bodily injury, a violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 2800.3. On appeal, defendant contended that the jury instruction impermissibly removed an element of the crime from the jury. The court of appeals thought that the error was harmless. Under state law, the court abandoned the reversal-per-se rule in favor of the generally applicable prejudicial error test of Cal. Const. art. VI, § 13. In applying this test, the court concluded that the instructional error was harmless under the California constitution because no reasonable probability existed that the outcome of defendant's trial would have been different. Under federal law, the court also found that harmless error review applied because the instructional error was not structural.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for evading a vehicle operated by a pursuing peace officer resulting in serious bodily injury because it found that while the trial court committed error by instructing the jury that the officers were peace officers, which was an element of the crime, the error was harmless under both the federal and state constitutions.

 

41 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Tillis


No. S060909.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


18 Cal. 4th 284; 956 P.2d 409; 75 Cal. Rptr. 2d 447; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 3646; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4691; 98 Daily Journal DAR 6619



June 18, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin County. Super. Ct. No. SC057561B. Michael N. Garrigan.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant and the state appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals, California, which affirmed defendant's conviction. The court of appeals held that the state violated Cal. Penal Code § 1054.1 and denied defendant due process by failing to disclose, before trial, certain evidence used during cross-examination to impeach a defense expert witness, but found that the errors were harmless.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed defendant's conviction on different grounds than the court of appeals. The court held that the undisclosed information fell outside the scope of the discovery statute and there was no discovery violation under any interpretation of the intent requirement. The court held that to establish a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 1054.1(a) on appeal, the record must show that a specific witness or witnesses were known to and intended to be called by the state, but were not disclosed to defendant. Nothing in the record even hinted at the existence of a witness. Under Cal. Penal Code § 1054(e), the court could not expand the scope of discovery beyond Cal. Penal Code § 1054 et seq., or other statutory provisions, or as mandated by the federal constitution. Defendant did not identify any statute outside § 1054 et seq. that had any bearing on this case. The federal constitution did not mandate disclosure of nonexculpatory facts, such as defendant's drug use and arrest. The court disapproved Hobbs v. Municipal Court, 284 Cal. Rptr. 655 (1991), to the extent it concluded due process reciprocity requirements expanded the scope of discovery beyond the provisions of § 1054.1.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction, but on different grounds than the court of appeals. To establish a violation of the discovery statute on appeal, the record must show that a specific witness or witnesses were known to and intended to be called by the state, but were not disclosed to defendant. Defendant's drug use and arrest were not exculpatory facts that had to be disclosed.

 

42 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Bolin


No. S019786.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


18 Cal. 4th 297; 956 P.2d 374; 75 Cal. Rptr. 2d 412; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 3645; 98 Daily Journal DAR 6653; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4680



June 18, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified August 12, 1998. Rehearing Denied August 12, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 5197.
Certiorari Denied March 8, 1999, Reported at: 1999 U.S. LEXIS 1765.
Rehearing denied by People v. Bolin, 1998 Cal. LEXIS 5197 (Cal., Aug. 12, 1998)
Writ of certiorari denied Bolin v. California, 526 U.S. 1006, 119 S. Ct. 1146, 143 L. Ed. 2d 213, 1999 U.S. LEXIS 1765 (1999)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Bolin (Paul Clarence) on H.C., 2004 Cal. LEXIS 3049 (Cal., Apr. 14, 2004)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Bolin v. Ornoski, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 29578 (E.D. Cal., Sept. 29, 2005)
Related proceeding at Bolin v. Gilman, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 130505 (N.D. Cal., Sept. 16, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County. Super. Ct. No. 41477-A. Gerald K. Davis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which found him guilty of cultivation of marijuana, attempted first degree murder, and two counts of first degree murder, with special circumstances. The jury found true allegations of personal firearm use and a prior serious felony conviction, and imposed the death penalty on the special circumstance allegation of multiple murder under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3).


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from his convictions and sentences with emphasis on his conviction of two counts of first degree murder, with the special circumstance of multiple murder, and resultant death sentence. The court affirmed because defendant failed to renew his postponed motion for a change in venue, exhaust his peremptories, object to excusals of jurors, establish ineffective counsel, or show prejudice from his shackling or the jury's consideration of prior unadjudicated criminal acts. The trial court properly admitted relevant photographs of the victim, prior consistent statements, and testimony about escape and blood spatters, and instructed the jury on aggravating and mitigating factors and reasonable doubt. The court found that defendant waived objection and was not prejudiced by the crime scene view; by testimony of the victim's family; by a technical defect in the verdict form; or by instructions on defendant's concealment and flight, special circumstances, and lesser-included offenses. The court found that premeditation was supported by sufficient evidence of defendant's rapid planning, motive, and manner of killing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the capital conviction and death sentence because sufficient evidence supported the finding of premeditation, the trial court properly instructed the jury and admitted relevant evidence, and defendant, with the assistance of effective counsel, waived objection to venue, jury composition, and the crime scene view.

Shulman v. Group W Productions, Inc.


No. S058629.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


18 Cal. 4th 200; 955 P.2d 469; 74 Cal. Rptr. 2d 843; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 3190; 26 Media L. Rep. 1737; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4105; 98 Daily Journal DAR 5679



June 1, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified July 29, 1998. Rehearing Denied July 29, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 4846.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior County: Los Angeles. Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five. B081390. Los Angeles. Super. Ct. No. BC031250. Lillian M. Stevens, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed except insofar as the Court of Appeal reversed and remanded for further proceedings on Ruth Shulman's cause of action for publication of private facts.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff accident victims appealed a judgment from the Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five, which reversed in part and remanded a summary judgment for defendant producers on plaintiffs' claims for invasion of privacy and unlawful intrusion.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff accident victims' rescue from a serious accident was recorded and broadcast on defendant producers' television program. Plaintiffs sued, alleging two claims: invasion of privacy, based on unlawful intrusion by videotaping the rescue, and public disclosure of private facts, based on the broadcast. The lower court reversed and remanded a summary judgment for defendants, finding that issues of fact existed as to both causes of action. The court reversed in part, holding that summary judgment was proper as to the claim for publication of private facts but was improper as to the claim for intrusion. The broadcast was newsworthy as a matter of law and, therefore, could not be the basis for tort liability under a private facts claim. As to intrusion, triable issues existed as to whether defendants invaded plaintiffs' privacy by accompanying plaintiffs in the helicopter and as to whether defendants tortiously intruded by listening to plaintiff's confidential conversations with a nurse at the rescue scene, without plaintiff's consent. Moreover, defendants had no U.S. Const. amend. I privilege to intrude on plaintiffs' seclusion and private communications.


OUTCOME: The court reversed in part a decision reversing and remanding a summary judgment for defendant producers on plaintiff accident victims' claims for invasion of privacy and public disclosure of private facts. Summary judgment was proper as to the private facts claim because the broadcast was newsworthy, but triable issues existed as to the intrusion claim, and defendants had no privilege to intrude on plaintiffs' seclusion and private talks.

People v. Millwee


No. S014755.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


18 Cal. 4th 96; 954 P.2d 990; 74 Cal. Rptr. 2d 418; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2957; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3757; 98 Daily Journal DAR 5187



May 18, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied July 8, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 4332.
Certiorari Denied February 22, 1999, Reported at: 1999 U.S. LEXIS 1179.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County. Super. Ct. No. CR-27404. Edward D. Webster and Robert D. Macomber, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which found him guilty of first degree murder with use of a rifle, robbery, and burglary, plus two special circumstance allegations for robbery murder and burglary murder and sentenced him to death for the murder with special circumstances.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's disabled mother died after he shot her in the forehead in order to steal property from the family home. A jury convicted him of first degree murder with use of a rifle, robbery, and burglary, plus two special circumstance allegations for robbery murder and burglary murder and sentenced him to death. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that there was no evidence overlooked during the proceedings to show counsel misconduct, and that the prosecution's quest for a death sentence was an impartial and legitimate exercise of discretion. It was also proper to admit impeachment evidence of the victim's fear of him and his unrelated shooting two days later to assess his credibility. Also, the court held that the juror excused for good cause was not incompetent, because she was able to understand the nature of the proceedings. The court also found that the jury instructions without lesser-included offenses were proper, because the verdicts showed that they would not have adopted any lesser degrees. Further, the trial court properly reviewed the evidence and determined that aggravation substantially outweighed mitigation and that the jury verdict was justified.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and his death sentence, holding that there was no professional misconduct on the part of defendant's trial counsel or the prosecution, character evidence was properly admitted for impeachment purposes, the juror who was excused was not incompetent, the instructions to the jury were properly given, and the sentencing court's finding that aggravation outweighed mitigation justified the death sentence.

Post-conviction relief granted at, in part, Post-conviction relief denied at, in part Barnett v. Superior Court, 146 Cal. App. 4th 344, 54 Cal. Rptr. 3d 283, 2006 Cal. App. LEXIS 2108 (Cal. App. 3d Dist., 2006)

PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Butte County. Super. Ct. No. 91850. William R. Patrick, Judge.

DISPOSITION: For the reasons stated above, we find no reversible error in the record. The judgment of death is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Butte County (California), convicting him of murder, robbery, assault with a firearm, and kidnapping, and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of one count of murder, two counts of robbery, one count of assault with a firearm, and four counts of kidnapping. The jury found true the special circumstances that the murder was committed while defendant was engaged in the crimes of robbery and kidnapping, and that the murder was intentional and involved the infliction of torture. The jury also found true allegations that defendant personally used a firearm in the commission of the robberies and kidnappings. The jury sentenced defendant to death. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that defendant's claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was not well founded. The record did not indicate that defendant and his counsel had become embroiled in such an irreconcilable conflict that ineffective representation was likely to result. The court further found that in light of the strong evidence of defendant's motive and opportunity to commit the murder, it could not conclude it reasonably probable that the outcome would have been different had defense counsel relied solely on defendant's uncorroborated version of events and strenuously pressed the defense of total innocence.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting defendant of murder, robbery, assault with a firearm, and kidnapping, and sentencing him to death was affirmed.

Landgate, Inc. v. California Coastal Com.


No. S059847.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


17 Cal. 4th 1006; 953 P.2d 1188; 73 Cal. Rptr. 2d 841; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2461; 98 Daily Journal DAR 4453; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3200; 28 ELR 21236



April 30, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified April 30, 1998. Certiorari Denied October 5, 1998, 67 U.S.L. Week 3235.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BC024391. Richard C. Hubbell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For all the foregoing reasons, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed and the cause is remanded to the Court of Appeal. The Court of Appeal shall remand to the trial court with directions to grant the Commission's motion for summary judgment against Landgate's takings claim and to deny Landgate's motion for summary adjudication.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant California Coastal Commission challenged the judgment of the California Court of Appeal that affirmed the summary judgment entered by the trial court in appellee developer's favor. Appellant maintained that a delay in appellee's development occasioned by appellant's regulatory process did not constitute a taking of property so as to require it to compensate appellee.


OVERVIEW: Appellant California Coastal Commission denied appellee developer's application to change a lot line and build on land it owned. Subsequently, a court ruled for appellee on the lot line issue and ordered appellant to reconsider appellee's application. Appellee agreed to changes in its plans and appellant approved the amended application. Appellee filed an action against appellant to recover for what it argued was a temporary taking of its property during the application controversy when it was deprived of all economically viable use of its land. The trial court granted summary judgment for appellee on its taking claims and awarded it attorney's fees. The trial court denied appellant's motion for summary judgment. The intermediate appellate court affirmed. The court reversed. The court undertook an extensive review of United States Supreme Court and other decisions on the taking issue. The court observed that virtually every court that has examined the issue has concluded that a regulatory mistake that results in delay does not, by itself, amount to a taking. The court ruled that such delay is an incident of property ownership and is not a taking.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that was no taking of appellee developer's property that required compensation. The court reversed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court and remanded with directions that the cause be returned to the trial court with directions to grant appellant California Coastal Commission's motion for summary judgment against the appellee's takings claim and to deny appellee's motion for summary judgment.

 

47 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Walrath


No. S059170.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


17 Cal. 4th 907; 952 P.2d 1124; 72 Cal. Rptr. 2d 856; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1685; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2504; 98 Daily Journal DAR 3437



April 6, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Lake County. Super. Ct. No. 30284. John J. Golden, Judge *.


*  Retired judge of the Lake Superior Court, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed and the case remanded for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former husband sought review of an order of the Court of Appeal (California), which affirmed a trial court determination that there was no reimbursement right for separate property monies used to acquire, improve, or pay down the indebtedness of community assets, in the division of property between appellant and respondent former wife.


OVERVIEW: Appellant former husband and respondent former wife were divorced. In the property division action, appellant sought reimbursement for separate property contribution to community property where the proceeds from the original acquisition were subsequently used for other community property. The trial court denied the request and the court of appeal affirmed. Appellant sought review of that decision. The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeal and remanded the case. The court found that under Cal. Civ. Code § 2640, the phrase "the property" included not only specific community property to which the separate property was originally contributed, but also any other community property that was subsequently acquired from the proceeds of the initial property, and to which the separate property contribution could be traced.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the case. The court held that appellant former husband was entitled to reimbursement for specific community property acquired with separate monies, as well as other community property subsequently acquired from the proceeds of the original property, as long as those separate proceeds could be traced and identified.

 

48 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Dawn D. v. Superior Court


No. S060966.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


17 Cal. 4th 932; 952 P.2d 1139; 72 Cal. Rptr. 2d 871; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1686; 98 Daily Journal DAR 3423; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2511



April 6, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied December 14, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.S. LEXIs 8095.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County. Super. Ct. No. D-157628. William A. Anderson, Jr., Commissioner.



DISPOSITION: Because Jerry cannot establish a constitutionally protected liberty interest in being allowed to form a parental relationship with Dawn's child, application to this case of sections 7611 and 7630 does not deprive him of due process. The judgment of the Court of Appeal therefore is reversed, and the cause is remanded to that court with directions to issue a writ of mandate directing respondent Superior Court of Riverside County to vacate its order denying Dawn's motion for judgment on the pleadings and to enter a new order granting the motion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeal (California), which denied petitioner's request for a writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Riverside County to vacate its judgment. Respondent had denied petitioner's motion for judgment on the pleadings after the real party in interest filed a complaint to establish a parental relationship with petitioner's child, and respondent had ordered a blood test.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner mother separated from her husband and began living with the real party in interest. Petitioner became pregnant but shortly afterwards returned to her husband. The real party in interest filed a complaint to establish a parental relationship. Petitioner moved for judgment on the pleadings, asserting that the real party in interest could not assert a valid claim to be the father of her child. Respondent trial court denied the motion. The court of appeal denied petitioner's request for a writ of mandate to compel respondent to vacate its order. The court reversed the judgment. The real party's interest in establishing a relationship with his child, born to a woman married to another man at the time of conception and birth, was not constitutionally protected. The U.S. Supreme Court had previously rejected due process protection for this interest and had expressly rejected the view that an unwed father's biological link to a child by itself gave rise to a protected liberty interest. Thus, the presumption of Cal. Fam. Code § 7611 and the standing rule of Cal. Fam. Code § 7630 could constitutionally be applied to preclude the real party in interest from establishing paternity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeal's judgment and directed respondent trial court to vacate its order that had denied petitioner mother's motion for judgment on the pleadings. Petitioner was entitled to judgment on the pleadings because the real party in interest did not have a constitutionally protected interest in establishing a relationship with petitioner's child.

Hughes v. Board of Architectural Examiners


No. S056373.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


17 Cal. 4th 763; 952 P.2d 641; 72 Cal. Rptr. 2d 624; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1453; 98 Daily Journal DAR 3025; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2190



March 26, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied May 20, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 3198.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County. Super. Ct. No. CV375591. John R. Lewis, Judge. *


*  Judge of the Sacramento Municipal Court, assigned by the Chief Justice prusuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the judgment of the Court of Appeal and remand the matter to that court to permit it to decide the remaining issue that it previously did not consider--whether the Board imposed an excessive sanction in revoking Hughes's license. ( DaFonte v. UpRight, Inc. (1992) 2 Cal. 4th 593, 604-605 [7 Cal. Rptr. 2d 238, 828 P.2d 140]; Cal. Rules of Court, rule 29.4(b).)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant board sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeal (California) that reversed a trial court judgment affirming defendant's decision to revoke plaintiff architect's license for violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 5583 and 5584.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff architect, though unlicensed, established an architecture firm in Washington D.C. Plaintiff concealed the fact that he was unlicensed and even held himself out to be licensed. The D.C. licensing board eventually disciplined plaintiff. Shortly thereafter, plaintiff applied to defendant board for a license to practice in California. On the application form, plaintiff neglected to reveal his past discipline. A license was eventually issued; however, when defendant learned of plaintiff's past, it revoked his license for violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 5583 and 5584. The trial court affirmed this decision, but on appeal the revocation was reversed on the ground §§ 5583 and 5584 did not apply to prelicensure conduct. Defendant sought review, and the court reversed and remanded. Sections 5583 and 5584 were ambiguous. Therefore, the court had to consider evidence of the legislature's intent beyond the words of the statutes, including the statutory scheme, the history and background of the statutes, and any considerations of constitutionality. The court concluded that the legislature did not intend these provisions to be limited only to postlicensure conduct.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversing a trial court judgment affirming defendant board's decision to revoke plaintiff architect's license was reversed and remanded. The disciplinary statutes under which plaintiff was charged applied to both prelicensure and postlicensure conduct.

Curran v. Mount Diablo Council of the Boy Scouts


No. S039738.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


17 Cal. 4th 670; 952 P.2d 218; 72 Cal. Rptr. 2d 410; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1451; 98 Daily Journal DAR 2802; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2034



March 23, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. C365529. Sally Grant Disco, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal, upholding the judgment rendered by the trial court in favor of defendant, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, whose application for assistant scoutmaster was rejected by defendant regional council because of his homosexuality, challenged the judgment of the California Court of Appeals, which affirmed a trial court decision not to preclude defendant from rejecting plaintiff's application and held that applying the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 51, violated the defendants' members' federal constitutional right of association.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was a former Eagle Scout whose application to become an assistant scoutmaster was rejected by defendant, a regional council of the Boy Scouts of America, after plaintiff publicly stated that he was a homosexual and publicly expressed his commitment to communicating to others his view as to the acceptability and morality of homosexuality. Plaintiff unsuccessfully sought an injunction prohibiting defendant from rejecting his application. The trial court concluded that application of the Unruh Civil Rights Act (Act), Cal. Civ. Code § 51, to preclude defendant from rejecting plaintiff's application violated the members' federal constitutional right of association. The intermediate appellate court affirmed the judgment, but held that defendant did not fall within the category of business establishments subject to the Act. The supreme court affirmed and held that, given the organization's overall purpose and function, the Boy Scouts reasonably could not be found to be a business establishment whose membership decisions were subject to the Act, as it was an expressive social organization whose primary function was to inculcate values in its youth members.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's decision to affirm the trial court's decision not to preclude defendant, regional council of the Boy Scouts of America, from rejecting plaintiff's application for assistant scoutmaster. The court held that with regard to its membership decisions, defendant was not a business establishment within the meaning of the Unruh Civil Rights Act.

Stop Youth Addiction, Inc. v. Lucky Stores, Inc.


No. S055373.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


17 Cal. 4th 553; 950 P.2d 1086; 71 Cal. Rptr. 2d 731; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 902; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1765; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1280



February 23, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County. Super. Ct. No. 743347-6. Ken M. Kawaichi, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the preceding reasons, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant retailer sought review of the decision from the Court of Appeal, Sixth District (California), which held that plaintiff, a private for-profit corporation, could maintain an action against defendant under the unfair competition law, Cal. Bus. and Prof. Code §§ 17200 to 17209, on behalf of the general public.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, a private, for-profit corporation, brought an action under the unfair competition law, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 17200 to 17209 (UCL), on behalf of the general public to prevent defendant retailer from selling cigarettes to minors in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 308. The trial court found that plaintiff could not bring the action, whereas the appellate court found that it could. Because selling cigarettes to minors violated the law, it violated the UCL as well. Case law held that UCL actions could be brought by private plaintiffs who had themselves suffered no injury, and the language of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17204 suggested that corporations did not have to demonstrate standing either. That Cal Penal Code § 308 and the Stop Tobacco Access to Kids Enforcement Act, Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 22950- 22959, did not contain an express private right of enforcement did not mean that suit under the UCL was preempted or impliedly repealed. The court found that defendant's litigation strategy and its counsel's motives were not reviewable. Accordingly, the court affirmed the appellate court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court that allowed plaintiff to bring suit against defendant because the statutory language suggested that private corporations could bring suit under the unfair competition law to compel compliance with criminal laws without suffering an injury. Other laws regulating the sale of tobacco to minors did not impliedly repeal the unfair competition law.

People v. Dennis


No. S007210.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


17 Cal. 4th 468; 950 P.2d 1035; 71 Cal. Rptr. 2d 680; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 483; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1651; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1174



February 19, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied April 15, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2226. Certiorari Denied October 5, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.S. LEXIS 6239.
Rehearing denied by People v. Dennis, 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2226 (Cal., Apr. 15, 1998)
Writ of certiorari denied Dennis v. California, 525 U.S. 912, 119 S. Ct. 257, 142 L. Ed. 2d 211, 1998 U.S. LEXIS 6239 (1998)
Motion granted by People v. Dennis, 2013 Cal. LEXIS 7958 (Cal., Sept. 25, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County. Super. Ct. No. 97196. John Schatz, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) that convicted him of first degree murder and second degree murder and that imposed the death penalty upon him.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder for killing his former wife and second degree murder for killing her fetus. On appeal, the court rejected his contention that the jury should have been instructed on Cal. Penal Code § 12022.9 (1985) as a lesser-included offense or a related offense to fetal murder. Section 12022.9 defined a sentence enhancement for inflicting injury upon a pregnant victim that resulted in the termination of a pregnancy, not a substantive offense. Thus, it was not an alternative or a lesser-included offense of fetal murder. Also, the jury did not have to be instructed on manslaughter as a lesser-included offense for the death of the fetus because there was no crime of manslaughter of a fetus. The court further held that the multiple-murder special circumstances that allowed the imposition of the death penalty applied because the evidence was sufficient to show that defendant had acted with express malice toward the fetus. Also, defendant waived his prosecutorial misconduct claims because he did not object to them, and an admonition would have been useless to cure them.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions and sentence.

Moss v. Superior Court


No. S057081.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


17 Cal. 4th 396; 950 P.2d 59; 71 Cal. Rptr. 2d 215; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 29; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1141; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 852

*  Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner contemnor father sought a writ of mandamus to set aside the contempt judgment of respondent Superior Court of Riverside County (California) for failure to pay child support.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, real party in interest mother, brought an action petitioner contemnor father for contempt to pay child support. Petitioner alleged that he was unable to pay child support because he was unemployed. Respondent mother alleged that petitioner refused to seek employment. The trial court held petitioner in contempt and imposed a sentence of 5 days in jail for each of 6 counts of contempt and 10 hours of community service for each of 6 counts. Petitioner alleged that an order to force him to seek employment violated his U.S. Const. amend. XIII right against involuntary servitude. The appellate court set aside the contempt judgment and concluded that the Todd decision was controlling. The court overruled its position in Todd to the extent that Todd held that it was unconstitutional to impose a contempt sanction on a parent who refused to work. Because petitioner justifiably relied on the Todd decision, the court affirmed the ruling of the appellate court.


OUTCOME: The court overruled its previous holding in the Todd decision that a parent who refused to work could not be held in contempt. Because petitioner contemnor father reasonably relied on case precedent, the court refused to apply its ruling retroactively and affirmed the appellate court's reversal of the trial court's finding of contempt.

People v. Jones


No. S021683.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


17 Cal. 4th 279; 949 P.2d 890; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 793; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 23; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 789; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1025



January 29, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied March 18, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1688. Certiorari Denied October 13, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.S. LEXIS 6524.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. A578017. Charles C. Lee, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We direct the trial court to modify the sentence on count 2 (see ante, p. 310), amend the abstract of judgment accordingly, and forward a copy of the amended abstract of judgment to the Department of Corrections. In all other respects, we affirm the judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendant of murder following sexual offenses and other felonies, and sentenced defendant to death.


OVERVIEW: The district attorney filed an information, which charged defendant with murder, kidnapping for the purpose of robbery, second degree robbery, forcible rape while acting in concert, and forcible oral copulation. A jury convicted defendant of all offenses and found true all the allegations. Following a penalty trial, the jury returned a verdict of death on the murder count, and the court entered judgment accordingly. On the other counts, the court sentenced defendant to life imprisonment without possibility of parole for kidnapping for robbery, five years for second degree robbery, nine years for forcible rape while acting in concert, and eight years for forcible oral copulation. The court held that the sentence imposed on the conviction of kidnapping for robbery was improper because the correct sentence was ordinary life imprisonment, but that defendant failed to show that the trial court abused its discretion or that defendant was denied the effective assistance of counsel. Therefore, the court modified the sentence with respect to the conviction for kidnapping for robbery, but affirmed the judgment in all other respects.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the defendant's convictions for murder, kidnapping for the purpose of robbery, second degree robbery, forcible rape while acting in concert, and forcible oral copulation, but modified the sentence on the conviction of kidnapping for the purpose of robbery because the court held that the correct sentence was ordinary life imprisonment.

Pitts v. County of Kern


No. S057270.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


17 Cal. 4th 340; 949 P.2d 920; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 823; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 21; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1039; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 800



January 29, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court. Kern County. Super.Ct. Nos. 215245 & 218820. Stephen P. Gildner, Rebecca A. Wiseman and John I. Kelly, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, and the case is remanded to that court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent county requested review of the decision of the appellate court which reversed the decision of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which entered summary judgment in favor of respondent in appellants' action for damages incurred as the result of alleged prosecutorial misconduct.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were convicted of numerous sex offenses committed against several children. The convictions were later reversed on appeal because of prosecutorial misconduct and appellants were released from state prison. The district attorney dismissed the charges and all of the child witnesses recanted and claimed their testimony was coerced. Appellants filed a complaint against the county which alleged in part civil rights violations based on alleged misconduct during the criminal prosecution. The trial court entered summary judgment in favor of respondent county because the trial court concluded that respondent was absolutely immune from liability for any act for which the district attorney was immune. The court of appeals reversed the trial court's decision. The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals because the court held that the district attorney represents the state, not the county, when preparing to prosecute and when prosecuting crimes, and when establishing policy and training employees in these areas and neither a state nor state officials may be found liable under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals because the district attorney represents the state, not the county, when preparing to prosecute and when prosecuting crimes, and when establishing policy and training employees in these areas and neither a state nor state officials may be found liable under civil right statute.

 

56 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Rodriguez


No. S055670.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


17 Cal. 4th 253; 949 P.2d 31; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 334; 1998 Cal. LEXIS 10; 98 Daily Journal DAR 508; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 378



January 15, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by People v. Rodriguez, 2017 Cal. App. Unpub. LEXIS 6387 (Cal. App. 2d Dist., Sept. 18, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BA094493. Bernard J. Kamins, Judge.
People v. Rodriguez, 47 Cal. App. 4th 424, 54 Cal. Rptr. 2d 801, 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 662 (Cal. App. 2d Dist., July 16, 1996)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed to the extent it is inconsistent with this opinion and the case remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision from a Court of Appeal (California), which denied defendant's petition for rehearing of its decision directing the trial court on remand to consider, outside of defendant's presence, whether it wished to exercise its discretion in dismissing prior felony conviction allegations against defendant for purposes of the Three Strikes law, Cal. Penal Code §§ 667(d)(1) and 1192.7(b)(1).


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of possessing cocaine base for sale. The trial court found defendant had previously been convicted of three serious felonies as defined in the Three Strikes law, Cal. Penal Code §§ 667(d)(1) and 1192.7(b)(1). The trial court did not strike any of the three prior felony conviction findings. The lower court remanded the decision with instructions that the trial court consider its discretionary authority to dismiss prior felony conviction allegations. However, the remand order did not require the trial court to conduct such inquiry in defendant's presence. Defendant argued this procedure violated his statutory and constitutional rights to be present with counsel at sentencing and pronouncement of judgment. The state supreme court reversed the lower court's decision to the extent it was inconsistent. Pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1385, it was just under the circumstances to require the presence of defendant and his counsel on remand, at the first occasion on which the trial judge was to consider whether to exercise his sentencing discretion in defendant's favor. The court also reversed the lower court's finding that an assault conviction constituted a strike.


OUTCOME: The lower court's decision directing the trial court to determine whether to exercise its discretionary authority with regard to dismissing a prior felony conviction for purposes of the Three Strikes law was reversed and, on remand, the trial court was instructed to conduct such hearing in the presence of defendant and his counsel. The state supreme court also reversed the lower court's finding that an assault conviction constituted a strike.

 

57 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Fuhrman


No. S055920.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


16 Cal. 4th 930; 941 P.2d 1189; 67 Cal. Rptr. 2d 1; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4982; 97 Daily Journal DAR 11246; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6975



August 28, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County. Super. Ct. No. CR 58011. W. Charles Morgan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order entered by the Court of Appeals (California), which affirmed his conviction, and enhanced sentence, for robbery and unlawful taking of an automobile, by imposing the penalties accorded by the Three Strikes Law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(b) through (i). Defendant claimed that his sentence should not have been enhanced because the two prior convictions were not brought and tried separately from each other.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to having his sentence enhanced by the Three Strikes Law, Cal. Penal Code §§ 667(b)- (i), defendant claimed that because his two prior convictions arose out of the same transaction and were not brought or tried separately, the trial court erred in not striking one of the prior convictions. The court affirmed the enhanced sentence. The court held that when a defendant had been convicted of more than one violent or serious felony offense in a single prior proceeding, each conviction may be counted as a strike under the Three Strikes Law. The court found that because the statutory language was clear and unambiguous, and because the phrase brought and tried separately was not expressly mentioned in §§ 667 (b)-(i), it was not necessary for the two prior offenses to result from separate incidents. The court stated that after defendant's present conviction, it held in a different case, that a trial court had discretion to strike one of the prior offenses and preclude enhancement in the interests of justice. The court was unaware if the trial court knew it had discretion to strike an offense; consequently, it suggested that defendant file a writ of habeas corpus.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's enhanced sentence under the Three Strikes Law. The court held that when a defendant had been convicted of more than one violent or serious felony offense in a single prior proceeding, each conviction may be counted as a strike under the enhancement statute.

 

58 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Manuel G.


No. S049304.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


16 Cal. 4th 805; 941 P.2d 880; 66 Cal. Rptr. 2d 701; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4976; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6853; 97 Daily Journal DAR 11084



August 26, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County. Super. Ct. No. J-147372. Ronald E. Owen, Judge..



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, and the case is remanded to that court for consideration of the additional contentions raised on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state petitioned for review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which reversed defendant juvenile's conviction under Cal. Penal Code § 69 for attempting by means of threats to deter a deputy sheriff from performing his duties. The reversal was based on a finding that the sheriff was not engaged in lawful performance of duties at the time of defendant's threats.


OVERVIEW: The state challenged the reversal of defendant juvenile's conviction under Cal. Penal Code § 69 for attempting by means of threats to deter a deputy sheriff from performing his duties. The sheriff had asked to talk to defendant at the time of defendant's threats. The court reversed and remanded the judgment, finding that defendant's threats to kill the officer and his fellow officers was an attempt to deter the sheriff's department's future investigation of gang-related activities and future contacts with defendant or other gang members. The court held that the encounter with defendant was consensual because the officer got out of his patrol car, defendant continued walking toward him, the officer asked whether they could talk, and defendant responded to his questions. The officer did not draw his gun or deter or stop defendant from continuing what he was doing. The officer approached defendant in a public place and asked questions, and this did not constitute coercive police conduct that would lead a reasonable person to believe that he was not free to leave. The officer was, therefore, engaged in lawful activity at the time that defendant threatened him.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded because defendant juvenile attempted to deter the officer from the performance of his duties in the future and the officer was acting lawfully at the time defendant made the threats.

 

59 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Kavanau v. Santa Monica Rent Control Bd.


No. S051847.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


16 Cal. 4th 761; 941 P.2d 851; 66 Cal. Rptr. 2d 672; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4977; 97 Daily Journal DAR 11069; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6858



August 26, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied January 20, 1998, 66 U.S.L.W. 3472.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BC 103933. Ricardo A. Torres.



DISPOSITION: Kavanau I determined that application of the Rent Board's 12 percent limit on rent increases violated Kavanau's right to due process. The remedy of future rent adjustments available to Kavanau under the due process clause precludes a finding of a taking in this case. Accordingly, we affirm the judgment of the Court of Appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant rental property owner challenged the decision of the Court of Appeal (California) that the trial court properly sustained the demurrer of respondent rent board to appellant's claims that a prior judgment finding respondent's rent limitations to violate appellant's due process rights entitled appellant to just compensation under the takings clauses of the federal and state constitutions and to damages under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OVERVIEW: In a prior case, appellant rental property owner obtained a judgment that rent limits set by respondent rent board denied him an adequate investment return and thus violated his due process rights. In this second suit, appellant sought just compensation for a taking under Cal. Const. art. I, § 19 and U.S. Const. amend. V, and damages under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The trial and intermediate appellate courts sustained respondent's demurrer. The supreme court affirmed. After reviewing general due process and unjust taking principles in the context of rent control, and seriously questioning appellant's first judgment, the court found that appellant had abandoned his § 1983 claim, leaving only the taking issue. The court concluded there had been no taking because neither of the two discrete taking categories, physical property invasion, or total loss of economic benefit, applied and under a multivariate balancing test, no taking was suggested. Because there was an available and adequate remedy for the due process violation found in the first case, namely a future increase in authorized rents, the court rejected appellant's claim that the first judgment mandated a finding of a taking.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the decision of the court of appeal, which held that the trial court properly sustained respondent rent board's demurrer to appellant rental property owner's claims seeking just compensation for property taking and damages for violation of the federal civil rights statute. Appellant had abandoned the civil rights damages claim and could prove no taking within the meaning of either the state or federal constitutions.

Los Angeles County Metropolitan Transportation Authority v. Continental Development Corp.


No. S051436.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


16 Cal. 4th 694; 941 P.2d 809; 66 Cal. Rptr. 2d 630; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4975; 97 Daily Journal DAR 11021; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6779



August 25, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied November 12, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 7679.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BC 009 683. Harvey A. Schneider, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed and the cause remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Los Angeles County Metropolitan Transportation Authority, filed an appeal from a judgment of a Court of Appeal (California), which affirmed a trial court judgment in appellant's condemnation action awarding appellee landowner compensation for property taken and for severance damages.


OVERVIEW: To acquire a strip of land for construction of a rail line, appellant, a transit authority, brought a condemnation action. Appellant proffered evidence that appellee landowner's remaining property would increase in value as a result of proximity to a station. Ruling the evidence inadmissible, the trial court found no special benefit because the station would be shared by numerous properties in the area. The court of appeal affirmed the trial court's award of compensation for property taken and severance damages. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded for a new trial on severance damages. The court concluded that the distinction in the case law between general and special benefits, whereby only special benefits could be set off against severance damages, no longer found support. The court overruled Beveridge v. Lewis, 137 Cal. 619, the case that applied the distinction. The court held that in determining entitlement to severance damages, the fact finder had to consider competent evidence relevant to any conditions caused by the project that affected the remainder property's fair market value, insofar as such evidence was neither conjectural nor speculative.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeal's judgment and remanded for a new trial on severance damages because the court concluded that the distinction between general and special benefits no longer found support in its original reasons. The court abolished the distinction. The court also ruled that the court of appeal erred in finding that the trial court abused its discretion in denying appellee landowner's motion for litigation expenses.

 

61 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Am. Acad. of Pediatrics v. Lungren


No. S041459.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


16 Cal. 4th 307; 940 P.2d 797; 66 Cal. Rptr. 2d 210; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4411; 97 Daily Journal DAR 10141; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6151



August 5, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Francisco County. Super. Ct. No. 884574. Maxine M. Chesney, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, we conclude that Assembly Bill 2274 violates the right of privacy set forth in article I, section 1, of the California Constitution. The judgment of the Court of Appeal, upholding the judgment of the trial court, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state attorney general appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeal (California), affirming the trial court's judgment, which sustained plaintiff organizations' facial challenge to a law requiring a pregnant minor to obtain parental consent or judicial authorization for an abortion and held that the law violated the minor's right to privacy under Cal. Const. art. I, § 7.


OVERVIEW: While California law permitted a minor to obtain pregnancy-related medical care without parental consent, the legislature amended former Cal. Health & Safety Code § 25958 to require parental consent or judicial authorization to obtain an abortion. In plaintiff organizations' action challenging the statute's constitutionality, the trial court and the appellate court agreed that the statute violated the right to privacy guaranteed under Cal. Const. art. I, § 7. On appeal, defendant attorney general maintained that the United States Supreme Court upheld similar parental consent laws under the federal constitution. The court responded that the state constitutional right of privacy was broader than the federal constitutional right. The statute in question intruded upon a fundamental autonomy privacy interest. The state's asserted compelling interests in protecting the health of minors and preserving parent-child relationships were undermined because the law permitted minors to obtain other medical care and the statute impeded the state's interests. The court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment. A statute requiring a pregnant minor to obtain parental consent or judicial authorization for an abortion was properly determined to be unconstitutional because the law infringed upon a minor's fundamental right to privacy guaranteed by the California Constitution.

People v. Williams


No. S004720.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


16 Cal. 4th 153; 940 P.2d 710; 66 Cal. Rptr. 2d 123; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4410; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6192; 97 Daily Journal DAR 10025



August 4, 1997, Decided

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment by which the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicted him of first-degree murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: During the guilt phase of the trial, the prosecutor introduced evidence that defendant shot the victim for being a member of a rival gang and that defendant directed intimidation of the state's eyewitness by having guns fired upon her occupied home. Without testifying in his own defense, defendant proffered an alibi that he was with his girlfriend through the testimony of other witnesses. During the penalty phase of the trial, the prosecutor introduced evidence of defendant's involvement in prior gang violence. Defendant testified that he dropped out of the gang when his five-year old son was born. The jury found defendant guilty of first degree murder and sentenced him to death. On appeal, the court upheld the admissibility of gang-related evidence and witness intimidation evidence during the penalty phase. The court overruled defendant's argument that the trial court's admission of gang paraphernalia during the penalty phase constituted error. After rejecting many other arguments, the court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for first degree murder and death sentence were affirmed. The court found no reversible error in any of points of error, which included challenges to the jury composition, the admissibility of gang membership, prior gang violence, and witness intimidation evidence, ineffective assistance of counsel, and improper jury instructions.
