People v. Bradford


No. S005707.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


15 Cal. 4th 1229; 939 P.2d 259; 65 Cal. Rptr. 2d 145; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 3699; 97 Daily Journal DAR 8941; 97 Daily Journal DAR 9003; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5537



July 14, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied September 17, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 5694.
Certiorari Denied May 18, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.S. LEXIS 3120, 66 U.S.L. Week 3747.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Super. Ct. No. A089976. Paul Boland, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's death sentence was automatically appealed from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), after a jury convicted defendant of two counts of first-degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189 and found true the special circumstance that defendant had committed multiple murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3).


OVERVIEW: An automatic appeal followed the imposed death penalty after a jury convicted defendant of two counts of first-degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189 and after the jury found true the special circumstance that defendant had committed multiple murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3). The court affirmed the judgment. There was sufficient evidence to support the jury's guilt findings. Evidence seized from defendant's apartment and vehicle was properly admitted because police had probable cause to execute a search warrant and had a reasonable belief that they could search for items that were not listed in the warrant. The search was not general or exploratory. Statements defendant made to police were properly admitted because they were made after defendant waived his Miranda rights and did not violate defendant's privilege against self-incrimination. Defendant properly waived his right to counsel and properly invoked his right to self-representation during the penalty phase. There were sufficient aggravating circumstances under Cal. Penal Code § 190.3, including defendant's previous violent activities, to support the imposition of the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a trial court decision that, after a jury trial, convicted defendant of two first-degree murder courts, found true the special circumstance the defendant committed multiple murder, and sentenced defendant to death. There was sufficient evidence to support the jury's finding during the guilt phase. There were sufficient aggravating circumstances, including defendant's history of violence, to support the imposed death penalty.

People v. Scott


No. S010334.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


15 Cal. 4th 1188; 939 P.2d 354; 65 Cal. Rptr. 2d 240; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 3700; 97 Daily Journal DAR 8931; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5574



July 14, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied September 3, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 5546. Certiorari Denied March 23, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.S. LEXIS 1918.
Rehearing denied by People v. Scott, 1997 Cal. LEXIS 5546 (Cal., Sept. 3, 1997)
Writ of certiorari denied Scott v. California, 523 U.S. 1025, 140 L. Ed. 2d 475, 118 S. Ct. 1310, 1998 U.S. LEXIS 1918 (1998)
Habeas corpus proceeding at In re Scott, 29 Cal. 4th 783, 129 Cal. Rptr. 2d 605, 61 P.3d 402, 2003 Cal. LEXIS 525 (2003)
Application granted by People v. Scott, 2004 Cal. LEXIS 1797 (Cal., Feb. 24, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Super. Ct. No. A747321. Margaret M. Grignon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On automatic appeal, defendant challenged his conviction from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for first-degree murder, for which defendant received the death penalty. Defendant claimed, among other things, that his conviction violated the double jeopardy clauses of the state and federal constitutions, that his waiver of a jury trial was invalid, and that his counsel rendered ineffective assistance in both trial phases.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for first-degree murder and his sentence of death. Defendant burned the victim to death after raping and beating her. Prior to the victim's death, defendant pled guilty to rape and assault of the victim. The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence. Among the claims rejected by the court was defendant's claim that his conviction violated double jeopardy. The court ruled that because the state could not proceed against defendant for murder until after the victim died, he was not placed in jeopardy for murder until that time. Thus, his prior guilty plea did not bar his subsequent prosecution. The court then rejected defendant's claims that his waiver of a jury trial was invalid. The court ruled that the record established that defendant and counsel expressly, knowingly, and voluntarily waived a jury trial as to all phases and that the trial court was thus required to accept the waiver. The court rejected defendant's claim that he received ineffective assistance of counsel in both phases of his trial. The court examined the numerous alleged errors and ruled that such were meritless or that counsel's decisions were valid, tactical decisions.


OUTCOME: Defendant's first-degree murder conviction and death sentence were affirmed. Because defendant was not chargeable with murder charge until the victim died, his prior guilty plea to rape and assault of the victim did not bar subsequent prosecution. Record established that his waiver of a jury trial was expressly, knowingly, and voluntarily made. Ineffective assistance claims were based upon meritless alleged errors or valid tactical decisions.

 

65 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Ramos


No. S005499.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


15 Cal. 4th 1133; 938 P.2d 950; 64 Cal. Rptr. 2d 892; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 2974; 97 Daily Journal DAR 8678; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5405



July 7, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied September 17, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 5693. Certiorari Denied March 23, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.S. LEXIS 1949.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, Super. Ct. No. C-42382. Francisco P. Briseno, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On automatic appeal, defendant challenged his convictions and sentence from the Superior Court of Orange County (California) for murder and robbery, for which he received the death sentence. Defendant claimed, among other things, that the trial court erred in not instructing the jury on intent and in denying his motion to suppress, that the jury venire was underrepresentative of Hispanics, and testimony from a jail-mate was improperly admitted.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his murder and robbery-related convictions and death sentence. Cal. Penal Code § 1239(b). The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentence. The court rejected defendant's numerous claims. Notably, the court ruled that: (1) the trial court properly did not instruct the jury on defendant's intent to kill because, under applicable case law, such an instruction was not required because defendant admitted that he committed the murder during the commission of a robbery; (2) the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion to quash the jury venire because defendant did not show a prima facie violation of right to have a fair cross-section of the community in the jury venire because there was no significant disparity between the number of Hispanics eligible for and the number reporting for jury service and there was no showing of systematic exclusion; (3) under the law of the case doctrine, the trial court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress, and; (4) testimony from defendant's jail-mate which related defendant's admission to the crimes, was relevant, was not inherently unreliable, and was thus properly admitted into evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and death sentence. Because defendant admitted to murder, an instruction on his intent was unnecessary. Under the law of the law the case doctrine, trial court properly denied his renewed motion to suppress. Defendant did not show a prima facie violation of an improper cross-section in the jury venire. Incriminating testimony from defendant's jail-mate was relevant and was not inherently unreliable.

Sinclair Paint Co. v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. S054115.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


15 Cal. 4th 866; 937 P.2d 1350; 64 Cal. Rptr. 2d 447; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 2967; 97 Daily Journal DAR 8242; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5059



June 26, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District. C021559. Superior Court of Sacramento County, Super. Ct. No. CV541310. Joe S. Gray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal, affirming the trial court's grant of summary judgment in Sinclair's favor, is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged a judgment of the Third Appellate District Court of Appeal (California), affirming a trial court judgment that granted summary judgment in favor of appellee paint company in its action for a refund of fees paid under the Childhood Lead Poisoning Prevention Act of 1991 (Act), Cal. Health & Saf. Code § 105275 et seq. Appellant contended the court erred in finding that the fees were an unconstitutional tax.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to the Childhood Lead Poisoning Prevention Act of 1991 (Act), Cal. Health & Saf. Code § 105275 et seq., appellant state imposed a fee of nearly $ 100,000 on appellee paint company. After appellee's administrative claim for refund was denied, appellee filed suit alleging that fees assessed under the Act were actually taxes imposed in violation of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 3. The trial court awarded appellee a refund of the fees it paid under the Act, and the appeals court affirmed. Appellant sought further review, arguing that the appeals court erred in affirming the trial court's finding that the fees were an unconstitutional tax. The reviewing court reversed. The court ruled that because the fees could be reasonably characterized as regulatory fees rather than taxes, the trial court erred in granting appellee's motion for summary judgment on the constitutional claims. The court based its decision that the fees were not taxes on the fact that they were charged in connection with regulatory activities, they did not exceed the reasonable cost of providing services necessary to the prevention of childhood lead poisoning and were not levied for unrelated revenue purposes.


OUTCOME: The reviewing court reversed the judgment awarding appellee paint company a refund of fees paid under the Childhood Lead Poisoning Prevention Act because the fees could be reasonably characterized as regulatory fees rather than taxes.

68 of 1449 DOCUMENTS




People v. Samayoa


No. S006284.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


15 Cal. 4th 795; 938 P.2d 2; 64 Cal. Rptr. 2d 400; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 2966; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4760; 97 Daily Journal DAR 7699



June 19, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified August 13, 1997. Rehearing Denied August 13, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Super. Ct. No. 84618. Douglas R. Woodworth, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), entering his convictions for first degree murder and residential burglary, in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189, 459, and his death penalty sentence. Defendant claimed various grounds for reversal, including that jury selection was improperly conducted and that he was denied effective assistance of a psychologist.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and residential burglary, in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189, and 459 and was sentenced to death. Defendant appealed, claiming inter alia, that there was no transcript of vital off-the-record discussions, that a jury skewed in favor of the death penalty was chosen, that his confessions were improperly denied suppression, and that he was denied effective assistance of a psychologist. The court affirmed. Because defendant failed to demonstrate that the discussion constituted judicial proceedings, defendant failed to prove that record was inadequate to permit meaningful appellate review of any issue raised upon appeal. The court found the trial court did not allow the selection of jurors that exhibited a bias towards the death penalty because the court employed the same standard when ruling on both parties' challenges. Defendant's confessions were properly admitted where defendant's refusal to have his confession tape-recorded was immaterial to the question of the voluntariness of his confessions. Defendant had no federal constitutional right to the effective assistance of a psychologist. Thus, the conviction was upheld.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's entry of defendant's conviction for first degree murder and residential burglary and death sentence. The appellate court found no merit to any of defendant's grounds for reversal. Defendant's confessions were admissible because his refusal to have confessions taped did not bear on the voluntariness of the confessions. Defendant had no right to the effective assistance of a mental health expert.


 

69 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Asgari v. City of Los Angeles


No. S051825.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


15 Cal. 4th 744; 937 P.2d 273; 63 Cal. Rptr. 2d 842; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 2550; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4134; 97 Daily Journal DAR 6909



June 2, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified July 16, 1997. Rehearing Denied July 16, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4429.


PRIOR HISTORY: Court Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Third Division. G013642. Superior Court of Orange County, Super. Ct. No. 597441. James Randal Ross, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed to the extent it affirms the award of damages for false arrest and intentional infliction of emotional distress, as well as related punitive damages,


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, city and police officer, sought review of the decision of the Court Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Third Division, that entered judgment in favor of plaintiff, citizen, in his action seeking damages for false arrest and related causes of action after he was acquitted of possession for sale of heroin following an undercover narcotics investigation.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff citizen filed an action against defendants, city and police officer, alleging false imprisonment. After a verdict in plaintiff's favor, defendants appealed, alleging that the trial court erred in instructing the jury that a police officer's liability for false arrest could include damages sustained by the arrestee after the filing of formal charges. The appellate court reversed, finding that this jury instruction was erroneous. The court reasoned that the instruction was based upon a federal cause of action for false imprisonment, which, in turn, was based on common law. The cause of action for false imprisonment in California was statutory, so the instruction was not applicable. The court also found that Cal. Gov't Code § 821.6 precluded the award of damages for false imprisonment after a lawful arraignment because it provided immunity to police officers for malicious prosecution. As a result, the court reasoned that permitting an arrestee to recover damages arising from incarceration following an arraignment on formal charges would effectively nullify the statutory immunity for malicious prosecution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment to the extent it affirmed the award of damages for false imprisonment, finding that the jury instruction was improper because the statute provided that public employees were immune from liability for injury caused by malicious prosecution, and such immunity precluded recovery of damages in a false arrest action that were attributable to the period of incarceration that followed arraignment.

 

70 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Helms


No. S053937.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


15 Cal. 4th 608; 936 P.2d 1230; 63 Cal. Rptr. 2d 620; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 2310; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3848; 97 Daily Journal DAR 6513



May 22, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four. A071204. Superior Court of Marin County, Super. Ct. No. SC052696A. Lynn Duryee * 


* Judge of the Municipal Court for the Marin Judicial District, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: In summary, the constitutional prohibition against retroactively increasing punishment for crimes is not implicated. We reverse the Court of Appeal's judgment insofar as it modified the judgment of conviction. In all other respects, we affirm the Court of Appeal's judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions for possession of a controlled substance and the decision of the Court of Appeal (California), which ordered that his sentences for those offenses were to run concurrently under the application of the newly enacted three strikes law, Cal. Penal Code § 1170.12.


OVERVIEW: Defendant received a four-year sentence for possession a controlled substance, and he was placed on probation. After that, the voters enacted Cal. Penal Code § 1170.12, the three strikes law. Later, defendant was again arrested for possession of a controlled substance, he was convicted, and he was sentenced to an indeterminate term under the three strikes law. The trial court revoked his probation, imposed the four-year sentence previously imposed, and committed defendant to prison. In so doing, it ruled that the three strikes law required defendant to serve his sentences consecutively. Defendant appealed. The court of appeals modified the judgment to have the sentences run concurrently, concluding that to sentence defendant to consecutive terms violated the state and federal ex post facto provisions. The supreme court reversed because the application of the three strikes law did not increase the punishment for defendant. The supreme court found the three strikes law did not eliminate any potential concurrency of defendant's second term, rather the new law's only effect was to determine that a three strikes offense must be consecutive to all other terms.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals that determined tat defendant's conviction for drug possession were to run concurrently was reversed, and defendant's conviction were affirmed because the application of the three strikes law did not increase the punishment for defendant. The court held that the trial court properly determined that the punishment for the three strikes offense was to be consecutive to all other terms of imprisonment imposed.

People v. Holt


No. S016076.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA

Rehearing denied by, 07/09/1997

Habeas corpus proceeding at, Request granted Holt v. Wong, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 48584 (E.D. Cal., July 7, 2006)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Holt (John Lee) on H.C., 2010 Cal. LEXIS 8615 (Cal., Sept. 1, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, Super. Ct. No. 39910. Robert T. Baca, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction and death sentence entered in the Superior Court of Kern County (California) after he was found guilty of murder, robbery, rape, sodomy, and burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 189, 212.5(a), 261(2), 286, 460(a), respectively. Defendant contended that there were errors in jury selection and that there was insufficient evidence of intent and that evidence did not conform to prosecution's theory of case.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted and sentenced to death for an incident in which he strangled, sexually assaulted, and robbed the victim in her home. A jury found defendant guilty of murder, robbery, rape, sodomy, and burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 189, 212.5(a), 261(2), 286, 460(a). Defendant contended that the jury was biased and that unbiased jurors were excluded from the panel. Moreover, he complained that the verdict did not indicate whether defendant was convicted of premeditated murder or felony murder. The appellate court affirmed the judgment and held that there were no prejudicial errors in jury selection or instruction. Moreover, the court held that defendant's felonious intent was evident from the circumstances of the crime, and it was not necessary for the jury to specify the theory under which defendant was convicted.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions for first degree murder, robbery, rape, sodomy, and burglary. The court also affirmed defendant's death sentence because there was no necessity for the jury to specify whether defendant was convicted of felony murder or premeditated murder, and there was sufficient evidence to support the murder conviction. Moreover, the jury was properly selected and instructed in both the conviction and penalty phase of the trial.

Professional Engineers v. Department of Transportation


No. S042591.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


15 Cal. 4th 543; 936 P.2d 473; 63 Cal. Rptr. 2d 467; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 2308; 97 Daily Journal DAR 6201; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3644



May 15, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied July 16, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4427.


PRIOR HISTORY: Sacramento County. Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District. C018317. Superior Court of Sacramento County, Super. Ct. No. CV336697, Eugene T. Gualco, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff engineers requested review of a decision by the Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District, Sacramento County, that dissolved an injunction prohibiting defendant transportation department from using private contractors to perform work traditionally done by plaintiffs. Plaintiff sought to enforce the injunction but defendant claimed that newly enacted 1993 Cal. Stat. 443 permitted defendant to hire outside contractors.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff engineers requested review of a decision by the court of appeals that dissolved an injunction. The injunction prevented defendant transportation department from contracting for engineering services that state civil servants had traditionally performed. After the injunction had been entered, 1993 Cal. Stat. 443 was enacted. Defendant claimed that 1993 Cal. Stat. 443 required that the injunction be dissolved and that it permitted defendant to hire private contractors. The court concluded that the activities authorized by 1993 Cal. Stat. 443 did not constitute a new state function but simply a new technique for the performance of an existing function. The court held that 1993 Cal. Stat. 443 was invalid to the extent that it conflicted with the civil service mandate in the California Constitution. The supreme court reversed the court of appeals judgment and held that the trial court's injunction was valid and should have remained in effect.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the court of appeals judgment that dissolved the trial court's injunction. The court found that the injunction should remain in effect to prevent defendant transportation department from hiring outside contractors. The court concluded that the statute at issue, which permitted defendant to hire contractors, was invalid to the extent that it conflicted with the California Constitution's civil service mandate.

 

73 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Christian v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


No. S055638.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


15 Cal. 4th 505; 936 P.2d 115; 63 Cal. Rptr. 2d 336; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 2307; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3538; 97 Daily Journal DAR 6026; 62 Cal. Comp. Cases 576



May 12, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected May 28, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: California Court of Appeal, Second District, Division One, Ct. App. B095182, annulled the WCAB's decision and directed reinstatement of the findings and award of the WCJ.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent employer and insurance carrier challenged a judgment of the California Court of Appeal, Second District, Division One, which annulled the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board's decision and reinstated the workers' compensation judge's award. The court of appeals held that multiple penalties were authorized by Cal. Lab. Code § 5814 against respondents for discontinuing petitioner employee's temporary total disability benefits.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employee became temporarily totally disabled (TTD) while working for respondent employer. After paying TTD benefits, respondent insurance carrier discontinued payments. The workers' compensation judge held that respondents' discontinuance of the payments was unreasonable and that petitioner was entitled under Cal. Lab. Code § 5814 to a penalty of 10 percent for each payment due that had not been made. The Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (board) amended the decision to impose a single § 5814 penalty against all TTD indemnity payments payable by respondents. Thereafter, the court of appeals annulled the board's decision and reinstated the multiple penalties. The supreme court reversed and held that § 5814 permitted multiple penalties for nonpayment of TTD benefits only when the unreasonable refusal was attributable to separate and distinct acts. The supreme court found that respondents terminated petitioner's TTD benefits by a single act when it concluded that petitioner was no longer entitled to them. As such, the supreme court held that the termination was not a separate and distinct act supporting an additional penalty each time a benefit check was due.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment that multiple penalties against respondent employer and insurance carrier were authorized by Cal. Lab. Code § 5814 because respondents' refusal to pay petitioner employee's temporary total disability benefits under the Workers' Compensation Act was not attributable to separate and distinct acts.

 

74 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Carpenter


No. S004654.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


15 Cal. 4th 312; 935 P.2d 708; 63 Cal. Rptr. 2d 1; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 1948; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3058; 97 Daily Journal DAR 5375



April 28, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied June 18, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 3555.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Super. Ct. No. A379894. Dion G. Morrow, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found appellant defendant guilty of first degree murder and a different jury returned a verdict of death. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) denied defendant's automatic motion to modify the verdict and sentenced defendant to death. Appeal was automatic.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of seven murders, an attempted murder, attempted rape, and rape murder. At trial, defendant admitted that he had killed a victim, but sought to cast doubt on whether he lay in wait for the victim and asserted that the semen found in another victim actually came from her boyfriend, not defendant. Defendant offered mitigating circumstances at the punishment phase; the jury rejected his contentions and returned a death penalty verdict. The court affirmed defendant's conviction and death penalty sentence, holding that any errors that may have occurred at trial either had not been preserved for appeal or were harmless. The court noted that the trial court did not abuse it discretion in making discretionary rulings and found the trial to be remarkably error free. The court reasoned that although the trial court should have given a cautionary instruction sua sponte when another victim testified that defendant had said, "I want to rape you;" failure to give the cautionary instruction was harmless because the victim's testimony was uncontradicted, and there was no evidence that it was fabricated or was inaccurately remembered or reported.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and death penalty sentence, holding that no reversible error occurred during defendant's trial.

People v. Jones


No. S009141.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


15 Cal. 4th 119; 931 P.2d 960; 61 Cal. Rptr. 2d 386; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 632; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1760; 97 Daily Journal DAR 3301



March 10, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified April 30, 1997. Rehearing Denied April 30, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 2320. Certiorari Denied November 3, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 6583.
Rehearing denied by, 04/30/1997
Modified by People v. Jones, 15 Cal. 4th 431a, 1997 Cal. LEXIS 2320, 97 D.A.R. 5615 (1997)
Writ of habeas corpus denied In re Jones, 1997 Cal. LEXIS 3327 (Cal., June 11, 1997)
Writ of certiorari denied Jones v. California, 522 U.S. 955, 118 S. Ct. 381, 139 L. Ed. 2d 297, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 6583 (1997)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Jones (Jeffrey Gerard) on H.C., 2003 Cal. LEXIS 7232 (Cal., Sept. 24, 2003)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Jones v. Ayers, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 26772 (E.D. Cal., Mar. 31, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, Super. Ct. No. 77173. Allen P. Fields, Judge.
People v. Jones, 1990 Cal. LEXIS 5360 (Cal., Nov. 28, 1990)



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction in the Superior Court, Sacramento County (California) for capital murder. Defendant argued that he was incompetent at hearings to determine his competency and at trial. Defendant also argued that the prosecution committed a Batson error in using preemptory strikes to exclude black members of the jury panel, and that error was committed in the striking of those opposed to the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of the capital murder of two people and sentenced to death. Defendant appealed, arguing that he was incompetent to stand trial, and that such incompetency was established at two separate competency hearings. Defendant further argued that the prosecutor committed a Batson violation by using preemptory strikes to exclude black members of the jury panel, and that it was error for the prosecution to use strikes to exclude members of the panel who were hesitant about the death penalty. The court affirmed, noting that two competency hearings had been held to determine defendant's competency, the first resulting in defendant's institutionalization, and the second of which ended in a jury finding defendant competent to stand trial. The court further held that defendant was malingering by exaggerating the symptoms of mental illness. As to defendant's Batson argument, the court stated that the prosecution gave a sufficient race-neutral reason, reservations about the death penalty, for striking black members of the jury panel. The court further held that a challenge for cause was proper as to members of the jury panel who would be unable to impose the death penalty.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for capital murder was affirmed. The court found that two competency hearings were held in order to determine defendant's competency to stand trial, and in the second hearing a jury found that defendant was competent to stand trial. The court also held that the prosecution did not commit a Batson violation, because the prosecution gave genuine nondiscriminatory reason for the preemptory challenges of black panel members.

People ex rel. Gallo v. Acuna


No. S046980.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


14 Cal. 4th 1090; 929 P.2d 596; 60 Cal. Rptr. 2d 277; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 33; 97 Daily Journal DAR 1023; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 724



January 30, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied June 27, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4093.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court Santa Clara County, No. 729322, Robert Michael Foley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed insofar as it invalidated paragraphs (a) and (k) of the preliminary injunction and concluded that defendant Blanca Gonzalez was not subject to its terms. Because our grant of review encompassed only those two of the fifteen provisions invalidated by the Court of Appeal, we do not address any other aspect of the preliminary injunction entered by the superior court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeal (California), which invalidated two provisions of a preliminary injunction entered by the trial court against defendant, criminal street gang, under the provisions of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 737, Cal. Civ. Code § 3480.


OVERVIEW: Preliminary injunction was entered against defendant, criminal street gang, under the provisions of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 737, Cal. Civ. Code § 3480. The appellate court upheld only provisions enjoining acts or conduct defined as crimes under the penal code. The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court, which invalidated two provisions of a preliminary injunction. The court held that defendants' association was not entitled to protection under U.S. Const. amend. I, because the gang was not formed for the purpose of engaging in protected speech or religious activities. The court held that the trial court's preliminary decree was not overbroad, because it was addressed to identifiable parties and to specific circumstances, and the enjoined acts were particularly described. The court held that the California Street Terrorism Enforcement and Prevention Act (STEP), Cal. Penal Code, §186.20 et seq., was not the exclusive means of enjoining defendants, because other legal remedies were not precluded by STEP. The conduct enjoined met the statutory definition of a public nuisance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court, which invalidated two provisions of a preliminary injunction entered against defendant, criminal street gang. The court held that the two challenged provisions fell within the trial court's equitable power to abate a public nuisance, and did not violate defendant's constitutional rights.

People v. Bradford


No. S016569.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


14 Cal. 4th 1005; 929 P.2d 544; 60 Cal. Rptr. 2d 225; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 9; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 520; 97 Daily Journal DAR 899



January 23, 1997, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court Los Angeles County, No. A820582, Michael Farrrell, Leon Kaplan, James Coleman, C. Bernard Kaufman and Alan B. Haber, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The conviction and sentence for robbery are reversed. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187(a), first degree robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211, rape under Cal. Penal Code § 261, and sodomy under Cal. Penal Code § 286(c), and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187(a), first degree robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211, rape under Cal. Penal Code § 261, and sodomy under Cal. Penal Code § 286(c). On appeal, the court reversed the robbery conviction, and held that it was error for the trial court to fail sua sponte to instruct the jury on the lesser-included offense of theft, because there was evidence which would justify conviction of the lesser offense. The court affirmed the rest of the trial court's judgment, including the death penalty. The court held that while the police officers wrongly questioned defendant after he invoked his right to counsel, the admission of his statement was not reversible error, because the evidence established that his statement was voluntarily made. The court held that defendant waived his right to complain of voir dire conducted in a closed courtroom by not objecting to it at the time. The court held that defendant failed to show ineffective assistance of counsel, and affirmed that the California death penalty statute was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for robbery because the trial court committed reversible error by failing sua sponte to instruct the jury on the lesser-included offense of theft but affirmed the remainder of the convictions and punishment because there was no reversible error in the admission of defendant's statements, voir dire, or closing argument, and defendant failed to prove ineffective assistance of counsel.

Kingston Constructors, Inc. v. Washington Metropolitan Area Transit Authority


No. S053577.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


14 Cal. 4th 939; 928 P.2d 581; 59 Cal. Rptr. 2d 866; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 5; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 248; 97 Daily Journal DAR 375



January 9, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied June 16, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 3740.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BC056254, Harvey A. Schneider, Judge.  



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant company sought review from the judgment of the Division Two of the Second Appellate District of the Court of Appeal (California) which affirmed the judgment of the trial court which held that under the Washington Metropolitan Area Transit Authority Compact, the trial court lacked jurisdiction of the action against respondent transit authority.


OVERVIEW: As part of a settlement agreement, appellant was assigned a California subcontractor's claims in a lawsuit against respondent Washington transit authority. The trial court rendered judgment in favor of appellant. On respondent's motion, the trial court issued an order vacating its judgment as void, holding it had no jurisdiction to render the decision. Appellant filed a notice of appeal and the appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment. On further review, the supreme court affirmed the judgment of the lower courts holding that the Washington Metropolitan Area Transit Authority Compact (WMATA), which established respondent was consented to by Congress pursuant to U.S. Const. art. I § 10 cl. 3 and became federal law. The compact expressly granted jurisdiction to the courts of Maryland and Virginia, because of respondent's zone encompassing geographic boundaries in those two states, and concurrently to the courts of the United States. The supreme court held that WMATA impliedly denied jurisdiction to the remaining states, because the transit zone was not within their geographic jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower courts holding that the trial court lacked jurisdiction to hear a cause of action based solely on respondent's compact which directed that judicial jurisdiction was expressly granted to the two states where geographic boundaries for the zone were located, and original jurisdiction in the United States District Courts.

 

79 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Loder v. City of Glendale


No. S043548.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


14 Cal. 4th 846; 927 P.2d 1200; 59 Cal. Rptr. 2d 696; 1997 Cal. LEXIS 3; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 136; 12 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 545; 97 Daily Journal DAR 222



January 6, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied October 6, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4577.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court Los Angeles County, No. C616659, Ernest George Williams, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, and the matter is remanded to the Court Appeal for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city challenged the judgment of a California court of appeals, which affirmed the trial court's judgment that defendant's drug testing program for all positions was impermissible in both preemployment and prepromotional contexts in plaintiff taxpayer's action to enjoin the use of public funds for the program. The court of appeals reversed a portion of the judgment upholding the program's validity as to many job classifications.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city created a drug and alcohol testing program for job applicants and promoted employees. Plaintiff taxpayer sought to stop the use of public funds for the program, alleging that the program violated the Confidentiality of Medical Information Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 56 et seq., U.S. Const. amend. IV, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 1. Defendant challenged the constitutional standard used and the application of that standard by the court of appeals. The court held that the testing program did not violate § 56 et seq. Urinalysis testing was a search under U.S. Const. amend. IV. The court held insofar as defendant's program imposed a drug testing requirement on every current employee who applied for and was offered a promotion, without regard to the nature of the position sought, was overbroad and unconstitutional. The court held that because the program was administered in a reasonable fashion as part of a lawful preemployment medical examination required of every job applicant, it was constitutionally permissible as to job applicants. Defendant had a significantly greater interest in testing job applicants than current employees seeking a promotion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals' judgment, which had held that defendant's city's drug and alcohol testing program in both the preemployment and prepromotional contexts was unconstitutional. The program was unconstitutional in the prepromotional context, but defendant was free to establish a new prepromotional program in light of subsequent statutory and constitutional authority. The program was constitutional for job applicants.

 

80 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Vons Companies, Inc. v. Seabest Foods, Inc.


No. S049039.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


14 Cal. 4th 434; 926 P.2d 1085; 58 Cal. Rptr. 2d 899; 1996 Cal. LEXIS 6522; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8972; 96 Daily Journal DAR 14881



December 12, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied October 6, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4595.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. JCCP3101, Robert Carroll Baxley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed and the matter is remanded for further proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant meat supplier challenged the judgment of the California Court of Appeal, which affirmed the trial court order holding that it lacked personal jurisdiction over respondent franchisees. After a lawsuit was filed against appellant alleging that it provided contaminated meat to franchises in Washington state, which caused injuries there, appellant filed a cross-complaint against respondent alleging it was negligent in preparing the food.


OVERVIEW: Appellant meat supplier was named as a defendant in a lawsuit filed in California which claimed that it had supplied contaminated food to fast food franchises, and as a result, customers of these franchises in Washington state were injured. Appellant filed a cross-complaint against respondent franchisees, alleging that their failure to properly cook the meat caused the injuries plaintiff complained of. Respondent, which was based in Washington state, filed a motion to quash the service of process upon it, claiming that the California court lacked personal jurisdiction over it. This motion was granted by the trial court, and affirmed by the appellate court. In reversing the judgment, the court found that respondent had sufficient minimum contacts with California to satisfy the first prong of the three-prong jurisdiction test. Though the contacts were not with appellant, the court found a substantial connection between them and the instant litigation, which satisfied the second prong of the jurisdiction test. Respondent did not provide a compelling case that the exercise of jurisdiction against them would be unreasonable, and thus the third prong of the test was satisfied.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower courts, holding that respondent franchisees had the requisite minimum contacts with California necessary for jurisdiction over it, that the claims in the litigation had a substantial connection with respondent's contacts with California, and that respondent had failed to present a compelling case that other considerations rendered jurisdiction in California unreasonable.

 

81 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Hazelton


No. S051561.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


14 Cal. 4th 101; 926 P.2d 423; 58 Cal. Rptr. 2d 443; 1996 Cal. LEXIS 6516; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8780; 96 Daily Journal DAR 14545



December 5, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, Nos. 0902395 and 0959791, Richard S. Flier, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeal (California), which reversed the trial court's decision that defendant's prior out-of-state felony conviction could not be counted for purposes of the "three strikes law," Cal. Penal Code § 1170.12.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with felonies in complaints that alleged he had two prior felony convictions under the legislative version of the "three strikes law," Cal. Penal Code § 667(d) & (e), one of which was an out-of-state conviction. Before adjudication, the initiative version of the "three strikes" law, Cal. Penal Code § 1170.12, became operative. The trial court ruled that under § 1170.12, defendant's out-of-state conviction could not be used for three-strike purposes. The intermediate appellate court reversed the trial court. On appeal, the court affirmed. Because § 1170.12 was ambiguous as to out-of-state convictions, the court turned to the legislative history to determine the voters' intent. The court found no evidence in the legislative history that the voters intended to exclude out-of-state convictions from the purview of the initiative's third-strike penalty or to adopt a "three-strikes" law that was more lenient than the legislative version which included out-of-state convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment which reversed the trial court's finding that defendant's out-of-state felony conviction could not be counted for purposes of the initiative version of the "three strikes law," because there was no evidence that voters intended to exclude out-of-state convictions.

 

82 of 1449 DOCUMENTS




People v. Humphrey


No. S045985.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


13 Cal. 4th 1073; 921 P.2d 1; 56 Cal. Rptr. 2d 142; 1996 Cal. LEXIS 4222; 96 Daily Journal DAR 10609; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6509



August 29, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court Fresno County. Ct. App. 5 F020267. Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District. Super. Ct. No. 460768-5. Hon. A. Dennis Caeton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District, which affirmed a trial court judgment convicting defendant of voluntary manslaughter with personal use of a firearm.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed the man with whom she lived, allegedly out of fear that he was going to kill her. Defendant introduced expert and nonexpert testimony tending to prove that her state of mind was affected by battered women's syndrome. The trial court instructed the jury on second-degree murder, voluntary and involuntary manslaughter, and self-defense, stating that an actual and reasonable belief the killing was necessary was a complete defense, that an actual but unreasonable belief was a defense to murder but not voluntary manslaughter, and that battered women's syndrome was not relevant to the reasonableness of defendant's state of mind. The court of appeal affirmed defendant's voluntary manslaughter conviction and the supreme court reversed. Evidence of battered women's syndrome was relevant to the reasonableness, as well as the subjective existence, of defendant's belief in the need to defend. The evidence was also relevant to defendant's credibility. To the extent it was relevant, the jury should have been allowed to consider it. The contrary instruction was prejudicial error because it was reasonably probable that it affected the verdict adversely to defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeal.


 

83 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Masbruch


No. S047206.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


13 Cal. 4th 1001; 920 P.2d 705; 55 Cal. Rptr. 2d 760; 1996 Cal. LEXIS 4218; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6380; 96 Daily Journal DAR 10419



August 26, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Fresno County Superior Court. Ct. App. 5 F021472. Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District. Super. Ct. No. 489358-2. Hon. John Fitch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We conclude the jury could have reasonably found that defendant utilized his initial display of the gun "at least as an aid in completing an essential element of" the subsequent crimes of rape and sodomy. ( People v. Chambers, supra, 7 Cal. 3d at pp. 672-673; § 261, subd. (a)(2), 286, subd. (c).) Accordingly, the evidence was sufficient to support imposition of section 12022.3(a) enhancements for those crimes. ( People v. Johnson (1980) 26 Cal. 3d 557, 576 [162 Cal. Rptr. 431, 606 P.2d 738, 16 A.L.R.4th 1255].) The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District, which affirmed a trial court judgment convicting defendant of using a firearm in the commission of rape and sodomy.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered the home of his victim, pointed a gun at her, tied her up, tied up her mother, robbed the home, and raped and sodomized the victim. Defendant did not display or mention the gun at any time after his initial display. The jury found, inter alia, that defendant used a firearm in the commission of rape and sodomy, and his sentence included two enhancements under Cal. Penal Code § 12022.3(a). The court affirmed. Although a substantial period of time elapsed between the one and only time defendant displayed the gun and the rape and sodomy of the victim, and although the victim did not testify that she feared the gun at the time the rape and sodomy occurred, the evidence supported the enhancement finding. The jury could conclude that the fear defendant's initial gun display instilled continued throughout the encounter and aided defendant in completing the crimes of rape and sodomy. Testimony that the victim feared the gun during the commission of each and every crime defendant committed after the initial weapon display was unnecessary. There was evidence from which the jury could infer that she continued to fear the gun at the time of the rape and sodomy.


OUTCOME: The judgment upholding defendant's conviction for using a firearm in the commission of rape and sodomy was affirmed. The jury could have reasonably found that defendant utilized his display of a gun to one of his victims at least as an aid in completing an essential element of the crimes of rape and sodomy that he committed approximately one hour after that display.

 

84 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Sade C.


No. S048796.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


13 Cal. 4th 952; 920 P.2d 716; 55 Cal. Rptr. 2d 771; 1996 Cal. LEXIS 4217; 96 Daily Journal DAR 10401; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6346



August 26, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Los Angeles County Superior Court. Ct. App. Nos. 2/3 B082230, B084043. Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three. Super. Ct. Nos. LK00851, CK09428. Hon. Larry P. Fidler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant indigent parent sought review from the Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three, which determined that appellant was not entitled to counsel for an appeal from a judgment, obtained by respondent department of children's services, adversely affecting the custody of his child. Appellant asserted various procedural and constitutional arguments.


OVERVIEW: Appellant indigent parent, a criminal and drug user, had a child who was thereafter declared dependent. Appellant's parental rights were terminated. Appellant was appointed counsel to represent him on appeal. After that appeal was unsuccessful, appellant again sought review and the question of whether the case of Anders, revolving around a right to counsel on appeal, applied to an indigent parent's appeal from a judgment, obtained by respondent department of children's services, adversely affecting custody of a child. On review, the court noted that generally, Anders was confined to apply to criminal appeals. The court explored the history behind the right to appointed counsel and noted that those convicted in criminal cases were afforded more protections than parents involved in parental right/juvenile court proceedings. The court held that an indigent parent adversely affected by a state-obtained decision on child custody or parental status was simply not a criminal defendant and was not afforded the same protections and considerations as under Anders. The court held that there was no reversible error or other defect to require a reversal. The judgment was affirmed accordingly.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision against appellant indigent parent because appellant was not entitled, under Anders or otherwise, to counsel for an appeal from the judgment in favor of respondent department of children's services, which terminated his custody rights. The court held that, as no claim of error or other defect was raised, the appeal was properly dismissed.

People v. Osband


No. S005233.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment by which the Superior Court Sacramento County (California) convicted him of murder committed during a burglary and robbery and after raping his victim, burglary, robbery, forcible rape, attempted murder, assault with intent to rape, and infliction of great bodily injury pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 190.2(a)(17)(vii), 190.2(a)(17)(i), 190.2(a)(17)(iii), 459, 211, 261(a)(2), 220, 187(a), 664, and 12022.7.

People v. Superior Court (Romero)


No. S045097.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


13 Cal. 4th 497; 917 P.2d 628; 53 Cal. Rptr. 2d 789; 1996 Cal. LEXIS 3025; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4494; 96 Daily Journal DAR 7229



June 20, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified on Denial of Rehearing August 21, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 4445.
Modified and rehearing denied by People v. Superior Court (Romero), 13 Cal. 4th 1016a, 1996 Cal. LEXIS 4445, 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6266, 96 D.A.R. 10209 (1996)


PRIOR HISTORY: San Diego County Superior Court. Super. Ct. No. SCD103345. Hon. William D. Mudd, Judge. Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One. Ct. App. D022175.
People v. Superior Court (Romero), 44 Cal. App. 4th 1073, 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 364, 1995 Cal. App. LEXIS 30 (Cal. App. 4th Dist., 1995)



DISPOSITION:  The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the decision overruling the trial court's striking of defendant's prior felony convictions, because the trial court was permitted to exercise its power to dismiss in cases charged under the Three Strikes Law. The court remanded with an order to vacate and permit defendant to withdraw his plea because the trial court's order was ineffective for failure to state the reasons for dismissal.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a writ from the Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One, which overruled the trial court's order striking defendant's prior felony convictions for purpose of the Three Strikes Law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(e)(2), after defendant was charged with possession of a controlled substance in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was eligible for a life sentence under the Three Strikes Law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(e)(2), following a felony charge in conjunction with two prior serious felonies. The trial court offered to strike the priors if defendant pled guilty and defendant agreed. The trial court overruled the prosecutor's objection that it had no power to dismiss the priors in a three strikes case unless requested by the prosecutor. The appellate court granted the district attorney's petition for a writ to vacate the order striking the priors. On defendant's petition for review, the court held that in cases charged under the Three Strikes Law, a court may exercise the power to dismiss granted in Cal. Penal Code § 1385, either on its own motion or on that of the prosecuting attorney, subject to strict compliance with the provisions of § 1385 and review for abuse of discretion. As to defendant, the court held that the trial court's order was ineffective because it did not explain the reasons for the dismissal. Thus, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part, and remanded with an order to vacate the judgment and permit defendant to withdraw his plea.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the decision overruling the trial court's striking of defendant's prior felony convictions, because the trial court was permitted to exercise its power to dismiss in cases charged under the Three Strikes Law. The court remanded with an order to vacate and permit defendant to withdraw his plea because the trial court's order was ineffective for failure to state the reasons for dismissal.

People v. Panizzon

SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


13 Cal. 4th 68; 913 P.2d 1061; 51 Cal. Rptr. 2d 851; 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1575; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2733; 96 Daily Journal DAR 4526

PRIOR HISTORY: Santa Barbara County Superior Court. Ct. App. No. B081510. Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six. Super. Ct. No. 194592. Hon. Ronald C. Stevens, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In light of our conclusion that the Court of Appeal should not have disregarded defendant's failure to comply with section 1237.5 and rule 31(d) or, alternatively, his waiver of appellate rights, we do not reach the merits of defendant's challenge to the sentence.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his life imprisonment sentence and conviction in the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California) on numerous felony charges based upon a no contest plea agreement. Defendant claimed cruel and unusual punishment in sentencing. The appellate court denied the People's request for dismissal but affirmed the judgment rejecting defendant's appeal on the merits. Penal Code § 1237.5 and Cal. R. Ct. 31(d). The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and remanded the cause to that court with directions to enter a dismissal of the appeal.


OUTCOME: The court found appellate error in denying the people's request for dismissal because defendant challenged the validity of his plea agreement and failed to procure a certificate of probable cause. The court alternatively held the claim was barred because the terms of the plea agreement prohibited such a challenge. The court reversed the judgment and remanded for a dismissal of the appeal.

Superior Court v. County of Mendocino


No. S046963.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


13 Cal. 4th 45; 913 P.2d 1046; 51 Cal. Rptr. 2d 837; 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1574; 96 Daily Journal DAR 4517; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2739



April 18, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four. Ct. App. No. A065039. Mendocino County Superior Court. Super. Ct. No. 68620. Hon. John K. Letton, Judge * 


*  Judge of the Trinity Superior Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, we conclude that the Court of Appeal erred in holding that section 68108 is unconstitutional on its face. The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent county sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four, which held that Cal. Gov't Code § 68108 violated the separation of powers doctrine embodied in Cal. Const. art. III, § 3. The action was brought by appellant superior court after respondent, pursuant to § 68108, provided furlough days for county employees and closed all superior courts on those days.


OVERVIEW: Under Cal. Gov't Code § 68108, respondent county provided county employees with furlough days. On these days, all superior courts of the county were closed. Appellant superior court challenged § 68108's constitutionality, and the appeals court found § 68108 in violation of the separation of powers doctrine under Cal. Const. art. III, § 3. Respondent sought review, and the court reversed. The court found that in enacting § 68108, the legislature had not violated the separation of powers doctrine although it had legislated with regards to inherent judicial powers. The court found separation of powers had not been violated because § 68108 had not defeated or materially impaired the judiciary's fulfillment of constitutional duties. The court also found nothing in the current provisions of the California Constitution that indicated the legislature lacked authority to enact statutory provisions designating holidays or other nonjudicial days on which trial courts would not be in session. Similarly, the court found the power to designate the days upon which a court would or would not be open was not a function reserved by the California constitution exclusively to the judicial branch.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment which held a statute was unconstitutional because the statute, which had provided furlough days for county employees and had resulted in the closing of all superior courts on those days, had not violated the separation of powers. The statute, enacted pursuant to legislative authority, had not impaired the judiciary's ability to perform constitutional functions and had not affected the public's access to justice.

People v. Arias

Smith v. Fair Employment & Hous. Com


No. S040653.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


12 Cal. 4th 1143; 913 P.2d 909; 51 Cal. Rptr. 2d 700; 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1389; 96 Daily Journal DAR 4067; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2465



April 9, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied June 27, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4211.


PRIOR HISTORY: Ct. App. No. C007654. Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District Butte County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed to the extent it vacates the award of damages for emotional distress. (See ante, fn. 5.) In all other respects, the judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Fair Employment and Housing Commission appealed a judgment from the Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District, which reversed petitioner's decision and held that the state could not prevent respondent landlord from discriminating against unmarried couples and that her inquiry into her tenants' marital status did not violate their right to privacy under Cal. Const. art. I, § 1.


OVERVIEW: Respondent landlord owned and leased rental units. Complainants agreed to rent a unit but when respondent found out they were not married she told them she could not rent to them because it would violate her religious beliefs. Complainants filed allegations against respondent with petitioner Fair Employment and Housing Commission. The administrative law judge decided in favor of complainants. Respondent sought review and the appellate court reversed. Petitioner sought review and the court held that the Fair Employment and Housing Act (FEHA), Cal. Gov't Code 12900 et seq., did protect unmarried cohabitants against housing discrimination and that respondent had violated FEHA. The court held that respondent's religion did not require her to rent apartments. Thus, she could avoid the burden on her religious exercise without violating her beliefs or threatening her livelihood. The court held that the appellate court erred in concluding Cal. Gov't Code § 12955 substantially burdened respondent's religious exercise. The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment to the extent it vacated the award of damages for emotional distress. In all other respects, the judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court to the extent it vacated the award of damages for emotional distress. In all other respects, the judgment of the appellate court was reversed. The court held that the Fair Employment and Housing Act did protect unmarried cohabitants against housing discrimination.

 

95 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Am. Acad. of Pediatrics v. Lungren


No. S041459


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


12 Cal. 4th 1007; 912 P.2d 1148; 51 Cal. Rptr. 2d 201; 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1387; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2338; 96 Daily Journal DAR 3865



April 4, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Granted May 22, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 2751. (See 16 Cal. 4th 307).


PRIOR HISTORY: San Francisco County Superior Court. Super. Ct. No. 884574. Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One. Ct. App. No. A058627. Hon. Maxine M. Chesney, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, which affirmed the judgment of the trial court that declared A.B. 2274, Reg. Sess.( Cal. 1988), invalid as violating the rights to privacy and equal protection of unemancipated minors who sought to terminate their pregnancies in favor of respondents, pediatric association and affiliated individuals.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, pediatric association and affiliated individuals, filed a complaint against the people and contended that A.B. 2274, Reg. Sess. (Cal. 1988), which prohibited unemancipated minors from obtaining abortions without the consent of a parent or judicial authorization was unconstitutional. The trial court declared Bill 2274, invalid and appellate state sought review. The appellate court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and held that Bill 2274, was constitutional under Cal. Const. art. I, § 7. The court held that Bill 2274 accommodated a balance of interests: the parent's right to control their child's development was advanced by the consent requirement, while the state's interest in limiting parental control when it might harm the child was advanced by providing a judicial bypass that included a prompt hearing before a juvenile court judge, confidentiality, and a right to counsel. The court held that the people had carried their burden of showing that the legislation substantively furthered a "compelling interest" and that there were no less intrusive means of accomplishing its legitimate objectives.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court with directions to reverse the judgment of the trial court and entered judgment in favor of the people. The court held that prohibiting unemancipated minors from obtaining abortions without either the consent of a parent or judicial authorization was valid.

Ehrlich v. City of Culver City


No. S033642.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


12 Cal. 4th 854; 911 P.2d 429; 50 Cal. Rptr. 2d 242; 1996 Cal. LEXIS 801; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1542; 96 Daily Journal DAR 2558



March 5, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied April 11, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. LEXIS 2098.


PRIOR HISTORY: Los Angeles County Superior Court. Ct. App. No. B055523. Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five. Super. Ct. No. C730079. Hon. John Zebrowski, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed; the cause is remanded to that court with directions to order the case returned to the City of Culver City.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner and developer challenged a judgment of the Second District Court of Appeal of California that found in favor of defendant city in a dispute over a development permit that exacted a fee as a condition of issuance.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff property owner and developer requested from defendant city permission to construct a condominium complex on land he already owned and on which he had operated recreational facilities. The city approved plaintiff's application conditioned upon payment of a $ 280,000 fee to be used for additional public recreational facilities. Plaintiff appealed an adverse judgment of the court of appeals. The court reversed, first noting that the issue was properly analyzed within the statutory framework of the Mitigation Fee Act, Cal. Gov't. Code § 66000 et seq. The court then held the city met its burden of showing an essential nexus between the permit condition and the public impact of the proposed development by demonstrating the connection between the rezoning necessary to construct the condominium complex and the imposition of the fee that would be expended in support of recreational purposes, as a means of mitigating that loss. However, the city failed to show the required rough proportionality between the magnitude of the fiscal exaction and the effects of the proposed development, and the court therefore remanded for further findings.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeal was reversed and the cause remanded to that court because the record was insufficient to sustain the city's assertion that plaintiff should pay a mitigation fee of $ 280,000 as a condition for approval of his request that the property at issue be rezoned to permit the construction of a condominium project.

 

98 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Gonzalez


No. S045546.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


12 Cal. 4th 804; 910 P.2d 1366; 50 Cal. Rptr. 2d 74; 1996 Cal. LEXIS 792; 96 Daily Journal DAR 2267; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1359



February 29, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Los Angeles County Municipal Court. Super. Ct. No. BR-33744. Ct. App. No. B086955. Hon. Lloyd M. Nash, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, and the matter remanded to the Court of Appeal for further proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both defendant accused and the state challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals (California), which determined that the trial court lacked authority to adjudicate an attack on an injunction issued by a court of superior jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Both defendant accused and the state petitioned for review of the decision below. Both parties asked the court to reject the holding that the superior court had original, exclusive jurisdiction over misdemeanor contempt charges involving a violation of superior court orders. Defendant asked that the court hold that both courts had authority to entertain a challenge to the validity of a superior court injunctive order in a contempt proceeding. Plaintiff asked the court to limit any challenge to the validity of the superior court injunction in the context of a misdemeanor contempt prosecution to a writ proceeding in the court of appeals or the present court. The court reversed the decision below, concluding that the municipal court and the appellate department of the superior court had authority to entertain defendant's challenge to the validity of the superior court injunctive order that was the basis of the charges of contempt. The court noted that settled state law had established that there could be no contempt of a void injunctive order, and that assertedly unconstitutional orders were subject to challenge when contempt was charged, and on review of any contempt judgment.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the matter was remanded. Both the municipal court and the appellate department of the superior court had authority to entertain defendant accused's challenge to the validity of the superior court's injunctive order, because defendant was entitled to challenge the injunction on constitutional grounds when he was charged with contempt and to seek appellate review of the contempt judgment on the same grounds.

 

99 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Bright


No. S044186.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


12 Cal. 4th 652; 909 P.2d 1354; 49 Cal. Rptr. 2d 732; 1996 Cal. LEXIS 200; 96 Daily Journal DAR 1290; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 870



February 5, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied June 17, 1996, Reported at: 1996 U.S. LEXIS 3947.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR132839, Norbert Ehrenfreund, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal, reversing the trial court's dismissal of the penalty allegation and remanding the case for reinstatement of that allegation, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the appellate court (California), which held that Cal. Penal Code § 664(a) had not established a greater degree of simple attempted murder, and which held that a verdict convicting defendant of attempted murder without a finding that the offense attempted was willful, deliberate, and premeditated murder had not been an acquittal, for double jeopardy purposes, of a greater offense under § 664(a).


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of attempted murder but was unable to make a finding on a premeditation allegation under Cal. Penal Code § 664(a). After the trial court declared a mistrial and set a retrial as to premeditation, defendant claimed that double jeopardy under U.S. Const. amend. V and Cal. Const. art. I, § 15 barred retrial because defendant had been placed in jeopardy on the charge of attempted premeditated murder. Defendant claimed that the conviction of attempted murder, without specifying the degree, constituted an acquittal of attempted premeditate murder and a conviction of second degree attempted murder, a lesser-included offense. The appellate court rejected the claim, and defendant sought review. The court affirmed and found that § 644(a) had not divided attempted murder into degrees. Instead, the court found that § 644(a) was a penalty provision. Therefore, the court found that the verdict finding defendant guilty of attempted murder had not constituted an acquittal of a greater degree of that offense and had not barred on double jeopardy grounds a retrial of the separate penalty allegation that the attempted murder was premeditated under § 644(a).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment holding a statute had not established a greater degree of attempted murder because the statute, which imposed greater punishment for attempted murder that was willful, deliberate, and premeditated, had been a penalty provision that increased punishment for the attempt. Therefore, defendant had not been placed in jeopardy by retrial of the penalty allegation when a verdict failed to make a finding on premeditation.
