People v. Lucas


No. S004788, Crim. No. 26422.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


12 Cal. 4th 415; 907 P.2d 373; 48 Cal. Rptr. 2d 525; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 7350; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 70; 96 Daily Journal DAR 96



December 29, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified on Denial of Rehearing February 21, 1996.
Modified by, Rehearing denied by People v. Lucas, 12 Cal. 4th 825a, 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1042 (1996)
Writ of certiorari denied Lucas v. California, 519 U.S. 838, 136 L. Ed. 2d 66, 117 S. Ct. 114, 1996 U.S. LEXIS 5049 (1996)
Habeas corpus proceeding at In re Lucas, 2004 Cal. LEXIS 6772 (Cal., July 26, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-471259, John A. Torribio, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant was entitled to automatic review of the judgment of the Los Angeles County Superior Court (California), which imposed the death penalty following his jury conviction of two counts of first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code §187, burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 459, as well as special circumstance findings for multiple murder, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3), and burglary murder, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(vii).


OVERVIEW: On appeal of his capital murder convictions, appellant first raised numerous arguments in an attempt to establish that his trial counsel was ineffective. The court carefully reviewed and rejected each of the claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, considering the abundant evidence in the record of defendant's involvement in the murders and the primary defense theory that appellant committed the crimes in a drugged state and therefore lacked the mental capacity to be held accountable. Similarly, the court carefully reviewed and rejected numerous other errors asserted by appellant with respect to the guilt phase of the trial and the penalty phase of trial. In each case, the court determined that the error either had not been preserved for appellate review, was not prejudicial and did not warrant reversal, or lacked merit. Appellant also claimed that the cumulative prejudice of the asserted judicial errors, prosecutorial misconduct, and ineffective assistance of counsel required reversal of his convictions. Having considered each claim on the merits, the court concluded that neither singly nor cumulatively did they establish prejudice requiring reversal of the convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in its entirety. Each of appellant's claims of error were reviewed on the merits and rejected. Although defendant contended that a combination of judicial error, prosecutorial misconduct, and ineffective assistance of counsel required reversal of the death judgment, the court found no possibility of any cumulative prejudicial impact on the penalty trial.

 

101 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Pacific Merchant Shipping Assn. v. Voss


No. S044869.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


12 Cal. 4th 503; 907 P.2d 430; 48 Cal. Rptr. 2d 582; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 7353; 96 Cal. Daily Op. Service 62; 96 Daily Journal DAR 69; 18 Int'l Trade Rep. (BNA) 2362



December 29, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied May 28, 1996, Reported at: 1996 U.S. LEXIS 3462.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. CV524434, Joe S. Gray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff shipping association appealed from an order of the court of appeals (California), which reversed a grant of summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs. On behalf of a class of marine carriers that paid an inspection fee imposed only on carriers of foreign agricultural goods, plaintiffs had filed an action against defendant state officials alleging that the inspection fee violated the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff shipping association won summary judgment from the trial court on its claim that Cal. Food & Agric. Code §§ 5352, 5353(b), and Cal. Code Regs. tit. 3, § 3560(b) were facially unconstitutional as unjustifiably discriminating against foreign commerce in violation of the negative aspect of the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3. The statutes and regulation authorized the state to levy an inspection fee on ships carrying agricultural goods into California from foreign countries. However, no fee was levied on carriers bringing agricultural goods in from other states, nor on carriers transporting goods intrastate. The court agreed with the trial court that the funding mechanism for inspections of ships carrying agricultural goods into California from foreign countries did not pass constitutional muster under the Commerce Clause. By imposing fees only on carriers of foreign goods, the court held that the funding scheme was facially unconstitutional. While such a facially discriminatory funding mechanism might be justifiable under the applicable "strictest scrutiny" standard, none of the arguments advanced by defendant state officials were sufficient justification.


OUTCOME: The court agreed with the trial court's judgment that the cited provisions for funding the agricultural inspection program were facially discriminatory against foreign commerce, and that the discrimination could not be justified under the controlling "strictest scrutiny" standard. The court therefore reversed the judgment of the lower appellate court holding the provisions to be nondiscriminatory.

 

102 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Larsen v. Dep't of Motor Vehicles


No. S040219.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


12 Cal. 4th 278; 906 P.2d 1306; 48 Cal. Rptr. 2d 151; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 7348; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9875; 95 Daily Journal DAR 17089



December 26, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 697690, Eleanor M. Palk, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, and that court is directed to enter judgment denying the petition for writ of mandate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent department of motor vehicles challenged the decision of the court of appeal (California), which held that before suspending a driver's license based on an out-of-state conviction, it must afford the driver an opportunity to challenge the constitutional validity of the conviction in a forum in this state.


OVERVIEW: Appellant driver was convicted of driving while ability impaired in New York. Respondent department of motor vehicles issued an order suspending appellant's license for six months. The superior court denied appellant's request for a writ of mandate, but the court of appeal reversed. The court reversed the court of appeal and held that appellant could not contest the validity of his New York conviction through an action for a writ of mandate. It held that the appropriate place to challenge the constitutionality of his conviction was the State of New York. It further determined that the act of respondent in suspending his driver's license was merely an administrative act performing a mandatory function and that it had no duty to decide the validity of the prior conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeal and determined that the act of respondent department of motor vehicles in suspending a driver's license based on a conviction of driving under the influence was performing a mandatory function and had no duty to decide the validity of the prior conviction. The court held that a constitutional challenge to the validity of the conviction should be brought in the rendering court system.

People v. Sanchez


No. S007780.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


12 Cal. 4th 1; 906 P.2d 1129; 47 Cal. Rptr. 2d 843; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 6801; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9597; 95 Daily Journal DAR 16651



December 14, 1995, Decided  
December 14, 1995, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modified by, Rehearing denied by People v. Sanchez, 12 Cal. 4th 825b, 1996 Cal. LEXIS 1043 (1996)
Writ of certiorari denied Sanchez v. California, 519 U.S. 835, 117 S. Ct. 108, 136 L. Ed. 2d 61, 1996 U.S. LEXIS 5010 (1996)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Motion denied by, Certificate of appealability granted, in part, Certificate of appealability denied, in part Sanchez v. Chappell, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 96408 (E.D. Cal., July 22, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 34648, Gerald K. Davis, Judge.
People v. Sanchez, 1989 Cal. LEXIS 2984 (Cal., Sept. 5, 1989)



DISPOSITION: We conclude the judgment should be affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision from the Superior Court of Kern County (California) which sentenced him to death for the first degree murder of three victims, based on the multiple murder special-circumstances as to two of the victims.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with the first degree murder of three people stemming from two different incidents. Defendant waived his right to a jury trial and submitted the case on the basis of the preliminary hearing transcripts. He was found guilty of the first degree murder, with special circumstances as to two of the murders and was given the death penalty. The court found that defendant had properly waived his constitutional rights in accepting the procedure and that there was sufficient evidence to convict based on his participation in the crimes. The fact that one of defendant's attorneys faced disbarment at the time of the trial did not render him ineffective or create a conflict in interest. Application of the reporter's shield law immunity, which allowed reporter to refuse to testify about nonpublished material given to him by defendant, did not constitute a violation of defendant's right to confrontation or deny him a fair trial. In reviewing the sentencing procedure, the court found no error in the voir dire, no abuse of discretion in admitting photographs, and sufficient aggravating circumstances to support the death penalty. Thus, the decision was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision which convicted defendant of first degree murder and sentenced him to death because it found that he had been properly informed of his constitutional rights, he had been represented by competent counsel, he had been afforded a fair trial, and there were special circumstances to support the death penalty.

Amwest Sur. Ins. Co. v. Wilson


No. S037405.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


11 Cal. 4th 1243; 906 P.2d 1112; 48 Cal. Rptr. 2d 12; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 6802; 95 Daily Journal DAR 16625; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9590



December 14, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C704879, Dzintra I. Janavs, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent insurer sought review of an order of the Court of Appeal (California), which held that Cal. Ins. Code § 1861.135, which exempted surety insurance from the requirements of Proposition 103, the Insurance Rate Reduction and Reform Act, was invalid.


OVERVIEW: Respondent insurer filed an action in superior court, challenging the validity of Proposition 103, the Insurance Rate Reduction and Reform Act (Act), as it applied to surety insurance. The Act imposed a rate rollback and required that rate increases be approved by the insurance commissioner. Subsequently, Cal. Ins. Code § 1861.135 was enacted, exempting surety insurance from the Act. Appellant intervenors, the Act's drafters, then filed a claim asserting that § 1861.135 was invalid because it did not further the Act's purposes. The superior court held that § 1861.135 was valid. The court of appeal reversed, finding § 1861.135 invalid. The court affirmed, holding that § 1861.135 was invalid because it did not further the Act's purposes. The court held that § 1861.135 amended the Act because it exempted surety insurance from the Act's rollback provision. The court further held that the legislature could not amend the Act in a way that was inconsistent with the Act's purposes. The court also noted that the court of appeal's opinion did not satisfy Cal. Const. art. VI, § 14 because the decision failed to state reasons for the concurrence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the court of appeals holding that the statutory exemption of surety insurance from the Insurance Rate Reduction and Reform Act (Act) was invalid because the exemption, as an amendment the Act, failed to further the purpose of the Act.

Before then, respondent county court adopted Fresno County Local R. 22, which transferred petitioner's duties to the court executive officer. CASE SUMMARY:

Defendant's counsel was not proven ineffective, defendant waived his right to be unshackled or present at the trial due to his unruly behavior, and the aggravating evidence introduced during the penalty phase was not prejudicial to defendant.

People v. Memro

Time for Granting or Denying Rehearing Extended, 02/28/1996
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Memro v. California, 519 U.S. 834, 117 S. Ct. 106, 136 L. Ed. 2d 60, 1996 U.S. LEXIS 5000 (1996)
Motion granted by People v. Memro (Harold Ray), 2011 Cal. LEXIS 4043 (Cal., Apr. 13, 2011)
Motion granted by People v. Memro (Harold Ray), 2011 Cal. LEXIS 7839 (Cal., July 27, 2011)
Writ of habeas corpus denied In re Renos, 2012 Cal. LEXIS 8295 (Cal., Aug. 30, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A445665, John A. Torribio, Judge.
People v. Memro, 38 Cal. 3d 658, 214 Cal. Rptr. 832, 700 P.2d 446, 1985 Cal. LEXIS 280 (1985)



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant was granted an automatic appeal of a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that imposed the death penalty after finding him guilty of two counts of first degree murder and one count of second degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was found guilty of the first degree murder of two young boys along with second degree murder of another young boy. The death penalty was assessed allowing appellant an automatic appeal. Upon review, appellant argued several grounds including a failure by counsel to conduct further voir dire regarding jurors' opposition to the death penalty, an allegation that his confession was coerced, a failure to preserve evidence in police records, a prosecutorial failure to disclose favorable information, and a failure to instruct the jury on a lesser-included offense. The court held no constitutional deficiency in counsel's performance on voir dire had been shown. Further, the court found beyond a reasonable doubt that appellant's confession was free and voluntary, that police records were purged in good faith and according to established procedures, that favorable evidence was only material if shown that the result of the proceedings would have been different by its admission, and concluded that appellant would have been entitled to an instruction on a lesser-included offense only if there was evidence absolving him from guilt of the greater offense. The penalty was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the superior court that returned a verdict of death upon appellant because the record was void of any constitutional deficiencies by counsel, appellant's confession was voluntarily given, and the evidence fully supported the charges presented.

 

110 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Kopp v. Fair Pol. Practices Com.


No. S038571.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


11 Cal. 4th 607; 905 P.2d 1248; 47 Cal. Rptr. 2d 108; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 6792; 95 Daily Journal DAR 15816; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9049



November 30, 1995, Decided



DISPOSITION:  Accordingly, we deny the relief requested by petitioners.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a taxpayer's association and members of the state assembly, sought to have a writ of mandate issued requiring respondent, state democratic party, to enforce provisions of Proposition 73, a local and statewide finance reform measure passed by voters in 1988.


OVERVIEW: In 1988, the voters of California enacted Proposition 73, a local and statewide finance reform bill that limited contributions to political candidates. Petitioner taxpayer's association and members of the state assembly sought enforcement of the proposition. Respondent, the state democratic party, argued doing so would violate the federal Constitution. As such, the issue before the state supreme court became whether a court may reform a statute to conform it to constitutional requirements rather than simply declaring it unconstitutional and unenforceable. The supreme court held that a court may reform a statute to satisfy constitutional requirements if it can conclude with confidence that it is possible to reform the statute in a manner that closely effectuates policy judgments clearly articulated by the enacting body, and the enacting body would have preferred such a reformed version of the statute rather than invalidation of the statute. The supreme court held that it should not, however, reform the provisions requested because neither reformation proposed would closely effectuate policy judgments clearly expressed by the electorate, thus reformation was impermissible.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioners request to issue a writ of mandate requiring respondent enforce provisions of Proposition 73 because the provisions were unconstitutional and rewriting or reforming it to make it constitutional may not clearly effectuate the policy judgments that were expressed by the voters when the bill was passed.

 

111 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Estrada


No. S043210.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


11 Cal. 4th 568; 904 P.2d 1197; 46 Cal. Rptr. 2d 586; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 6653; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8843; 95 Daily Journal DAR 15297



November 20, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. KA010224, Robert M. Martinez, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment from the Court of Appeal (California), that affirmed defendant's conviction for felony murder under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(d) (1990) and held that the trial court had no sua sponte duty, absent a request, to define for the jury the phrase reckless indifference to human life.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of felony murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(d) (1990). Defendant sought review and argued that the trial court had a sua sponte duty to define the statutory phrase "reckless indifference to human life" when it instructed the jury regarding the felony murder allegation. The appellate court affirmed defendant's conviction and found that the trial court was not required, in the absence of a request, to further explain the statutory phrase to the jury. Defendant sought further review. The supreme court found that defendant failed to request an explanatory instruction on reckless indifference to human life. Moreover, the statutory phrase "reckless indifference to human life" was commonly understood to mean that a defendant subjectively appreciated that his or her conduct created a grave risk to human life, and thus amply conveyed the meaning of § 190.2(d). As such, the supreme court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court because the trial court had no sua sponte duty, absent a request, to further explain the statutory phrase "reckless indifference to human life" for the jury.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court that found a trial court had no sua sponte duty, absent a request, to define the phrase "reckless indifference to human life" because the statutory phrase was commonly understood to mean that a defendant subjectively appreciated that his or her conduct created a grave risk to human life, and thus properly conveyed the meaning of the felony murder statute.

 

112 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Sanders


No. S004484, Crim. No. 22891.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


11 Cal. 4th 475; 905 P.2d 420; 46 Cal. Rptr. 2d 751; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 6654; 95 Daily Journal DAR 15309; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8846



November 20, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified January 30, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. A368603 and A364692 James M. Ideman, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant was granted an automatic appeal of a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which imposed the death penalty after finding him guilty of murder including multiple-murder and felony-murder-robbery special circumstances, robbery, attempted robbery, assault with a deadly weapon and conspiracy.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was found guilty of murder, robbery, attempted robbery, assault with a deadly weapon and conspiracy. The crimes took place while appellant and others were robbing a restaurant while employees were closing the establishment. Appellant was assessed the death penalty from which an automatic appeal was allowed. Upon review, appellant argued several grounds including the exclusion of his expert's testimony regarding the accuracy of eyewitness identification, the denial of a motion to change venue because of pretrial publicity, the exclusion of former testimony of an unavailable witness, prosecutorial misconduct, and errors in jury instructions. The court held that the expert's theory regarding the inaccuracy of eyewitness identification lacked scientific acceptance, a change of venue was not required as the county was the most populous metropolitan area in the state considerably offsetting any adverse publicity, there was no reasonable likelihood that comments by the prosecution caused injury to appellant, and appellant had not requested modification of jury instructions regarding accomplice liability. Verdict modification was denied, and the penalty was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court vacated three of appellant's multiple-murder special circumstances based on duplicity, but affirmed the decision of the superior court that returned a verdict of death upon appellant.

Santa Clara County Local Transportation Authority v. Guardino


No. S036269.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


11 Cal. 4th 220; 902 P.2d 225; 45 Cal. Rptr. 2d 207; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 5383; 95 Daily Journal DAR 13017; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7622



September 28, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified on Denial of Rehearing December 14, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY: Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District. Ct. App. No. H010835.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner agency appealed a decision from the Sixth Appellate District Court (California), which denied petitioner's writ of mandate to validate a proposed sales tax to fund transportation projects pursuant to the Local Transportation Authority and Improvement Act, Cal. Pub. Util. Code §§ 180000-180264, in favor of respondent auditor controller.


OVERVIEW: A tax to fund transportation projects proposed by petitioner agency under the Local Transportation Authority and Improvement Act, Cal. Pub. Util. Code §§ 180000-180264, was approved by a majority but less than two-thirds of the voters. Nonprofit organizations and individuals opposed to the ordinance filed an action in the lower court. Respondent auditor controller refused to sign bonds until the tax was determined to be valid. Petitioner filed a writ of mandate in the appellate court to compel respondent to sign bonds and to validate the tax. Petitioner claimed the tax did not violate the two-thirds voter approval imposed by Cal. Gov't Code § 53722, a provision of Proposition 62, or the two-thirds voter approval imposed by Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4, a provision of Proposition 13. The appellate court held the tax invalid under Proposition 13. Petitioner sought review and the court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court on the ground that the tax was invalid under Proposition 62. The court held that the statutory ground was adequate and therefore dispositive and that there was no need to reach the constitutional question in advance of the necessity of deciding it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court which denied petitioner agency's writ of mandate to validate proposed sales tax in favor of respondent auditor controller. The court held that the tax was invalid under Proposition 62.

In re Morse


No. S041048.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


11 Cal. 4th 184; 900 P.2d 1170; 44 Cal. Rptr. 2d 620; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 4966; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6998; 95 Daily Journal DAR 11919



September 1, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified November 16, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY: State Bar Ct. No. 88-0-10896-AKG.



DISPOSITION: Petitioner Ivan O. B. Morse is suspended from the practice of law in the State of California for four years from the date this decision is final. Execution of the order of suspension is stayed, and Morse is placed on probation for a period of four years, subject to the following conditions:


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney challenged an order in a disciplinary action from the State Bar Court (California) finding that he had engaged in a mass mailing of unlawful, misleading advertisements and recommending a one-year stayed suspension and a three-year probation, conditioned on a 60 day actual suspension.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney sent a mass mailing of advertisements to California homeowners offering assistance in the filing of homestead declarations for a fee. The attorney general obtained an injunction against him, and fines were assessed against him. Petitioner filed a civil action against the state but was defeated through all levels of appeal. In the current disciplinary action, it was found that petitioner had engaged in a mass mailing of unlawful, misleading advertisements and a 60 day actual suspension was ordered. The court affirmed the decision on the issue of misconduct, but increased the period of actual suspension to three years. The court found that petitioner's rights under U.S. Const. amend. I had not been violated since protected commercial speech could not be misleading and that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17537.6, regulating homestead services, was not unconstitutionally vague. The court concluded that petitioner's advertisements were misleading based on California's homestead laws under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 704.710-704.850. The court increased the actual suspension to deter petitioner from further wrongdoing and to require a showing of fitness to practice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order in a disciplinary action finding that petitioner attorney had engaged in a mass mailing of unlawful, misleading advertisements but increased the actual suspension from 60 days to three years to deter him from further wrongdoing and to require a showing of fitness to practice.

 

115 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Smiley v. Citibank (s.D.), N.A.


No. S041711.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


11 Cal. 4th 138; 900 P.2d 690; 44 Cal. Rptr. 2d 441; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 4967; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7006; 95 Daily Journal DAR 11931



September 1, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Cert. granted January 19, 1996, 64 U.S.L.W. 3500.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BC059202, Melvin B. Grover, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons stated above, we conclude that the judgment of the Court of Appeal must be affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from a decision of the Court of Appeal, Second Appellate District for the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) affirming summary judgment in favor of defendant bank, finding late payments included in the term "interest" under the National Bank Act of 1864, 12 U.S.C.S. § 85.


OVERVIEW: The court reviewed and affirmed the lower court's decision on independent grounds, construing the meaning of the word interest to include late fees in application of the National Bank Act of 1864 (Bank Act), 12 U.S.C.S. § 85.The court held that California law was preempted as to defendant bank since the displacement of the usury laws of sister states had always been implicit in the structure of the Bank Act. Because the term "interest" in the National Bank Act of 1864, 12 U.S.C.S. § 85 covered late payment fees, if such fees were allowed by a national bank's home state, it embraced defendant's late payment fees, which were permitted by it's home state. Since defendant's home state allowed late payment fees, plaintiff's defective complaint could not be cured by amendment alleging as a material fact that it did not, and therefore summary judgment was proper.


OUTCOME: Affirmed summary judgment for the defendant as the National Bank Act pre-empted California law and the term "interest" included late payments under defendant's applicable home-state law.

 

116 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Cal. ex rel. State Lands Com v. Superior Court


No. S037729.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


11 Cal. 4th 50; 900 P.2d 648; 44 Cal. Rptr. 2d 399; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 4964; 95 Daily Journal DAR 11839; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6950



August 31, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 506331, Ronald B. Robie, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal denying the petition for writ of mandate is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state challenged an order from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) that granted a motion for summary judgment in favor of respondent landowners holding that they owned land developed as the result of natural accretion under Cal. Civ. Code § 1014.


OVERVIEW: Over the course of many years, hydraulic mining, wing dams, levees, and dredging caused sediment to be washed upstream and to form into a parcel of land. The land's ownership was disputed by petitioner state and respondent land owners, and the trial court granted summary judgment under Cal. Civ. Code § 1014 in favor of respondents on the issue of hydraulic mining alone, holding that the land was the result of natural accretion and therefore did not belong to petitioner, and the court of appeal denied petitioner's writ of mandate. On appeal, the court affirmed but rejected the basis for that order. The court determined that it was appropriate to review this case despite the fact that the parties had reached a settlement because the appeal raised issues of continuing public importance. The court found that the source of the sediment was originally artificial, but the large distance between hydraulic mining and the resulting parcel made it impossible to determine if the mining was the direct cause of the accretion; therefore, the summary judgment in favor of respondents was appropriate. The court also held that Cal. Pub. Res. Code 1014 prohibited an award of attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment in favor of respondent landowners that found that they owned land developed as the result of accretion but disagreed with the reasoning. The court found that hydraulic mining had created artificial accretion, but the large distance between the mining and the parcel made it impossible to determine if the mining was the direct cause of the accretion; therefore, the land did not belong to petitioner state.

 

117 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Heikes


No. S041579.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


10 Cal. 4th 1211; 899 P.2d 1349; 44 Cal. Rptr. 2d 155; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 4787; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6747; 95 Daily Journal DAR 11519



August 24, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 181992, Patrick L. McMahon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Husband's transfer in 1976 of his separate real property to the joint ownership of his wife and himself gave wife a vested property interest that cannot constitutionally be impaired through retroactive application of the reimbursement provisions of section 4800.2 that first took effect on January 1, 1984. The trial court's ruling to the contrary, affirmed by the Court of Appeal, was in error. Accordingly, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-wife sought review of an order by a court of appeals (California) that affirmed the trial court's decision to allow a new trial to determine respondent ex-husband's and appellant's interest in land divided as community property in their action for dissolution of marriage on the ground that former Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2 (1984), could be applied retroactively to land transfers made prior to the date that § 4800.2 took effect.


OVERVIEW: In 1976 respondent ex-husband transferred two parcels of land he owned as separate property to appellant ex-wife and himself as joint tenants. The property was divided as community property in their action for dissolution of marriage. In a separate case, the court held that where a spouse had not gained a vested interest in property, former Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.2 (1984) could be applied retroactively. Section 4800.2 required reimbursement for separate property contributions to the acquisition of any property that was divided as community property, unless waived in writing. On that basis, respondent argued that the court had changed its views on the constitutional restriction on retroactive application of § 4800.2. The trial court ordered a new trial as to the parties' interests in the two parcels of land, and the court of appeals affirmed. The court reversed and held that the applicability of § 4800.2 was limited by the due process clause of the Cal. Const. art. I, § 7 to property acquired on or after January 1, 1984 and that the 1976 transfer gave appellant a vested property interest that could not be constitutionally impaired through retroactive application of § 4800.2.


OUTCOME: The court overruled the court of appeals judgment and held that respondent ex-husband's transfer in 1976 of his separate real property to the joint ownership of appellant ex-wife and himself gave appellant a vested property interest that could not be constitutionally impaired through retroactive application of property reimbursement statute's provisions that first took effect on January 1, 1984.

 

118 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



California Correctional Peace Officers Assn. v. State Personnel Bd.


No. S041269.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


10 Cal. 4th 1133; 899 P.2d 79; 43 Cal. Rptr. 2d 693; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 4784; 95 Daily Journal DAR 11119; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6523



August 17, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BS010132, William W. Huss, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal reversing the judgment of the superior court and remanding for further proceedings is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeal (California) reversed and remanded a judgment that ordered respondent state Personnel Board to hold hearings and/or render decisions in appellant association's administrative appeal from the state department of corrections' disciplinary actions against about 50 civil service employees. The court reasoned that the time limit of Cal. Gov't Code § 18671.1 was mandatory and jurisdictional. Both parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The state department of corrections initiated disciplinary actions against about 50 civil service employees. The employees appealed to respondent state Personnel Board for a formal adjudicatory hearing. Appellant employee association moved to dismiss on the ground that respondent failed to hold a timely hearing and/or to render a timely decision. Respondent denied the motions. The appellate court reversed and remanded the superior court's order that required respondent to hold hearings and render decisions, finding that the statutory time limit for rendering a decision on an employee appeal was mandatory and jurisdictional. Both parties sought further review. The court affirmed on different grounds, holding that respondent did not lose jurisdiction over the appeal when it failed to render a decision within the directory time limit of Cal. Gov't Code § 18671.1. However, employees who had not waived the time limit could seek a writ of mandate to compel respondent to decide the appeal by a date certain. If respondent made a decision in the interim, the employee could dismiss the mandate proceeding and seek review by petition for writ of mandamus under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the reversal of a judgment that ordered respondent state Personnel Board to hold hearings and/or render decisions in appellant association's administrative appeal from the state department of corrections' disciplinary actions against about 50 civil service employees, holding that judicial review, including mandamus, was available to such employees where respondent failed to comply with directory time limits.

 

119 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Adoption of Michael H.


No. S039871.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


10 Cal. 4th 1043; 898 P.2d 891; 43 Cal. Rptr. 2d 445; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 4314; 61 A.L.R.5th 769; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6006; 95 Daily Journal DAR 10263



July 31, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied March 18, 1996, Reported at: 1996 U.S. LEXIS 1838.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. A37093, Michael D. Wellington, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant adoptive parents filed a petition to terminate respondent natural father's legal status as a child's father, Cal. Fam. Code § 7662, and respondent filed a petition to establish a parental relationship, Cal. Fam. Code § 7630(a)(1). The trial court held that respondent had a constitutional right to veto the child's adoption unless he was an unfit parent. The California Court of Appeal affirmed. The court granted review to appellants.


OVERVIEW: A teenage girl became pregnant with respondent's child, and the two agreed to give the baby up for adoption. Prior to the birth, respondent and the birth mother parted ways, and respondent later decided that he wanted to keep his child. He searched for an attorney who would take his case for free, sought help from the media and politicians, and researched the law himself. Appellants, the adoptive parents, sought to terminate respondent's parental rights. The trial court held that respondent's efforts demonstrated a commitment to his parental responsibilities sufficient to invoke his U.S. Const. amend. XIV right to veto the adoption, and the appellate court affirmed. The court reversed, holding that respondent did not promptly come forward as soon as he learned of the pregnancy and demonstrate a full commitment to his parental responsibilities, as required to perfect his inchoate constitutional right. The court held that the disparate treatment of unwed mothers and unwed fathers under California law was substantially related to important government objectives. The court directed the trial court to terminate respondent's parental rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeal and remanded the case to the trial court for entry of judgment against respondent on his petition to declare a father-child relationship, and in favor of appellants on their petition to terminate respondent's parental rights and adopt the child. The court held that the father was not a presumed father and had not acted promptly enough to perfect his constitutional right to veto adoption.

People v. Hawkins


No. S014199.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


10 Cal. 4th 920; 897 P.2d 574; 42 Cal. Rptr. 2d 636; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 4068; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5702; 95 Daily Journal DAR 9674



July 20, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified October 18, 1995. Rehearing Denied October 18, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 6292.
Writ of habeas corpus denied In re Hawkins, 1995 Cal. LEXIS 5198 (Cal., Aug. 30, 1995)
Modified by, Rehearing denied by People v. Hawkins, 11 Cal. 4th 415a, 1995 Cal. LEXIS 6292 (1995)
Writ of certiorari denied Hawkins v. California, 517 U.S. 1193, 116 S. Ct. 1685, 134 L. Ed. 2d 786, 1996 U.S. LEXIS 3108 (1996)
Writ of habeas corpus denied In re Hawkins, 1999 Cal. LEXIS 1335 (Cal., Mar. 9, 1999)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Hawkins v. Wong, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 92175 (E.D. Cal., Sept. 2, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. CR81617, Rothwell B. Mason, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For all of the foregoing, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) of two counts of first degree murder, one count of robbery, and one count of attempted murder, and was sentenced to death. His case was automatically appealed pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1239(b).


OVERVIEW: Substantial circumstantial evidence tied defendant to a murder, including the testimony of a ballistics expert concluding that a projectile removed from the victim's body was fired from the same gun used to murder another victim. Defendant was tied by eyewitness evidence to this second murder. Defendant was convicted of two counts of first degree murder, as well as one count each of robbery and attempted murder. The jury also found true the felony murder special circumstance under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(i), as well as the multiple murder special circumstance under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3). Defendant was sentenced to death. The court affirmed, rejecting numerous alleged error in both the guilt and penalty phases of defendant's trial. The court found that defendant was not entitled to inform the penalty phase jury that his first guilt phase jury deadlocked on his punishment, that evidence of an uncharged crime was properly admitted to explain a witness' prior inconsistent statement, that the manner in which defendant killed justified a finding of premeditation, and that the court had no sua sponte duty to instruct the jury on lesser-related offenses.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions of murder, robbery, and attempted murder, rejecting his contentions that the trial court committed various error in both the guilt and penalty phases of his trial, including alleged error relating to the jury charge, the admission of evidence, the sufficiency of the evidence, and prosecutorial misconduct.

Adams v. Commission on Judicial Performance


No. S042149.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


10 Cal. 4th 866; 897 P.2d 544; 42 Cal. Rptr. 2d 606; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 4069; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5688; 95 Daily Journal DAR 9655



July 20, 1995, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Commission on Judicial Performance filed a recommendation that petitioner judge be removed from office, asserting that he had committed wilful misconduct in office, prejudicial conduct, and improper action. Petitioner filed a petition to modify or reject the recommendation, or for a new hearing before the commission, asserting that he was denied due process of law at his hearing.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Commission on Judicial Performance (commission) conducted a preliminary investigation into petitioner trial court judge's conduct. Respondent then filed a notice of formal proceedings against petitioner, recommending that he be removed from office. The court agreed. It found that clear and convincing evidence supported the commission's findings that petitioner committed prejudicial conduct under Cal. Const. art. VI, § 18(d), including his involvement in the purchase of two vehicles, a celebratory dinner, a fishing trip with a law firm that appeared before him, the acceptance of a fee write-off from another firm appearing before him, and his provision of incomplete answers during the commission's investigation into his conduct. Thus, the court ordered petitioner removed from office, but permitted him to return to the practice of law if he passed a professional responsibility examination and was otherwise qualified. Petitioner's allegations of lack of due process in his hearing before the commission were rejected.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the petitioner judge removed from office, but permitted him to resume the practice of law if he passed the professional responsibility examination. Removal was justified because he accepted gifts or other benefits from attorneys and firms appearing before him and because he deliberately provided false information to the body investigating his alleged misconduct.

People v. Stanley


No. S004605, Crim. No. 23532.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


10 Cal. 4th 764; 897 P.2d 481; 42 Cal. Rptr. 2d 543; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 3767; 95 Daily Journal DAR 9004; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5274



July 6, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified September 13, 1995.
Modified by, Rehearing denied by People v. Stanley, 11 Cal. 4th 219d, 1995 Cal. LEXIS 5683 (1995)
Stay granted by People v. Stanley, 1996 Cal. LEXIS 537 (Cal., Jan. 30, 1996)
Writ of habeas corpus denied In re Stanley, 2002 Cal. LEXIS 4849 (Cal., July 17, 2002)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Butte County, No. 79825, Jean Morony and Reginald Watt, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's conviction for first degree murder entered by the Superior Court of Butte County (California), was automatically appealed, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1239(b), because of the jury's decision to sentence defendant to the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Upon a conviction for first degree murder and death sentence, defendant pursued an automatic appeal. The court affirmed the conviction and sentence. The court stated that the denial of defendant's motion of suppress evidence was proper based on the "law of the case" doctrine and that an intervening decision was a clear break from existing precedent. The statutory provisions governing the transfer of venue were followed by the trial court. The court also ruled that there was substantial evidence to support defendant's conviction for first degree murder and that the jury was properly instructed as to its elements, including the issue of special circumstances. Defendant's motion to sever the special circumstances issue from the issue of guilt was properly denied. The resolution of defendant's competency prior to sentencing comported with the applicable statutory and constitutional provisions. Uncharged violent acts committed by defendant were properly introduced because of their possible relevance to the existence of aggravating circumstances that could have warranted the death penalty. The court found that comments made by the prosecutor were fair comment on the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's first degree murder conviction and death sentence.

 

123 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court (Aishman)


No. S033133.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


10 Cal. 4th 735; 896 P.2d 1387; 42 Cal. Rptr. 2d 377; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 3712; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5170; 95 Daily Journal DAR 8816



July 3, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. ECR6713, Robert E. May, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people sought review of a decision entered by the Court of Appeal (California), which granted its petition for writ of mandate. The people contended that while the appellate court correctly rejected a constitutional challenge to a "hate crimes" statute, Cal. Penal Code. § 422.75, it erroneously concluded that the statute contained an implied specific intent requirement.


OVERVIEW: The people sought and were granted a writ of mandate by an appellate court after respondent superior court granted a motion to dismiss those offenses brought pursuant to a "hate crimes" statute, Cal. Penal Code § 422.75. The people sought review of the writ of mandate because it contended that the appellate court incorrectly concluded that § 422.75 contained an implied specific intent requirement. The court agreed with the people and held that scienter was not an element of § 422.75. The court stated that § 422.75 merely increased the punishment for a felony motivated by prohibited bias. The statute's application was not dependent on the perpetrator acting for the purpose of interfering with the victim's legal rights. Therefore, the court concluded that an element of specific intent was not a part of § 422.75. The court further stated that when a crime was motivated by several factors, the applicability of § 422.75 was dependent upon whether the perpetrator's bias motivation was a substantial factor in bringing about the offense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the writ of mandate granted to the people. However, the court ruled that the appellate court incorrectly concluded that specific intent was an element of a "hate crimes" statute and held that the statute only required that the bias motivation of the perpetrator be a cause in fact of the offense.

 

124 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re M.S.


No. S035200.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


10 Cal. 4th 698; 896 P.2d 1365; 42 Cal. Rptr. 2d 355; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 3713; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5161; 95 Daily Journal DAR 8803



July 3, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 116302 and 116304, Donald S. Mitchell, Douglas C. Munson, and Alex Saldamando, Judges; Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 141289, Wilmont Sweeney, Judge, and Claudette Brooks, Referee.



DISPOSITION: To ensure correct application of section 654 in the calculation of minor A.G.'s maximum commitment time, as to A.G. alone the matter is remanded to the juvenile court for further proceedings consistent with the opinion of this court. In all other respects the judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juveniles appealed the order of the Court of Appeal (California), which affirmed the rejection of their constitutional challenges to two hate crimes statutes, Cal. Penal Code §§ 422.6 and 422.7.


OVERVIEW: Defendant juveniles were convicted of hate crimes proscribed by Cal. Penal Code §§ 422.6, 422.7. They challenged the validity of those statutes on various constitutional grounds. The court rejected the constitutional challenges and affirmed the convictions. The court held that § 422.6 was not constitutionally vague because it criminalized only those threats that conveyed the imminent threat of violence. The court further stated that § 422.7 was constitutionally permissible because it increased punishment for misdemeanors motivated by prohibited bias. As such, the statutes, the court concluded, punished conduct, not the thoughts that may have served as a motivator. The statutes were also constitutionally valid because they required the specific intent to interfere with a person's legally protected rights under state or federal law. The court stated that when multiple factors were present, the questioned statutes were violated if the prohibited bias was a substantial factor in bringing about the crime. The court remanded to recalculate one defendant's sentence because under Cal. Penal Code § 654, she could not be punished for both assault and battery and violating § 422.6.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that rejected defendant juveniles' constitutional challenge to hate crimes statutes because the statutes properly required specific intent to interfere with legally protected rights, and the statutes punished conduct, not thought or speech. The court remanded to recalculate one defendant's sentence because she should not have been punished for both assault and battery and a hate crime.

 

125 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Warfield v. Peninsula Golf & Country Club


No. S031285.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


10 Cal. 4th 594; 896 P.2d 776; 42 Cal. Rptr. 2d 50; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 3711; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5073; 95 Daily Journal DAR 8607



June 29, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 258736, Aram Serverian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, and the case is remanded to that court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, female golfer, filed a petition for review of the judgment of the Court of Appeal (California), which sustained the trial court's directed verdict for defendant country club, concluding that plaintiff failed to prove that defendant was a business establishment within the meaning of the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 51, and that plaintiff lacked standing to pursue a claim for denial of the right to fair procedure.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff female golfer requested membership in defendant country club after a judgment of dissolution of marriage awarded her ownership of a membership in defendant. Defendant denied plaintiff membership on the basis of a bylaw that permitted defendant to terminate a female spouse's membership in this circumstance. Plaintiff brought suit for damages and injunctive relief. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrer, and after an appeal, plaintiff's case went to trial on remand. The trial court granted defendant's motion for a directed verdict, and the verdict was affirmed on appeal. Plaintiff brought a petition for review. The court held that defendant was a business establishment within the meaning of the Unruh Civil Rights Act (Act), Cal. Civ. Code § 51, and that plaintiff's rights under the Act had been violated when she was excluded from membership. Defendant's regular business transactions with nonmembers were sufficient to bring defendant within the Act's broad reference to all business establishments. The Act's application to defendant did not violate its members' right of association and privacy under U.S. Const. amend. I and XIV or Cal. Const. art. I, § 1.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiff female golfer's petition for review, and it reversed and remanded the lower court's judgment. The court held that defendant country club was a business establishment under the Unruh Civil Rights Act (Act), and that defendant had violated the Act by excluding women from proprietary membership. The court found that the Act did not violate the constitutional rights of association and privacy of defendant's members.

Customer Co. v. City of Sacramento


No. S035410.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


10 Cal. 4th 368; 895 P.2d 900; 41 Cal. Rptr. 2d 658; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 3373; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4459



June 12, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied July 13, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 4451.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 355131, Jeffrey L. Gunther and Joe S. Gray, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant company sought review from a Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) judgment for appellee city on appellant's suit seeking recovery for inverse condemnation under Cal. Const. art. I, § 19 and under the Tort Claims Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 810 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant company brought suit against appellee city to recover for damage that had been caused to appellant's convenience store when police officers fired tear gas into it to subdue a felony suspect. When initial judgment for appellee was affirmed, appellant sought further review solely on its claim for inverse condemnation under Cal. Const. art. I, § 19. The court found that incidental damage to private property by police officers in the course of efforts to enforce the law did not qualify as a basis for an inverse condemnation claim. Further, allowing such an action would allow recovery for damage, attorney fees, and prejudgment interest beyond what was otherwise available under the Tort Claims Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 810 et seq. Thus, the court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, holding that because inverse condemnation claims did not extend to cover incidental damage caused by law enforcement in their enforcement of the law, there was no basis for recovery by appellant in that regard.

People v. Wims


No. S036641.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


10 Cal. 4th 293; 895 P.2d 77; 41 Cal. Rptr. 2d 241; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 3139; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4194; 95 Daily Journal DAR 7199



June 5, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied February 20, 1996, Reported at: 1996 U.S. LEXIS 1061.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 142861, Alex Saldamando, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed insofar as it reversed imposition of the section 12022(b) sentence enhancements of defendants Wims and Ford. In other respects, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People appealed an order of a Court of Appeals (California) in favor of defendants, which reversed a one-year sentencing enhancement that was imposed under Cal. Penal Code § 12022(b).


OVERVIEW: The people sought review of an order that reversed the trial court's imposition of sentencing enhancements for convictions of robbery and use of a deadly weapon. The court granted respondent's petition for review, holding that the trial court erred in failing to instruct the jury as to the factual elements that the prosecution was required to prove before the jury could properly find "true" allegations made under Cal. Penal Code § 12022(b). The court further held that despite the error, there was no impairment of the right to trial by jury nor was there a deprivation of a liberty interest in the exercise of jury sentencing discretion. Moreover, the court held that defendants were not prejudiced by the error. Thus, the decision of the court of appeals was reversed insofar as it reversed imposition of sentence enhancements, and the court affirmed the decision in all other respects.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate division insofar as it vacated the trial court's imposition of sentence enhancements for use of a deadly or dangerous weapon. The court held that the trial court's instruction, while erroneous, was not prejudicial to defendants. The court affirmed the remainder of the judgment.

 

129 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Jenkins


No. S039538.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


10 Cal. 4th 234; 893 P.2d 1224; 40 Cal. Rptr. 2d 903; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2755; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3708



May 18, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 142617, Paul R. Teilh, Judge. * 


* Retired judge of the Santa Clara Superior Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, and the matter is remanded to that court for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant and the state sought review of an order from the Court of Appeal (California), which reversed defendant's sentence of with a minimum of 20 years that was imposed by the superior court under the habitual offender provisions of Cal. Penal Code § 667.7. The court of appeal held that § 667.7 was inapplicable to convictions for murder. The state argued against the exemption of murder and for additional enhancements.


OVERVIEW: In cross appeals from application of Cal. Penal Code § 667.7 in defendant's sentencing, the court of appeal held that § 667.7 was inapplicable to murder convictions. In cross petitions for review, the parties both argued against inapplicability. The state argued also that additional enhancements should have been included in fixing defendant's sentence. The court reversed the decision of the court of appeal, ruling that a defendant who was convicted of murder and met the requirements for sentencing as a habitual offender under § 667.7 was to be sentenced under that section rather than Cal. Penal Code § 190, which governed sentences for murder. The terms of any applicable sentence enhancements, including an enhancement imposed for a prior serious felony conviction under Cal. Penal Code § 667(a), were to be included in the calculation of the minimum period of imprisonment that a defendant was required to serve before becoming eligible for parole. The court held that § 667.7 did not preclude the imposition of consecutive life terms upon a defendant who committed several offenses, each of which was punishable under § 667.7. The court remanded to the court of appeal.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded to the court of appeal, holding that a murderer was to be sentenced as an habitual offender if he qualified, the terms of any applicable sentence enhancements, including an enhancement imposed for a prior serious felony conviction, were to be included in the calculation of the minimum period of imprisonment, and the habitual offender sentencing law did not preclude the imposition of consecutive life terms.

Powers v. City of Richmond


No. S039547.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


10 Cal. 4th 85; 893 P.2d 1160; 40 Cal. Rptr. 2d 839; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2600; 95 Daily Journal DAR 5885; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3418



May 8, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 9104616, David A. Dolgin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff candidate for city council sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals of California that denied plaintiff's petition for writ of mandate and granted defendant city's motion to dismiss. Originally, plaintiff filed the action in the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) and sought to compel defendant city to prepare and release a report pursuant to the Public Records Act, Cal. Gov. Code, § 6250 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff candidate for city council filed suit against defendant city pursuant to the Public Records Act, Cal. Gov. Code, § 6250 et seq., (PRA). Plaintiff wanted the trial court to compel defendant to prepare and release a report. Plaintiff then sought review in the court of appeals by a direct appeal and a petition for writ of mandate. The court of appeals denied the writ and granted defendant's motion to dismiss the direct appeal. Plaintiff appealed, contending that the appellate jurisdiction provision of Cal. Const. art. VI, § 11, conferred a right of direct appeal from final orders and judgments in proceedings in which trial courts exercised original jurisdiction. The court affirmed because the textual analysis of Cal. Const. art. VI, § 11, did not support plaintiff's contention that the appellate jurisdiction provision conferred a right to a direct appeal in such cases and the provision permitted some variation provided that the constitutional powers of the courts were not impaired. In addition, the court concluded that the appellate jurisdiction provision did not confer a constitutionally guaranteed right to a direct appeal in PRA cases.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying plaintiff candidate for city council's writ of mandate and granting defendant city's motion to dismiss plaintiff's direct appeal to the court of appeals was affirmed because the appellate jurisdiction provision of the California Constitution did not confer a right to a direct appeal in proceedings in which trial courts exercised original jurisdiction and particularly in Public Records Act cases.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order by the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which convicted him of murder and several other related offenses and imposed the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of his conviction for robbery-murder, burglary-murder, attempted rape-murder, and multiple murder special circumstance, and the imposition of the death penalty. Sufficient evidence was presented to sustain a jury verdict of guilt, and the penalty of death was appropriate considering the brutal nature of the killings, the robbery, and the sexual assault committed by defendant.

In re York


No. S032327.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


9 Cal. 4th 1133; 892 P.2d 804; 40 Cal. Rptr. 2d 308; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2181; 95 Daily Journal DAR 5451; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3208



April 27, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, Nos. 161088, 161238, 161957, 163882 and 163935, John J. Garibaldi and Robert Michael Foley, Judges; Municipal Court for the Santa Clara Judicial District of Santa Clara County, Nos. C9327030, C9327239, C9327432, C9327583, C9327609 and C9327981, Marliese G. Kim, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Having determined that petitioners' challenges to the decision of the Court of Appeal are without merit, we affirm the judgment of that court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, 11 individuals charged with having committed one or more felonies involving controlled substances, sought review by writ of habeas corpus of the judgment of the court of appeal (California) decision holding that the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) could condition an own-recognizance release upon a defendant's agreement to submit to random drug testing and warrantless search and seizure.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners were unable to post the bail prescribed for their offenses and were given the choice of remaining in custody pending trial upon the charges, or obtaining an own-recognizance (OR) release upon their agreement to comply with specified conditions requiring that petitioners submit to drug and, in some instances, alcohol testing and permit search and seizure of his/her person, residence, and vehicle without a search warrant. Petitioners sought review by writ of habeas corpus, challenging the court of appeal's decision holding that under certain circumstances, a court or magistrate could condition OR release upon a defendant's agreement to submit to random drug testing and warrantless search and seizure. Petitioners contended that the imposition of such conditions upon OR release was contrary to Cal. Penal Code § 1318 and violated several provisions of the federal and state constitutions. The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals, holding that neither statutory nor constitutional provisions upon which petitioners relied prohibited a court, in appropriate circumstances, from conditioning OR release upon defendant's agreement to comply with the challenged terms.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeal, holding that neither the statutory nor the constitutional provisions upon which petitioners, 11 individuals charged with felonies involving controlled substances, relied prohibited a court, in appropriate circumstances, from conditioning an own-recognizance release upon defendant's agreement to submit to random drug testing and warrantless search and seizure during that period.

Tobe v. City of Santa Ana


No. S038530.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


9 Cal. 4th 1069; 892 P.2d 1145; 40 Cal. Rptr. 2d 402; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2179; 95 Daily Journal DAR 5239; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3027



April 24, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 696000, James L. Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Orange County (California) denied plaintiff taxpayer's petition for writ of mandate to bar enforcement of the ordinance and plaintiff homeless persons' demurrers to the complaints that charged plaintiffs with violating the ordinance. The Court of Appeals of California invalidated Santa Ana, Cal., Mun. Code art. VIII, § 10-400 et seq., on constitutional grounds, and respondent city and prosecutor appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayers filed suit seeking a writ of mandate to bar enforcement of Santa Ana, Cal., Mun. Code art. VIII, § 10-400 et seq. The trial court struck a portion of § 10-400, but otherwise denied the writ. Plaintiff homeless persons were charged with violating § 10-400. Petitioners, people who were charged with violating the ordinance, demurred unsuccessfully to the criminal complaints and sought mandate to compel respondent municipal court to sustain the demurrers. Both actions were consolidated on appeal. The court of appeals held that § 10-400 was unconstitutional and the court reversed this decision. Plaintiffs contended that the statute was unconstitutional on its face and as applied. The court held that the petitions had not perfected an "as applied" challenge and § 10-400 was valid on its face. The ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad. The ordinance did not unconstitutionally restrict the right to travel and did not allow punishment based upon status in violation of U.S. Const. amend. VIII and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals that invalidated the ordinance on constitutional grounds because the ordinance was valid on its face and there was no challenge to the ordinance as applied. In addition, the ordinance did not unconstitutionally restrict the right to interstate travel and did not violate the United States and California constitutions by allowing punishment based upon status.

People v. Champion


No. S004486.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


9 Cal. 4th 879; 891 P.2d 93; 39 Cal. Rptr. 2d 547; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2012; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2536; 95 Daily Journal DAR 4423



April 6, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified on Denial of Rehearing June 1, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 365075, William B. Keene, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Defendant Ross's two duplicative multiple-murder special circumstances and defendant Champion's duplicative multiple-murder special circumstance are ordered stricken. In all other respects, the judgments, including the death sentences as to both defendants, are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review by automatic appeal of an order by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that found appellants guilty of special circumstances of robbery murder, burglary murder, and multiple murder. Additionally, it found one appellant guilty of a rape-murder special circumstance. Both appellants received death sentences by the jury at the penalty phase.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were found guilty of special circumstances of robbery murder, burglary murder, and multiple murder and one of rape-murder special circumstance. The jury sentenced both to death. Appellants challenged the judgments and sentences on a number of grounds, arguing that the trial court erred in granting the prosecution's motion to join appellants' cases for trial. Over appellant's objection, the trial court granted the prosecution's motion for joinder of the cases. The court found that the trial court's refusal to sever the cases did not prejudice appellants. Appellants objected to the fact that potential jurors were not asked if jury service would pose a hardship, and that all discharged prospective jurors were wage earners, violating their right to a fair and impartial jury. The court held that appellants' failure to object failed to preserve the issues on appeal. Appellants objected to admission of their taped conversation as a violation of their rights to remain silent. The court found no error, as no secret police interrogation occurred. The court affirmed the judgments and death sentences of both appellants.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and sentence of the trial court that found appellants guilty of special circumstances of robbery murder, burglary murder, and one of rape-murder, but struck appellants' multiple murder convictions, where they were not prejudiced by joinder the cases, they failed to preserve many of their arguments for review, and admission of their taped conversation was proper where no secret police interrogation was involved.

Salazar v. Eastin

SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA

DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.

OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayers filed for injunctive relief and a writ of mandate against respondents, state defendants and a school district, alleging that student transportation fees under Cal. Educ. Code § 39807.5 violated the state constitution's free school clause, Cal. Const. art. IX, § 5, and the equal protection clause. Respondent school district was dismissed, and the superior court ruled for the other respondents, holding that appellants did not join all necessary parties. Subsequently, respondents in the first case filed a motion to vacate. The superior court vacated the injunction. The court of appeal reversed. On review, this court reversed, holding that the order vacating the injunction was proper because appellants failed to prove that any local school district misapplied § 39807.5.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeal's order reversing the superior court's judgment vacating a prior injunction preventing respondents, state defendants, from imposing student transportation fees because appellant taxpayers failed to prove that the statute, which allowed for such fees, was unconstitutionally applied.

DeVita v. County of Napa


No. S037642.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


9 Cal. 4th 763; 889 P.2d 1019; 38 Cal. Rptr. 2d 699; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 706; 95 Daily Journal DAR 2999; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1736



March 6, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Napa County, No. 61839, Herbert W. Walker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For all of the foregoing, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant residents challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeal (California), which affirmed the trial court's judgment that Measure J was a valid exercise of the initiative power, thereby denying appellants relief against respondent county. Appellants argued that the general plan could not be amended by initiative and that future boards of supervisors' authority to amend the general plan could not be limited by mandatory voter approval.


OVERVIEW: The voters of Napa County enacted Measure J, an initiative that amended the land-use element of the county's general plan to preserve agricultural land. Measure J made the redesignation of existing agricultural land and open space conditional on voter approval until the year 2021. Appellant residents brought suit against appellee county, arguing that the general plan could not be amended by initiative and that future boards of supervisors' authority to amend the general plan could not be limited by mandatory voter approval. The trial court concluded that Measure J was a valid exercise of the initiative power. An intermediate court affirmed the judgment. The court found its appellate questions to be whether a county's general plan could be amended by an initiative of the county's electorate acting pursuant to Cal. Const. art. II, § 11, and whether the electorate could properly impose the 30-year voter approval requirements. The court affirmed. Cal. Gov't Code §§ 65100- 65763 did not prohibit the exercise of the initiative power to amend the land use element of a general plan. The court found no statutory or constitutional defect in Measure J's voter approval provisions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower courts' judgments. It concluded that the statutory provisions governing local land use planning did not prohibit the exercise of the initiative power to amend the land use element of a general plan. General plans could be amended by the exercise of the local right of initiative. In addition, they found no statutory or constitutional defect in Measure J's voter approval provisions.

 

138 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Rossi v. Brown


No. S035265.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


9 Cal. 4th 688; 889 P.2d 557; 38 Cal. Rptr. 2d 363; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 708; 95 Daily Journal DAR 2966; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1758



March 6, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 942286, Stuart R. Pollak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court directing issuance of a peremptory writ of mandate should be reversed and the petition denied. The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tax collector challenged the writ of mandate issued by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), and affirmed by the Court of Appeal of California, in response to respondent taxpayers' petition to require appellant to resume collecting a utility tax from residential users, subsequent to a voter initiative to prospectively repeal the tax.


OVERVIEW: The Board of Supervisors reversed an earlier ordinance that exempted residential users from a utility tax on the use of electricity, gas, water, and telephone service. A voter initiative petition seeking to prospectively repeal the tax on residential users qualified for the ballot and was adopted in an election. Appellant tax collector stopped collecting the tax. Respondent taxpayers filed a petition for a writ of mandate, seeking to compel appellant to resume collecting the utility tax from residential users. The trial court issued a peremptory writ of mandate granting the requested relief. Appellant sought review of the writ. It was affirmed in the intermediate court. Appellant petitioned the court for review. The court reversed the lower courts and directed that the petition for writ of mandate be denied. The plain language of the city charter and the California Constitution controlled. While the referendum power expressly precluded a referendum on statutes and ordinances that impose a tax, no such limitation was imposed on the people's exercise of their reserved initiative power. The lower court erred in equating the initiative power with the referendum power.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower courts directing and affirming the issuance of a peremptory writ of mandate against appellant tax collector was reversed and respondent taxpayers' petition was denied. The use of the initiative power to prospectively repeal a tax and to bar future adoption of the tax did not have the same effect as a referendum on a tax ordinance and thus was not prohibited under the city charter and the constitution.
