Bewley v. Franchise Tax Bd.


No. S033257.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


9 Cal. 4th 526; 886 P.2d 1292; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 298; 1995 Cal. LEXIS 5; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 611; 95 Daily Journal DAR 1050



January 23, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 913043, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, and the cause is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from decision of the California Court of Appeal finding plaintiffs were entitled to a tax refund because 31 U.S.C.S. § 3124(a), exempting stocks and obligations of the United States government from state taxation, prohibited California from taxing income from a repurchase agreement.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed appellate court's decision granting plaintiffs tax refund. Refund was granted on the basis that plaintiff's income from a repurchase agreement was exempt from state taxation because of 31 U.S.C.S. § 3124(a) and imposition of the tax violated the supremacy clause of the United States Constitution, U.S. Const., art. VI, cl. 2. Court reversed and held 31 U.S.C.S. § 3124(a) did not exempt the state from taxing income from repurchase agreements involving federal securities. Income from repurchase agreements did not constitute interest on obligations of United States government, as stated in 31 U.S.C.S. § 3124(a). Court remanded because neither the trial nor appellate courts addressed plaintiff's argument that the tax violated the intergovernmental tax immunity doctrine of the supremacy clause of the United States constitution, U.S. Const., art. VI, cl. 2.


OUTCOME: Court reversed appellate court's decision, finding 31 U.S.C.S. § 3124(a) did not prohibit states from taxing income from repurchase agreements because the income did not constitute interest on United States obligations. The court remanded plaintiff's constitutional challenge to the tax because lower courts did not address issue.

Advanced Micro Devices, Inc. v. Intel Corp.


No. S033874.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


9 Cal. 4th 362; 885 P.2d 994; 36 Cal. Rptr. 2d 581; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 6588; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 59; 95 Daily Journal DAR 128



December 30, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 626879, Read Ambler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We conclude the challenged portions of the arbitrator's award were within his authority to fashion remedies for a breach of contract. The superior court correctly confirmed the award under section 1286. The judgment of the Court of Appeal, reversing that of the superior court, is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation challenged the decision of the Court of Appeals (California), which reversed an order of the superior court upholding a decision of an arbitrator in a dispute between appellant and appellee corporation based on a written agreement between the parties.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporation and appellee corporation entered into an agreement that would allow the exchange of information between the parties and allow each party the rights to second source each other's products. A dispute over the rights and obligations of the parties arose and the matter went to arbitration in accordance with the terms of the agreement. The arbitrator found that appellee extensively breached its obligations to act in good faith and deal fairly. The arbitrator awarded appellant permanent and royalty-free license to any intellectual property owned by appellee. He also awarded appellant a two-year extension of certain patent and copyright licenses. The superior court upheld the award, but the court of appeals reversed. Appellant challenged the decision and the supreme court reversed. The court held that the arbitrator's remedy was rationally derived from the contract and the breach. The parties had agreed that any relief awarded could be judicially reviewed only to determine if the award exceeded the arbitrator's powers. The court held that the arbitrator's decision was within the scope of his authority as measured by that standard.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appeals court that held that the award of the arbitrator was not rationally connected to the agreement between appellant corporation and appellee corporation. The court reversed, holding that its review was limited to determining if the award exceeded the arbitrator's powers. Because the arbitrator's decision was within the scope of his authority, the award favoring appellant corporation was upheld.

People v. Heitzman


No. S035624.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


9 Cal. 4th 189; 886 P.2d 1229; 37 Cal. Rptr. 2d 236; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 6589; 95 Daily Journal DAR 78; 95 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9



December 30, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-88586, Luis A. Cardenas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Court of Appeals (California) which reversed the superior court's dismissal of her indictment for elder abuse under Cal. Penal Code 386(a). The state also sought review on the ground that the court of appeals improperly construed the statute and impermissibly added elements to the crime of elder abuse.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating Cal. Penal Code § 368(a) for abuse of her elderly father. The magistrate determined that, although defendant did not have custody of her father as did her brothers, there was probable cause to believe she owed a duty of care to her father and that she had been grossly negligent in failing to carry out that duty. Defendant challenged the charge on grounds that the statute was unconstitutionally vague because it failed to adequately define the class of persons having a legal duty to protect elders from abuse. The trial court agreed and dismissed the case. The state appealed and the Court of Appeals reversed, holding that defendant was under a duty to repel a threat to her father's well being. Defendant appealed and the supreme court reversed. The court held that criminal liability under the statute was properly based, not on the relationship between the defendant and her father, but rather between defendant and the alleged abusers, her brothers. Because there was no evidence that defendant had a legal duty to control the conduct of either of her brothers, the trial court order of dismissal was proper.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals and held that the trial court properly dismissed the indictment against defendant for elder abuse. The court held that the statute could not impose criminal liability on defendant because she had no legal duty to control her brothers who were the caretakers and alleged abusers.

Domar Electric, Inc. v. City of Los Angeles


No. S036526.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


9 Cal. 4th 161; 885 P.2d 934; 36 Cal. Rptr. 2d 521; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 6581; 94 Daily Journal DAR 18217; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9802



December 28, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BS020805, Robert H. O'Brien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed and the matter is remanded to allow that court to address whether Domar may otherwise be entitled to relief based on the alternative grounds identified in its notice of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city and intervenor controls company petitioned for review of a judgment from the Court of Appeal (California), which reversed a trial court judgment against plaintiff electric company in plaintiff's action seeking a mandate to prevent defendant city from entering a computer control system contract with any company other than plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff electric company submitted the lowest bid on a contract to provide a computer control system for defendant city. Defendant declared the bid nonresponsive for failure to timely provide the required good faith effort documentation related to a minority and women-owned business outreach program. Instead, the contract was awarded to intervenor controls company, which submitted the next lowest bid. The trial court denied plaintiff's request for a writ of mandate preventing defendant from entering the contract with intervenor. The court of appeal reversed the judgment on the ground that defendant's rejection violated the city charter requirement that contracts be awarded to the lowest bidder. The supreme court reversed the court of appeal's judgment, determining that the outreach program was not void in the absence of explicit charter authorization. Further, the supreme court held that there was no conflict between the outreach program and the purposes of competitive bidding. Therefore, the program was not void under the charter. The supreme court remanded to the court of appeal for consideration of plaintiff's alternative grounds for relief.


OUTCOME: The court reversed judgment which held that defendant city's rejection of plaintiff electric company's bid violated city charter requirement that contracts be awarded to lowest bidder. The court held that the charter requirement did not prevent defendant from requiring potential contractors to comply with a subcontractor outreach program that involved no bid preferences. The court remanded for consideration of alternative grounds for relief.

People v. Crittenden


No. S010685.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


9 Cal. 4th 83; 885 P.2d 887; 36 Cal. Rptr. 2d 474; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 6570; 94 Daily Journal DAR 18013; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9702



December 22, 1994, Decided

144 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Dibb v. County of San Diego


No. S035914.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


8 Cal. 4th 1200; 884 P.2d 1003; 36 Cal. Rptr. 2d 55; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 6214; 94 Daily Journal DAR 17455; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9438



December 12, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 633058, Mack P. Lovett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As we observed in Reuter, supra, 220 Cal. 314, in which we construed the predecessor to article XI, sections 3(a) and 4(e) of the Constitution, "[t]he general purpose [of the provision] was to give local self-government or county home rule to counties of the state by means of charters .... [P] The people of the state in the adoption of this amendment had good cause to believe, and evidently did believe, that they were thereby providing a means whereby they might have home rule in their local and county affairs, including the right, in the words of the amendment, to provide for the powers and duties of their county officers. The amendment as adopted by them, when construed as a whole, is not only susceptible of such a construction, but cannot be given any other reasonable interpretation." (220 Cal. at pp. 326, 327.) We agree with the Younger court's similar observation that "home rule," as that term was used in Reuter, supra, 220 Cal. 314, and as that concept is reflected in article XI, sections 3(a) and 4(e) of our state Constitution, contemplates the right of the people of a charter county to create their own local government and define its powers within the limits set out by the Constitution. (Younger, supra, 93 Cal.App.3d at p. 869.) Construing the constitutional provisions in light of that purpose, we conclude the charter county of San Diego has constitutional authority under article XI, sections 3(a) and 4(e) of the Constitution to confer the power to issue subpoenas on the CLERB. Accordingly, we affirm the judgment of the Court of Appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizen sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that held that respondent county had the authority under Cal. Const. art. XI, §§ 3 and 4 to create a citizens review board to review public complaints about the county sheriff and probation departments, and vest that board with power to subpoena witnesses and documents.


OVERVIEW: Respondent county amended its charter to provide for the creation of a citizens board (board) to review public complaints about the county sheriff and probation departments, and vest that board with power to subpoena witnesses and documents. Appellant citizen filed a taxpayer's suit under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 526a to enjoin respondent from spending funds to implement the board, arguing that there was no legal authority for the creation of the board and that the board was not legally authorized to issue subpoenas. The trial court rejected both arguments, and appellant sought review. The court held that respondent had the authority to create the board under Cal. Gov't Code § 3100.1 and that creation of the board was a proper exercise of charter county authority under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 4(h). The court also held that under the concept of home rule as reflected in Cal. Const. art. XI, §§ 3(a) and 4(e), the people of a charter county had the power to create their own local government and define its powers within the limits set out by the constitution, including the power to issue subpoenas. The court, therefore, affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court that held that respondent county had the right to create a citizens review board and to vest that board with power to subpoena witnesses and documents. The court held that the creation of the board was proper under the state constitution provisions regarding charter counties.

Conservatorship of Susan T.


No. S035032.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


8 Cal. 4th 1005; 884 P.2d 988; 36 Cal. Rptr. 2d 40; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 6211; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9381; 63 U.S.L.W. 2392; 94 Daily Journal DAR 17330



December 8, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Lake County, No. 8808, Robert L. Crone, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Although the Court of Appeal concluded the exclusionary rule applied to conservatorship proceedings under the act, it found the introduction of the illegally seized evidence had no adverse impact on the jury's verdict and affirmed the judgment of the trial court finding Susan T. to be gravely disabled within the meaning of the act. We conclude the evidence was properly admitted and, on that basis, affirm the judgment of the Court of Appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the holding of the Court of Appeals (California), that although photos of appellant's apartment, taken without a warrant, should have been excluded from evidence in a conservatorship proceeding under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5000 et seq., the jury would have found appellant gravely disabled on the basis of other, properly obtained evidence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was removed from her apartment by a crisis worker from respondent county mental health department, in response to a physician's report that she constituted a danger to herself and others due to her schizophrenia. In a conservatorship proceeding under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5000 et seq., the trial court admitted photos of appellant's apartment taken by a mental health worker, and found appellant gravely disabled. The court of appeals held that the exclusionary rule applied to conservatorship proceedings, but that the illegally seized evidence had no adverse impact on the jury, because it would have found her gravely disabled on the basis of other properly obtained evidence. Appellant sought review, and the court affirmed, but on the grounds that the evidence was properly admitted. The court reasoned that although generally the exclusionary rule had been held applicable to only criminal and quasi-criminal proceedings, the social costs of applying the rule to conservatorship proceedings outweighed the rule's deterrent effect, and that the official conduct associated with the search was not so egregious as to offend the traditions and collective conscience.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, but found that the evidence was properly admitted. The court held that the exclusionary ruled did not apply to conservatorship proceedings because, under a balancing test, the social costs of excluding illegally obtained evidence outweighed the rule's deterrent effect upon mental health workers.

Griset v. Fair Political Practices Com


No. S029701.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


8 Cal. 4th 851; 884 P.2d 116; 35 Cal. Rptr. 2d 659; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 6031; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9009; 94 Daily Journal DAR 16731



November 28, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Cert. Denied April 24, 1995, 63 U.S.L. Week 3771.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 638654, Robert A. Knox, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, candidate and committees, challenged the decision from the Superior Court of Orange County (California) which denied their writ of mandamus petition and found Cal. Gov't Code § 83405 constitutional, which required that candidates for public office and their supporting groups identify themselves on any mass mailings they sent to prospective voters.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, candidate for city council and two committees he controlled, sent prospective voters mass mailings that did not contain the identifying information required by Cal. Gov't Code § 84305. The Fair Political Practices Commission (FPPC) brought charges and appellants challenged the charges arguing that § 84305 was unconstitutional because those who sent out mass mailings designed to influence the outcome of an election are entitled, under U.S. Const. amend. The trial court denied appellants initial writ of mandamus and also denied their motions for declaratory and injunctive relief as well as their motion for summary adjudication. Appellants challenged only the denial of the writ. The court held that, because the statute did not impair appellants ability to engage in the desired activity, and because the state had a compelling interest in assisting voters in making reasoned choices at the polls, and because the statute was narrowly tailored in its requirements to serve that interest, the statute did not violate any constitutional rights. The court therefore affirmed the lower court's ruling upholding the statute.


OUTCOME: The judgment that the statute requiring candidates for public office, and individuals or groups supporting a candidate or ballot measure, to identify themselves on any mass mailings is constitutional was affirmed because it was narrowly tailored to serve a compelling state interest in assisting voters in making a reasoned choice at the polls.

Voters for Responsible Retirement v. Board of Supervisors


No. S034268.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


8 Cal. 4th 765; 884 P.2d 645; 35 Cal. Rptr. 2d 814; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 6029; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8944; 94 Daily Journal DAR 16564



November 23, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Trinity County, No. 92CV021, Steven E. Jahr, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Shasta Superior Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: We therefore conclude that Ordinance No. 1161 was not subject to referendum. The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, with directions to affirm the judgment of the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent county sought review of judgment from the Superior Court of Trinity County (California) concluding that respondent county's decision to enter into an agreement enabling its employees to join a retirement plan was subject to approval or rejection by local referendum pursuant to Cal. Const. art. XI, § 1(b).


OVERVIEW: Respondent county sought review of judgment from the lower court holding that Cal. Const. art. XI, § 1(b) dictated that respondent county's decision to enter an agreement enabling its employees to join a retirement plan was subject to approval or rejection by local referendum. The court reversed and held that Cal. Const. art. XI, § 1(b) neither restricted nor secured the local right of referendum on employee compensation decisions, but Cal. Gov't Code § 25123(e) restricted the right to a referendum because the ordinance specifically related to the implementation of a memorandum of understanding (MOU) between respondent and its employee associations. Ordinances specifically related to the adoption of a MOU with employee organizations are ordinances required by law to take effect immediately under Cal. Elec. Code § 3751(a)(2), and therefore, are exempt from the referendum procedures applicable to county ordinances pursuant to §§ 3753 and 3754. Moreover, Cal. Gov't Code § 25123 (e) did not unconstitutionally deprive county voters of the right to repeal such ordinances by referendum because the referendum restriction advanced legislative goals that were a matter of statewide concern.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and held the ordinance enabling county employees to join a retirement plan was not subject to referendum. The ordinance specifically related to the adoption of a memorandum of understanding between respondent and its employee associations and it was required to take effect immediately. The court also held that voters were not unconstitutionally deprived of their right to repeal such ordinances by referendum.

 

149 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Bransford


No.S033486.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


8 Cal. 4th 885; 884 P.2d 70; 35 Cal. Rptr. 2d 613; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 5884; 94 Daily Journal DAR 16557; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8939



November 23, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied January 19, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 236.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court of Imperial County, Nos. MV-40329-B and MVI-39979-B, Joseph Zimmerman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed judgments from the Court of Appeal (California) that affirmed their convictions for driving with 0.08 percent or more of alcohol in their blood, in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(b). Defendants complained principally of rulings excluding evidence of their personal breath-alcohol concentration to blood-alcohol concentration, or partition, ratios.


OVERVIEW: Holding that partition ratios were irrelevant to a conviction based on breath-alcohol levels under Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(b), the court affirmed the convictions. The court explained that § 23152(b) criminalized the act of driving either with the specified blood-alcohol level or with the specified breath-alcohol level. This differed from the predecessor statute which criminalized driving with a specified blood-alcohol level only. Under the predecessor statute, breath-alcohol levels were converted to a blood-alcohol level based on a standard partition ratio set forth in Cal. Code Regs. tit. 17, § 1220.4(f). Personal partition levels were relevant under that scheme to impeach the standard conversion for an accused's breath-alcohol level; however, no more, as § 23152(b) defined a new crime, unrelated to blood-alcohol level, and not dependent on conversion. All that mattered was whether an accused's breath-alcohol level was 0.08 or greater. As such, § 23152(b) also did not unconstitutionally, conclusively presume either blood-alcohol level or intoxication, and did not exclude relevant evidence in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(d).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions for driving with 0.08 percent or more of alcohol in their blood. The court held that defendants' proffered personal partition ratio evidence was irrelevant to a determination of how much alcohol was in their breath, which was what the statute they were convicted under criminalized.

 

150 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Freeman


No. S004787.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


8 Cal. 4th 450; 882 P.2d 249; 34 Cal. Rptr. 2d 558; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 5392; 94 Daily Journal DAR 14941; 31 A.L.R.5th 888



October 24, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:
Rehearing denied by People v. Freeman, 1995 Cal. LEXIS 124 (Cal., Jan. 5, 1995)
Stay granted by People v. Freeman, 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2431 (Cal., Apr. 12, 1995)
Writ of certiorari denied Freeman v. California, 515 U.S. 1149, 132 L. Ed. 2d 839, 115 S. Ct. 2592, 1995 U.S. LEXIS 4194 (1995)
Request granted In re Freeman, 2004 Cal. LEXIS 6930 (Cal., July 28, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 26421, Stanley P. Golde, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant automatically appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) which denied his automatic motion to modify the verdict and sentenced defendant to death. A jury had convicted defendant of murder, five counts of robbery, and three counts of attempted robbery and had also found that the murder was committed in the commission of a robbery. After the penalty trial, the jury had imposed the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: After a jury convicted defendant of murder committed in the commission of a robbery and imposed the death penalty, defendant appealed. In affirming, the court found that the record did not support defendant's contention that he was functionally deaf and that such impairment compromised his right to have been personally present at trial. The court also held that it was not error to deny defendant's petition for substitution of counsel because defendant failed to state sufficient grounds for substitution. The court rejected defendant's argument that he was prohibited from receiving a fair trial by the disclosure of his prior convictions for armed robbery. The court held that defendant's claim of prosecutorial misconduct in disclosing the prior convictions was waived because defendant's counsel failed to object to such disclosure. Moreover, defense counsel's disclosure of defendant's prior convictions, prior to prosecution's reference to them, was a tactical decision that counsel had to be allowed to make without fear of appellate second-guessing. Additionally, an accomplice's grant of immunity was not reason to doubt that accomplice's competency to testify at trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant of murder committed during the commission of a robbery and imposing capital punishment because defendant failed to demonstrate that he was deprived of his constitutional right to have been present at trial, that he had ineffective assistance of counsel, that prosecution engaged in misconduct by referring to his prior convictions, and that an accomplice was incompetent to testify.

 

151 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Jasmon O.


No. S030556.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


8 Cal. 4th 398; 878 P.2d 1297; 33 Cal. Rptr. 2d 85; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 4474; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6849; 94 Daily Journal DAR 12518



September 2, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. J147148 and A37229, Bonnie Dumanis, Referee, and Sheridan E. Reed, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal in D014729 is reversed. The order of the Court of Appeal in D015741 dismissing the appeal from the judgment in the proceeding pursuant to former Civil Code section 232 is reversed. The appeal is reinstated and transferred to this court, and the judgment of the superior court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, the San Diego County Department of Social Services, sought review of the decision of the court of appeal (California), which reversed the trial court's judgment that granted a petition filed by respondent to terminate appellant father's parental rights upon making a de novo determination that it would be detrimental to return the child to appellant after being in foster care for seven years.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, the county department of social services, placed appellant father's child in a foster home. Appellant sought to regain custody. After the third permanent placement hearing, a referee found that it would not be in the child's best interest to be placed with appellant. The referee found that the child had established no bond with appellant and had developed a serious mental illness based on separation anxiety from leaving the foster home. Respondent instituted proceedings to terminate appellant's parental rights pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 332. The trial court determined it would be detrimental to return the child to appellant and terminated his parental rights. The court of appeal reversed the trial court's decision. On further appeal, respondent contended that the termination proceeding was separate from the dependency proceeding and that the trial court's order was supported by substantial evidence. The state supreme court accepted respondent's contentions and reversed, holding that the courts may consider the damaging effects caused by severing a long-term bond with foster parents on a petition for termination of parental rights.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed the court of appeal's decision, which dismissed the appeal from an order terminating appellant father's parental rights. The supreme court affirmed the ruling of the trial court, which granted respondent department's petition to terminate appellant's parental rights, ruling that termination of parental rights was the least detrimental alternative for the child.

 

152 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



20th Century Ins. Co. v. Garamendi


No. S032502.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


8 Cal. 4th 216; 878 P.2d 566; 32 Cal. Rptr. 2d 807; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 4269; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6367; 94 Daily Journal DAR 11530



August 18, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified on Denial of Rehearing September 29, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. BC046216, BS016789, and BC023983, Dzintra I. Janavs, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons stated above, we conclude that we must reverse the judgment of the superior court and direct that court to render judgment in favor of the Insurance Commissioner and Voter Revolt and against 20th Century, Hartford, State Farm, et al.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In three consolidated cases, respondent insurance companies and appellants, commissioners and voter organization, brought appeals and cross-appeals challenging the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment in favor of respondents in the action by respondents to review the implementation of Proposition 103's rate rollback requirement provision.


OVERVIEW: Appeals and cross-appeals were brought by respondent insurance companies and appellants, commissioners and voter organization, after the superior court's judgment, in favor of respondent, in the action to review the implementation of an initiative statute that was designated on the ballot as Proposition 103. The court reversed the judgment of the superior court and directed the court to render judgment in favor of appellants, commissioner and voter organization. The court held that the rate regulations, both generally and specifically as to rollbacks, came within the ratesetting exception, and fell outside the Office of Administrative Law (OAL) review requirement, and therefore were not invalid because of OAL disapproval. The court proceeded to the merits, holding that the OAL was incorrect in concluding that the rate regulations generally were beyond the authority conferred on appellant commissioners and were inconsistent with the initiative's terms on the grounds that they impermissibly restrict an insurer's right to obtain relief from confiscatory rates. The court held that the inability to operate successfully was a necessary, but not a sufficient, condition of confiscation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the previous judgment and directed the court to render judgment in favor of appellants, commissioner and voter organization, against respondent insurance companies, because the rate regulations came within the ratesetting exception, the rate regulations were not beyond the authority conferred on appellant commissioners, and regulations did not impermissibly restrict respondent's right to obtain relief from confiscatory rates.

154 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Western Steamship Lines, Inc. v. San Pedro Peninsula Hospital


No. S033710.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


8 Cal. 4th 100; 876 P.2d 1062; 32 Cal. Rptr. 2d 263; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 3898; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5854; 94 Daily Journal DAR 10626



July 28, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified September 22, 1994. Rehearing Denied September 22, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 5183.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SOC 79732, Charles E. Frisco, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed. The matter is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant hospital sought review of a judgment entered by a California court of appeals, which held that the Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 3333.2, did not apply to plaintiff cruise line's claim against defendant for equitable indemnification and apportionment of the damages awarded to plaintiff's employee's guardian in an underlying personal injury action.


OVERVIEW: An employee of plaintiff cruise line fell ill while working aboard one of plaintiff's ships. When the ship docked, the employee was rushed to defendant hospital. She suffered a cardiac arrest and never regained consciousness. The employee's legal guardian filed suit against plaintiff in Florida. The jury found in favor of the employee. After plaintiff settled with the guardian for $ 6 million, plaintiff filed suit seeking equitable indemnification from defendant. The jury found that plaintiff, defendant, and the treating doctor were negligent and fixed the relative fault of defendant at 30 percent. The trial court concluded that the $ 250,000 non-economic damage cap of the Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act (MICRA), Cal. Civ. Code § 3333.2, was inapplicable to plaintiff's claim and entered judgment against defendant for $ 1.8 million. The appellate court affirmed. The supreme court reversed, holding that in furtherance of MICRA's goal of reducing medical malpractice insurance costs, § 3333.2 was applicable in an indemnification action brought by a concurrent tortfeasor and that plaintiff thus had no right to indemnification beyond $ 250,000 for non-economic damages.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment by the appellate court, which determined that the non-economic damage cap provision of the Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act (MICRA) was inapplicable to plaintiff cruise line's indemnification claim against defendant hospital, because application of the cap furthered MICRA's goal of reducing medical malpractice insurance costs. The court remanded the matter for further proceedings.

 

155 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Tyrell J.


No. S030266.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


8 Cal. 4th 68; 876 P.2d 519; 32 Cal. Rptr. 2d 33; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 3897; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5846; 94 Daily Journal DAR 10633



July 28, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified October 20, 1994. Rehearing Denied October 20, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 5597.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 69805, Glenda S. Allen-Hill, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: It thus appears the juvenile court correctly denied the minor's suppression motion, and that the Court of Appeal erred by reversing that decision. Accordingly, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent state sought review of the Court of Appeals (California), which reversed a juvenile court's denial of appellant minor's motion to suppress evidence in a proceeding in which appellant was charged with possession of marijuana for the purpose of sale pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 and Cal. Health & Saf. Code § 11359.


OVERVIEW: Appellant minor was placed on probation after he committed a battery. As a condition of his probation, appellant was required to submit to a search of his person and property, with or without a warrant, by any police officer. Following a gang-related incident, an officer searched appellant and found a bag of marijuana in his pants. Appellant was arrested and charged with possession of marijuana for the purpose of sale pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 and Cal. Health and Saf. Code § 11359. The juvenile court denied appellant's motion to suppress. The court of appeals reversed, concluding that the officer did not have probable cause to conduct the search and that the probation condition did not validate the search because the officer was unaware of it. On appeal, the court reversed, concluding that the search was reasonable under U.S. Const. amend IV because appellant had a reduced expectation of privacy in light of the probation condition. Additionally, the search furthered the juvenile probation system's goals of rehabilitating youngsters and deterring further antisocial conduct. The court found that the officer's lack of knowledge of the condition was irrelevant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment entered by the appellate court, which reversed a juvenile court's denial of appellant minor's motion to suppress marijuana seized in a warrantless search. The search was reasonable in light of the fact that appellant, due to a probation condition, had a reduced expectation of privacy in his person and possessions.

Hensler v. City of Glendale


No. S032210.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


8 Cal. 4th 1; 876 P.2d 1043; 32 Cal. Rptr. 2d 244; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 3785; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5721; 94 Daily Journal DAR 10398



July 25, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified September 22, 1994. Rehearing Denied September 22, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 5182.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NCC41335, Joseph R. Kalin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner sought review of the Court of Appeal (California), which affirmed the lower court's judgment of dismissal, holding that plaintiff's inverse condemnation action against defendant city was barred under the 90-day statute of limitation imposed by Cal. Gov't Code § 66499.37.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city adopted an ordinance prohibiting construction on major ridge lines. Plaintiff landowner claimed that the ordinance precluded development of 40 percent of his 300-acre tract and brought an inverse condemnation action three years after the city rejected his proposed development on the ridge lines. The appellate court dismissed the action as barred by the statute of limitations. The court sought to establish the statute of limitations applicable to a complaint in inverse condemnation alleging a taking of real property resulting from the adoption, or application to plaintiff's property, of an ordinance enacted pursuant to the Subdivision Map Act, Cal. Gov't Code §66410 et seq. The court concluded that an action in inverse condemnation, whether or not joined with an action in administrative mandamus, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §1094.5, challenging the ordinance or its application to plaintiff's property, was governed by Cal. Gov't Code § 66499.37 unless it alleged the existence of a final judgment establishing that there had been a compensable taking of plaintiff's land. The court affirmed the dismissal of the complaint pursuant to the § 66499.37 90-day statute of limitations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of plaintiff landowner's claim because the complaint was brought more than three years after an adverse decision from defendant city and it did not allege a taking that had been judicially established, therefore the claim was barred by the 90-day statute of limitations.

People v. Fudge


No. S004790, Crim. No. 26424


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


7 Cal. 4th 1075; 875 P.2d 36; 31 Cal. Rptr. 2d 321; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 3316; 94 Daily Journal DAR 9375; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5121



June 30, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A759252, Ronald E. Cappai, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was assessed the death penalty upon his first degree murder conviction, Cal. Penal Code §187, with enhancements for the personal use of a firearm in the commission of the murders, Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5, and multiple-murder special-circumstances. Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3). Appeal was automatic from the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1239(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of five counts of first degree murder in a second trial and received a sentence of death. Defendant received an automatic appeal. In affirming the conviction and punishment, the court concluded the trial court committed no reversible error in juror challenges and the replacement of a juror. Although the trial court erred by refusing to give at least a revised version of defendant's proposed instruction on eyewitness identification, the error was harmless because the overall strength of the evidence of guilt, the detail in defense counsel's closing argument, and other jury instructions were relevant to eyewitness identification. A favorable expert's testimony was relevant and admissible mitigating evidence. However, the court held that the trial court's exclusion of the testimony was harmless error because the strength of the evidence clearly outweighed the mitigating evidence. The court further held that the trial court's decision qualifying a detective as an expert was not a manifest abuse of discretion and would have assisted the jury in understanding photographic evidence of the crime scene. Polygraph evidence was deemed to have been properly excluded.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and death penalty sentences in their entirety because it found no reversible error by the lower courts, and the strength of the evidence was sufficient to support the convictions. The court rejected several arguments set forth by defendant as harmless error, including exclusion of expert testimony regarding mitigating evidence, and exclusion of a proposed jury instruction on eyewitness identification.

People v. Kirkpatrick


No. S004642.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


7 Cal. 4th 988; 874 P.2d 248; 30 Cal. Rptr. 2d 818; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 2989; 94 Daily Journal DAR 8163; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4419



June 13, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified August 11, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A590144, Coleman A. Swart, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sentenced him to death, pursuant to Cal. Pen. Code § 190.1, et seq., after convicting defendant of two counts of first degree murder, two counts of robbery, and two counts of burglary. After finding special circumstances, pursuant to Cal. Pen. Code § 190.2, the jury imposed the death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed Taco Bell employees in the course of a robbery. Defendant was convicted of two counts of first degree murder, two counts of robbery, and two counts of burglary, and sentenced to death, pursuant to Cal. Pen. Code § 190.2, upon the finding of special circumstances that defendant had been convicted of more than one offense of murder in the proceeding, that the murders were committed in the perpetration of robbery and burglary, and that defendant used a firearm to commit the offenses. The court held that, although defense counsel interfered with defendant's attempted exercise of his right to self-representation, defendant's counsel was not ineffective. The court held that the prosecutor's argument was within the boundaries of permissible victim impact argument. The court held that the jury instructions could not have led a reasonable juror to misapprehend the penalty determination. The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which convicted defendant of two counts of first degree murder, two counts of robbery, and two counts of burglary, and sentenced him to death. The court held that the lower court had not abused its discretion in denying defendant's motion for self-representation because it was not made within a reasonable amount of time before the guilt phase and that defendant's counsel was not ineffective.

People v. Hobbs


No. S023739.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


7 Cal. 4th 948; 873 P.2d 1246; 30 Cal. Rptr. 2d 651; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 2790; 94 Daily Journal DAR 7697; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4174



June 6, 1994, Decided  
June 6, 1994


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Yuba County, No. 6678, Dennis J. Buckley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Superior Court of Yuba County (California), which convicted defendant of having under her control, and knowingly making available, room or space to store or distribute methamphetamine.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed the decision of the lower appellate court. Defendant pled nolo contendre to and was convicted for facilitating the storage or manufacturing of methamphetamine. A search warrant was issued based on, among other evidence, a sealed document containing testimony of an informant. The lower appellate court reversed the conviction, holding that defendant's due process right to reasonable access to information to challenge the warrant had been violated. On appeal, the court held that the nolo contendre plea did not preclude review and that, pursuant to Calif. Evid. Code §§ 1041 and 1042 and common law, the informant's information was properly sealed and kept confidential to assert the informant's privilege. The court held that defendant's motion should be denied based on the fact that it was not reasonably probable defendant could have prevailed on her motions to quash the search warrant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower appellate court's decision on the grounds that the trial court properly sealed the information supporting the issuance of a search warrant, and that defendant could not have quashed the search warrant.

 

160 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Planned Parenthood Shasta-Diablo, Inc. v. Williams


No. S031721.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


7 Cal. 4th 860; 873 P.2d 1224; 30 Cal. Rptr. 2d 629; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 2472; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3798; 94 Daily Journal DAR 7073



May 26, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, No. 110090, Dennis W. Bunting, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant anti-abortion picketers challenged the injunction as modified by the Court of Appeals (California), the provisions of which effectively barred appellants from the public sidewalk in front of respondent clinic by requiring that all picketing, demonstrating or counseling take place on the public sidewalk directly across the street. Appellants argued that the place restriction violated their First Amendment rights.


OVERVIEW: Respondent clinic obtained an injunction which restricted appellant anti-abortion picketers' demonstrating and counseling, to the public sidewalk across the street from the clinic. The court found that the public sidewalk directly in front of the clinic from which appellants were barred constituted a public forum. The court held that the place restriction was content neutral as the injunction made no reference to the content of appellants' speech. No speech of any particular content was singled out for better or worse treatment by the government. The court held that the place restriction served a substantial governmental interest in protecting the patients' physical and emotional health and safety, and that it was narrowly tailored to effectuate that government interest. The court ruled that U.S. Const. amend I did not give appellants the right to a captive audience, but simply the opportunity to win the attention of passersby and engage them in conversation if the latter so desired. The court affirmed the appellate court and found that the injunction was not broader than necessary to achieve the state's interest, and left appellants ample alternative avenues of communication.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the place restriction imposed by the injunction served a significant governmental interest in safeguarding the health and safety of respondent clinic's patients, and was narrowly tailored to further that interest. The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court and concluded that the restriction allowed appellant anti-abortion picketers adequate alternative means of conveying their anti-abortion message.

 

161 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Davis


No. S033327.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


7 Cal. 4th 797; 872 P.2d 591; 30 Cal. Rptr. 2d 50; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 2195; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3517; 94 Daily Journal DAR 6630



May 16, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR-122543, Michael D. Wellington, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged a decision of the Court of Appeals (California) that reversed defendant's conviction for murder of a fetus during the course of a robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187(a). Defendant had argued that viability of a fetus was an element of fetal murder under the statute and that the trial court prejudicially erred by not instructing the jury pursuant to Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No. 8.10.


OVERVIEW: Following a jury trial, defendant was convicted of murder of a fetus during the course of a robbery, assault with a firearm, and robbery. Defendant contended that the trial court erred by not instructing the jury pursuant to Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No. 8.10. The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court which held that fetal viability was not a required element of murder in Cal. Penal Code § 187(a), and reversed defendant's murder conviction and set aside the special circumstance finding. The court found that every prior decision that had addressed the viability issue had determined that viability of the fetus was prerequisite to a murder conviction under § 187(a), and therefore application of its unprecedented construction of the statute to defendant would violate due process and ex post facto principles. The court found that its "redefinition" of the crime amounted to a major change in the law that could not be applied to defendant. The court also found that the trial court erred when it substantially lowered the viability threshold by giving its modified version of Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No. 8.10.


OUTCOME: The court determined that viability was not an element of fetal homicide, and that the third party killing of a fetus with malice aforethought was murder as long as the fetus had progressed beyond the embryonic stage of seven to eight weeks. The court affirmed the appellate court and held that its decision would apply prospectively only and not to defendant, and that the trial court committed prejudicial error in instructing the jury.

 

162 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Morehart v. County of Santa Barbara


No. S030829.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


7 Cal. 4th 725; 872 P.2d 143; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 804; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 2194; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3404; 94 Daily Journal DAR 6396



May 12, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As modified May 26, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 176474, William L. Gordon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed with directions to dismiss the appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent landowners challenged the order of the Court of Appeals (California), which reversed the decision of the trial court that zoning ordinances enacted by appellants, County of Santa Barbara and its board of supervisors, requiring merger of parcels, were preempted by the Subdivision Map Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 66451.10 et seq., and therefore invalid. Respondents argued that the trial court's order was not properly appealable.


OVERVIEW: Respondent landowners challenged the denial of a coastal development permit by appellants, the County of Santa Barbara and its board of supervisors. Respondents argued that appellants' zoning ordinances were invalid as local legislation in an area preempted state law. Because the issue was of statewide importance, the court decided the case on the merits although it found that the appellate court lacked jurisdiction over the appeal. The court held that local legislation in conflict with general law was void. The court found that conflicts existed if the ordinance duplicated, contradicted, or entered an area fully occupied by general law, either expressly or by legislative implication. The court held that the Subdivision Map Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 66451.10 et seq., preempted local zoning ordinances that required, for issuance of a development permit, the merger of parcels that would not have been eligible subjects of merger under the act. The court found that the subject matter of the ordinance was partially covered by general law couched in such terms that indicated clearly that a paramount state concern would not tolerate further or additional local action.


OUTCOME: The court agreed with respondent landowners that the zoning ordinance of appellants, the County of Santa Barbara and its board of supervisors, was invalid because general state law reflected a paramount state concern that statewide uniform standards govern the use of compulsory merger as a means of controlling development. The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court, and directed it to dismiss the appeal.

 

163 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Machupa


No. S032885.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


7 Cal. 4th 614; 872 P.2d 114; 29 Cal. Rptr. 2d 775; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1822; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2907; 94 Daily Journal DAR 5504



April 25, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 912915-6, Richard E. Arnason, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeal (California), which reversed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to suppress evidence seized under a search warrant, on the grounds that defendant did not consent to police officers' previous warrantless entry into his home upon which they based their warrant application.


OVERVIEW: While investigating a shooting, police officers were approached by defendant, who stated in response to questions that he owned two guns, which the officers asked to see. The officers stated that they would have to go with defendant because of the shooting. Defendant entered his house followed by the officers, who saw what appeared to be marijuana. The officers asked to look through the rest of the house, they found marijuana, and arrested defendant. That evening, the officers obtained a search warrant for the house and seized evidence. The trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress, which the intermediate appellate court reversed on the grounds that defendant did not consent to the officers' initial warrantless entry. On appeal, the court affirmed that reversal, holding that the "good faith" exception did not apply. That exception, which permits the admission of evidence seized under a warrant later determined to be unsupported by probable cause if the officers acted in reasonable reliance upon the warrant, did not apply where the initial violation of U.S. Const. amend. IV was the product of police misconduct rather than magisterial misconduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment which reversed the denial of defendant's motion to suppress evidence seized under a search warrant, because the "good faith" exception allowing admission of evidence seized under a warrant later found to be defective if police officers reasonably relied upon that warrant did not apply where officers initially made a warrantless, nonconsensual entry into defendant's home.

 

164 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Provigo Corp. v. Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Bd.


No. S031492.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


7 Cal. 4th 561; 869 P.2d 1163; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 638; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1391; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2483; 94 Daily Journal DAR 4663



April 7, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Ct. of App. Nos. A058137 and A058534.



DISPOSITION: We conclude the Court of Appeal erred in reversing the Board's decision to uphold petitioners' suspensions. Accordingly, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, California Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board, sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeal (California), which reversed respondent's decision to uphold petitioner grocery stores' suspension of licenses for selling alcohol to minors in violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25658.


OVERVIEW: The California Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control (department) suspended petitioner grocery stores' licenses for unlawfully selling alcohol to minors in violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25658. The minors were working as part of a decoy program with police departments. Respondent, California Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board, upheld the department's decision and the court of appeal reversed. On subsequent appeal, the court reversed, holding that Cal. Const. art. XX, § 22, which prohibited persons under 21 from purchasing alcohol, did not afford a defense to a charge of petitioners' unlawful sales in situations involving underage decoys. The court found that police involvement in criminal activity for the purpose of investigating possible violations was permissible even if technical violations occurred, such as the underage decoys purchasing alcohol. The use of decoys to expose illicit activity did not constitute entrapment because there was no pressure employed by the decoys to get petitioners to sell the alcohol. A due process violation had not occurred because the mere use of underage decoys to enforce liquor laws did not constitute outrageous conduct.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision which reversed respondent, California Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board's, decision to uphold petitioner grocery stores' suspension of licenses for selling alcohol to minors. The court held that the state constitutional provision forbidding purchases of alcoholic beverages by minors did not afforded a defense to the charge of petitioner's unlawful sales in situations involving underage decoys.

 

165 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Waste Management of the Desert v. Palm Springs Recycling Center, Inc.


No. S029150.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


7 Cal. 4th 478; 869 P.2d 440; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 461; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1217; 94 Daily Journal DAR 4255; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2306



March 31, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. I-64239, Ross G. Tharp, Judge. * 


* Retired judge of the San Diego Superior Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed with one modification.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff waste company challenged a judgment from the appellate court (California), which reversed the trial court's grant of injunctive relief to plaintiff, in plaintiff's action regarding defendant recycling company's ordinance and agreement violations relating to the collection of recyclable materials.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff waste company entered into an agreement regarding the collection and disposal of waste within a city's limits. The agreement also contained a provision granting plaintiff the exclusive right to collect all recyclable materials. Defendant recycling company sent trucks into the city to collect recyclable materials, plaintiff and city demanded defendant's cessation, and sought a preliminary and permanent injunction prohibiting defendant from collecting recycling materials within the city. Defendant contended the city exceeded its police power by enacting an ordinance regarding the removal of waste and improperly entered into an agreement with plaintiff. The trial court granted injunctive relief and the appellate court reversed pursuant to the California Integrated Waste Management Act of 1989 (the Act) Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 40059(a)(2). The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment with one modification. The court held that the Act did not allow an exclusive franchise for the collection of recyclable not discarded by their owner. The court reasoned the legislature did not intend for the Act to cover items that were not discarded by their owners.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment reversing the trial court's injunctive relief to plaintiff waste company, in plaintiff's action regarding defendant recycling company's ordinance and agreement violations relating to the collection of recyclable materials. The court held that defendant could collect recyclable materials, so long as they were not discarded by their owners and, therefore, did not constitute waste.

 

166 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Santa Clara County Counsel Attys. Assn. v. Woodside


No. S031593.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


7 Cal. 4th 525; 869 P.2d 1142; 28 Cal. Rptr. 2d 617; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1215; 94 Daily Journal DAR 4272; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2297; 145 L.R.R.M. 2981



March 31, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 697174, Martin C. Suits, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Justice Court for the Avenal Judicial District sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney association challenged an order from the Court of Appeals, Santa Clara County (California), which reversed a decision of the trial court, favoring appellants, in their action against respondent county for declaratory and injunctive relief arising out of respondent's failure to meet and confer on wages pursuant to the Meyers-Milias-Brown Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 3500.


OVERVIEW: Appellant attorney association filed an action in the trial court for declaratory and injunctive relief. Appellants' alleged that respondent county failed to meet and confer on wages, as required by the Meyers-Milias-Brown Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 3500 et seq. ("MMBA"). Respondent denied that MMBA extended to attorneys employed in the office of the county counsel, and that the duty of loyalty imposed upon the attorneys towards respondent, as their client, precluded such a suit. The trial court found for appellants and enjoined respondent from terminating appellants for filing the action. The trial court held that members of appellant did not have to resign in order to file the suit. The appellate court reversed and appellants challenged the decision. The supreme court reversed, holding that the MMBA authorized the suit. The court held that the MMBA created an exception to the general rule that a client could discharge an attorney at will. Respondent was statutorily forbidden from discharging attorneys for exercising their rights under MMBA, although respondent was free to rearrange assignments of appellants within its office, as long as it was done in a nonpunitive way.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's reversal of the trial court decision favoring appellant attorney association in its action for declaratory and injunctive relief. The court held that appellants were not precluded from bringing an action under state statute against respondent county for its failure to meet and confer on wages. Respondent was properly enjoined from terminating appellants but could issue non-punitive reassignments.

 

167 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Colantuono


No. S029545.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


7 Cal. 4th 206; 865 P.2d 704; 26 Cal. Rptr. 2d 908; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 12; 94 Daily Journal DAR 1205; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 755



January 31, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. KA006205, William J. McVittie, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Court of Appeal (California), which affirmed his conviction for assault with a deadly weapon.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of assault with a deadly weapon. He appealed and the appellate court affirmed. On further appeal, defendant contended that the trial court's supplemental instruction to the jury which accompanied Cal. Jury Instructions Criminal No. 9.02 (5th ed. 1988), improperly created a mandatory presumption as to his intent. The court affirmed, holding that the trial court's supplemental instruction did not create a constitutionally impermissible presumption nor did it warrant reversal of defendant's conviction. The court found that the trial court's instruction described conduct constituting an assault, including the element of general criminal intent and that based on the instruction, once the jury found that defendant willfully engaged in the conduct described in the instruction, they would have necessarily determined the question of intent independently of any legal presumption. The court further held that the evidence was sufficient to support the jury's conclusion and there was nothing in the record to suggest that the instruction prejudicially affected the jury's deliberation on the question of defendant's intent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court, holding that the trial court's supplemental instruction to the jury did not establish an unconstitutional presumption of defendant's intent, rather it defined the conduct constituting assault with a deadly weapon, including the element of general intent.

 

168 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Hill v. National Collegiate Athletic Assn.


No. S018180.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


7 Cal. 4th 1; 865 P.2d 633; 26 Cal. Rptr. 2d 834; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 9; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 681; 9 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 716; 94 Daily Journal DAR 1141



January 28, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 619209, Conrad Lee Rushing, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal affirming the permanent injunction against the NCAA's drug testing program is reversed. This case is remanded with instructions to direct entry of a final judgment in favor of the NCAA. The NCAA shall recover its costs.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant athletic association sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (California), which affirmed the superior court's decision to grant a permanent injunction precluding defendant from enforcing its drug testing program against plaintiff student athletes and other athletes attending intervenor university on the ground that defendant failed to establish a "compelling state interest" in support of its program.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court reversed the decision to uphold the permanent injunction against defendant athletic association and directed entry of a final judgment for defendant, determining that defendant's drug testing program did not violate plaintiff student athletes' state constitutional right to privacy. The court found that although defendant, a private organization, was properly subject to a claim for invasion of privacy, defendant was not required to justify its drug testing program through proof of a "compelling state interest" and the absence of any alternative means of accomplishing that interest. The court noted that plaintiffs had no right to participate in intercollegiate athletic competition and that plaintiffs' reasonable expectation of privacy had been diminished by the notice and consent elements of defendant's testing program. The court concluded that defendant's decision to enforce a ban on the use of drugs by means of drug testing as its information-gathering procedure had been reasonably calculated to further its legitimate interest in maintaining the integrity of intercollegiate athletic competition and in protecting plaintiffs' health and safety.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the permanent injunction against defendant athletic association and remanded the case for entry of judgment for defendant on plaintiff student athletes' state constitutional invasion of privacy claims. Defendant had not violated plaintiffs' privacy rights through its use of a drug testing program that was reasonably calculated to further defendant's legitimate interest in safeguarding intercollegiate athletic competition.

 

169 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Alfredo A. v. Superior Court


No. S024618.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


6 Cal. 4th 1212; 865 P.2d 56; 26 Cal. Rptr. 2d 623; 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1218; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 538; 94 Daily Journal DAR 932



January 24, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied March 18, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1387.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner juvenile applied for review of the judgment of the California Court of Appeal, Second Appellate District, that denied his petition for writ of habeas corpus, treated as a petition for writ of mandate, which challenged respondent juvenile court's official position that a judicial determination of probable cause was not constitutionally required within 48 hours for continued detention of a juvenile arrested without a warrant.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner juvenile was taken into custody without a warrant, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 602 and 625, on suspicion of possession of cocaine base. He petitioned for writ of habeas corpus, demanding a probable cause hearing within 48 hours of arrest to justify any continued detention, since respondent juvenile court's official position was that a 48-hour hearing was not constitutionally required. A wardship petition was filed under § 602, but petitioner was released when no detention report was prepared for the 72-hour detention hearing required by Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 632(a). Treating his petition as one for writ of mandate, the appellate court denied him relief and he appealed. The supreme court ruled that it would consider the merits, even though petitioner had been released, because the issue was capable of repetition. In affirming, the court held that, although a probable cause was required within 48 hours for adult arrestees, a 48-hour hearing for juveniles was not required by U.S. Const. amend. IV or XIV because juvenile proceedings were fundamentally different from adult criminal proceedings. The 72-hour hearing mandated by § 632(a) was sufficient.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying petitioner relief was affirmed because, inter alia, juvenile proceedings were fundamentally different from adult criminal proceedings, such that, although a probable cause hearing was required within 48 hours of a warrantless arrest of an adult, a hearing within 72 hours, as required by statute, was constitutionally permissible for juveniles.

 

170 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Traverso v. People ex rel. Dept. of Transportation


No. S029371.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


6 Cal. 4th 1152; 864 P.2d 488; 26 Cal. Rptr. 2d 217; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6428; 94 Daily Journal DAR 62; 94 Cal. Daily Op. Service 74



December 30, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 160324, Laurence K. Sawyer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal, finding section 5463 facially invalid and ordering the superior court to enter judgment in favor of Adco, is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a state department of transportation, appealed a judgment from the Court of Appeal (California), which reversed a trial court's grant of summary judgment to defendant in an action by plaintiff billboard owner challenging the constitutionality of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 5463 and defendant's actions in removing one of plaintiff's billboards. The court of appeal found the first paragraph of section 5463 was unconstitutional on its face.


OVERVIEW: After repeated conversations and correspondence between the parties, defendant, a state department of transportation, began to remove a billboard owned by plaintiff pursuant to its authority under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 5463. The statute permitted defendant, on 10 days' written notice, to revoke a permit or license and remove and destroy any off-premises billboard that failed to comply with the provisions of the California Outdoor Advertising Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 4200 et seq. Plaintiff challenged the constitutionality of section 5463 and defendant's action. The trial court granted defendant's motion for summary judgment, but a court of appeal, which found the first paragraph of section 5463 was unconstitutional on its face, reversed it. The court disagreed and reversed, finding section 5463 was valid on its face. Although the notice period was relatively short, it met the notice component of due process. The court also found an implied right to a hearing. The court concluded that although it agreed with the court of appeal that plaintiff possessed protectable property interests in its billboard and permit, nevertheless, plaintiff received all the process it was due.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeal, finding the statute facially invalid and ordering the superior court to enter judgment in favor of plaintiff was reversed because the statute was facially valid and its application to plaintiff billboard owner did not offend state and federal due process principles.

Greener v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


No. S034084.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


6 Cal. 4th 1028; 863 P.2d 784; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 539; 58 Cal. Comp. Cases 793; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6376; 93 Daily Journal DAR 16535; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9676



December 27, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. CV524400, Joe S. Gray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, claimant representatives, appealed an order of the Court of Appeal of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), that held that the trial court erred when it granted defendant Workers' Compensation Appeals Board's motion to quash service of summons and dismissed plaintiffs' action on the ground that it lacked personal and subject matter jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, claimant representatives, filed a complaint seeking a declaration that amendments to Cal. Lab. Code §§ 4903 and 5710, that terminated the power of defendant workers' compensation appeals board to make awards and allow liens for attorney fees to representatives who were not attorneys, were unconstitutional. Defendant filed a motion to quash service of summons and to dismiss, which the trial court granted. The appellate court reversed, reasoning that the action did not challenge an order of the board and did not interfere with the duties of the board because the board had no duty to act under an invalid statute. The court reversed, finding that the dispute centered around the impact of Cal. Lab. Code § 5955 and presented a question of subject matter jurisdiction. The court concluded that the legislature intended to reserve to the appellate courts jurisdiction to entertain challenges to the constitutional validity of workers' compensation law. The court held that § 5955 permitted a petition for writ of mandate in "proper cases" and limited jurisdiction over such petitions to the appellate courts where the criteria for relief under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1985 were met.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the judgment of the trial court, which granted defendant workers' compensation appeals board's dismissal, was upheld on other grounds. The court held that plaintiffs, claimant representatives, were challenging the constitutionality of workers' compensation legislation, which was a proper case for a petition for writ of mandate in the appellate courts. Therefore, the trial court did not have jurisdiction.
