Gikas v. Zolin


No. S030724.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


6 Cal. 4th 841; 863 P.2d 745; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 500; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6371; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9595; 93 Daily Journal DAR 16453



December 23, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 635699, Jeffrey T. Miller, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeal erred in holding that the DMV was precluded from relitigating the lawfulness of the arrest. The judgment of the Court of Appeal is therefore reversed. Each party shall bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent department of motor vehicles petitioned for review of a judgment from the Court of Appeal (California), which reversed a trial court judgment that upheld the suspension of appellant's driver's license following his arrest for driving while under the influence of alcohol. The court of appeal held that respondent was precluded from relitigating the lawfulness of appellant's arrest.


OVERVIEW: Appellant driver was arrested for driving while under the influence and was served with an administrative per se order of driver's license suspension. At trial, appellant's motion to suppress evidence was granted and the criminal case was dismissed. Thereafter, respondent department of motor vehicles suspended appellant's license, rejecting appellant's argument that respondent was collaterally estopped from relitigating the legality of his arrest. The trial court denied appellant's petition for writ of administrative mandamus, but the court of appeal reversed, finding that respondent was collaterally estopped from relitigating the issue. The supreme court reversed the court of appeal's judgment. The supreme court determined that under Cal. Veh. Code § 13353.2(e), respondent would be precluded from suspending appellant's license if appellant was "acquitted" of the criminal charges. However, the supreme court held that appellant was not "acquitted" within the meaning of section 13353.2(e) because the ruling in the criminal proceeding did not represent a judicial determination in appellant's favor of one or more of the factual elements of section 13353.2(a).


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment which held that respondent department of motor vehicles was precluded from relitigating the lawfulness of appellant driver's arrest at a hearing to suspend appellant's driver's license. The court of appeal erred in holding that respondent was precluded from relitigating the issue because appellant was not "acquitted" of the criminal charges within the meaning of the California Vehicle Code.

 

173 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Gerken v. Fair Political Practices Com.


No. S025815.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


6 Cal. 4th 707; 863 P.2d 694; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 449; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6367; 93 Daily Journal DAR 16121; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9434



December 20, 1993, Decided



DISPOSITION:  Petitioners' application for a writ of mandate is denied. Accordingly, intervener's demurrer is overruled as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, individuals and entities, sought a writ of mandamus from the court (California) that proposition 73 was invalid and proposition 68 was revived by operation of law. An alternative writ directed respondents, commission and board, to cease implementation of proposition 73 and enforce proposition 68, or show cause why a writ should not issue. Respondents did not participate, but intervenors, state and organizations, opposed the petition.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, individuals and entities, sought a writ of mandamus, concerning propositions 73 and 68, which were competing alternative initiatives for campaign contribution reform that were approved by the voters, against respondents, commission and board. Respondents did not participate on the merits, but intervenors, state and organizations, opposed it. Because proposition 73 received the most votes, the court held that only proposition 73 was enforceable. A federal court then enjoined the enforcement of proposition 73's restrictions on campaign contributions and petitioners again came before the court. The court held that the federal injunction did not turn alternative, competing measures into complementary, supplementary measures appropriate for merging. The court noted that proposition 73 contained a severability clause. The court analyzed whether the provisions to be severed were significant and whether the electorate's attention had been sufficiently focused upon the parts to be severed. The court found that the remaining enforceable provisions of proposition 73 were grammatically, functionally, and volitionally separable and valid, and denied the request for a writ.


OUTCOME: The application of petitioners, individuals and entities, for a writ of mandamus against respondents, commission and board, was denied, and the demurrer of intervenors, state and organizations, was overruled as moot. While a federal court restrained enforcement of portions of the proposition, the remaining portions were substantial, enforceable, and valid. The injunction did not transform the propositions into measures appropriate for merger.

People v. Cudjo


No. S006014.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


6 Cal. 4th 585; 863 P.2d 635; 25 Cal. Rptr. 2d 390; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6125; 93 Daily Journal DAR 15794; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9187



December 13, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified February 9, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A746168, Howard J. Schwab, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of death is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California). Defendant used a deadly weapon to commit the murder, and committed the murder while engaged in the commission of robbery and burglary, counts on which he was also convicted. The jury fixed the penalty for the murder at death. The trial court denied the automatic motion to modify the penalty verdict and defendant's appeal was automatic.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder during the commission of a robbery and burglary, with a deadly weapon, and was sentenced to death. He contended that the trial court erred when it excluded evidence that another had confessed to the murder. The ruling was erroneous, but defendant was not thereby prejudiced. The trial court was required to have weighed the evidence's probative value against possible dangers, and no dangers were presented. When it sustained the prosecutor's objection to this evidence, the trial court abused its discretion, but no constitutional violation resulted. The exclusion of the testimony was harmless, because it was not reasonably probable that admission of the testimony would have affected the outcome. Defendant contended his trial counsel was ineffective for failing to have objected to the playing of a tape-recorded interview and for failing to have requested a jury instruction that another's taped statements could not have been considered for the truth of the matters asserted. Defendant also alleged harm from a perfunctory closing argument. However, the court was not persuaded that any of the contentions with regard to counsel caused ineffective assistance.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions for burglary, robbery and murder, and the death sentence, were affirmed. The exclusion of another's testimony, while erroneous, was harmless because it was not reasonably probable that admission would have affected the outcome. All complaints of harm that resulted from counsel's alleged incompetence were without merit, as there was no indication that such actions fell below the required standard.

People v. Bacigalupo


No. S004764, Crim. No 26404.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


6 Cal. 4th 457; 862 P.2d 808; 24 Cal. Rptr. 2d 808; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6009; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9016; 93 Daily Journal DAR 15433



December 7, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at People v. Bacigalupo, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6416 (Cal., Dec. 7, 1993)
Writ of habeas corpus denied In re Bacigalupo, 1994 Cal. LEXIS 2504 (Cal., May 12, 1994)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Bacigalupo v. California, 512 U.S. 1253, 114 S. Ct. 2782, 129 L. Ed. 2d 894, 1994 U.S. LEXIS 5116 (1994)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Bacigalupo (Miguel Angel) on H. C., 2003 Cal. LEXIS 9279 (Cal., Nov. 25, 2003)
Application granted by People v. Bacigalupo (Miguel Angel), 2009 Cal. LEXIS 8107 (Cal., Aug. 12, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 93351, Thomas Charles Hastings, Judge.
Bacigalupo v. California, 506 U.S. 802, 113 S. Ct. 32, 121 L. Ed. 2d 5, 1992 U.S. LEXIS 4947 (1992)



DISPOSITION: We affirm the judgment in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) that convicted him of two counts of first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189 after finding true respondent's special circumstance allegations of robbery-murder and multiple murder. Appellant contested imposition of the 1978 California death penalty law, Cal. Penal Code § 190.3, as being in violation of the U.S. Const. amend. VIII.


OVERVIEW: The state charged appellant with two counts of first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189. The court affirmed the judgment that convicted him under the jury verdict that found to be true respondent's special circumstance allegations of robbery-murder and multiple murder and the jury imposed death penalty. The court determined that the selection factors of the 1978 California death penalty law, Cal. Penal Code § 190.3, which were not used to determine death eligibility were not subject to the U.S. Const. amend. VIII standard used to evaluate death eligibility criteria. The court held that the particular penalty selection factors of § 190.3 that appellant challenged did not violate the U.S. Const. amend. VIII when evaluated against the appropriate standard. The court determined that because the jury properly considered the circumstances of appellant's capital crimes for the purpose of making an individualized determination of the appropriate penalty--after he was already properly determined to be within the narrowed class of death-eligible murderers--there could be no violation of U.S. Const. amend. VIII's prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: After finding true respondent's special circumstances allegations of robbery-murder and multiple murder, the court affirmed the judgment that convicted appellant of two counts of first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code sections. The court affirmed the imposition of capital punishment under the 1978 California death penalty law because the use of its factors did not violate the Eight Amendment of the United States Constitution.

People v. Webb


No. S006938.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


6 Cal. 4th 494; 862 P.2d 779; 24 Cal. Rptr. 2d 779; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6010; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9026; 93 Daily Journal DAR 15448



December 7, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Stay granted by People v. Webb, 1994 Cal. LEXIS 1394 (Cal., Mar. 23, 1994)
Writ of certiorari denied Webb v. California, 513 U.S. 839, 115 S. Ct. 123, 130 L. Ed. 2d 68, 1994 U.S. LEXIS 5852 (1994)
Application denied by, Without prejudice People v. Webb (Dennis Duane), 2011 Cal. LEXIS 10127 (Cal., Sept. 28, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. 14497, Warren C. Conklin, Judge.
People v. Webb, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6417 (Cal., Dec. 7, 1993)



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order from the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California) that convicted him of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187, robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211, and burglary under Cal. Penal Code § 459 and sentenced him to death, having found special circumstances under Cal. Penal Code §§ 190.2(a)(3), (17)(i), and (17)(vii).


OVERVIEW: During a robbery, defendant tormented two victims for a prolonged period of time with their children present and killed them. The trial court convicted him of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187, robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211, and burglary under Cal. Penal Code § 459 and sentenced him to death, having found special circumstances under Cal. Penal Code §§ 190.2(a)(3), (17)(i), and (17)(vii). On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the trial court did not err in denying his motion to change venue or in restricting his discovery of an informant's psychiatric records that were not material to his guilt. Defendant was not denied due process when police failed to preserve a gun later admitted into evidence. No error occurred when fingerprint evidence obtained by a laser procedure was admitted when the reliability of that procedure was manifest at trial, and defendant was not entitled to Miranda warnings prior to discussions with an informant because they were not the product of custodial interrogation. Defendant was also not entitled to counsel during these conversations because he was in custody for another charge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's conviction and death penalty sentence for murder because trial court did not err in rulings on venue change, admission of evidence, and defendant's entitlement to Miranda warnings and counsel during his conversations with an informant.

In re Zacharia D.


No. S028817.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


6 Cal. 4th 435; 862 P.2d 751; 24 Cal. Rptr. 2d 751; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6008; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8969; 93 Daily Journal DAR 15363



December 6, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. J-421268, Gale P. Hickman, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, and the case is remanded to that court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Orange County Social Services Agency challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeal (California) that reversed the judgment of the superior court, sitting as the juvenile court, against appellants, mother and biological father. Respondent contended that appellant father was not entitled to reunification services or custody under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 361.5, 361.2 and that appellant mother's parental rights were correctly terminated.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Orange County Social Services Agency filed a dependency petition on behalf of the minor child of appellants, mother and biological father. The court reversed the court of appeal judgment that reversed the juvenile court termination of appellant mother's parental rights to the minor child in favor of respondent and remanded the cause for further consistent proceedings. The juvenile court properly concluded that the biological father was not a "parent," as defined by the Uniform Parentage Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 7004. Thus he was not entitled to either reunification services or to custody under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 361.5, 361.2 because he delayed before seeking to establish paternity, which rendered § 361.2 inapplicable. He also failed to file the requisite motion to modify under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 388. The juvenile court's implied finding that it was not in the minor's best interests to grant appellant father reunification services or custody was supported by substantial evidence. The juvenile court correctly terminated appellant mother's parental rights based on the proceeding determinations and the fact that respondent mother did not appear in the court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the court of appeal judgment that reversed the juvenile court judgment for respondent Orange County Social Services against appellants, mother and biological father, because he was not a legal parent due to his late paternity claim; thus, he was not entitled to either reunification services or immediate custody when substantial evidence did not exist. Appellant mother's parental rights were correctly terminated.

In re Matthew C.


No. S025565.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


6 Cal. 4th 386; 862 P.2d 765; 24 Cal. Rptr. 2d 765; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6007; 93 Daily Journal DAR 15353; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8963



December 6, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Stanislaus County, No. A-23790, Donald B. Cantwell, Judge, and J. P. Hagan, Referee.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, and that court on remand should address appellant's claims on the merits.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In her case against respondent Stanislaus County Department of Social Services, appellant mother challenged the decision of a court of appeal (California). It held that pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §366.26(k) a juvenile court's order that terminated reunification services and set a §366.26 hearing was not an appealable order, but was reviewable only by extraordinary writ filed before the §366.26 hearing was held.


OVERVIEW: On June 14, 1990, upon a rehearing granted to appellant mother from a Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §366.21 hearing, the juvenile court terminated reunification services and ordered a hearing pursuant to §366.26. On November 5, 1990, the §366.26 hearing was held and the juvenile court terminated appellant's parental rights to her dependant son in favor of respondent Stanislaus County Department of Social Services. Appellant filed a notice of appeal challenging the June 14th order, and the appellate court held that pursuant to §366.26(k) it was not an appealable order and was only reviewable by an extraordinary writ filed before the §366.26 hearing was held. The court reversed and held that the language of §366.36(k) and the rules of appellate procedure did not support the appellate court's conclusion. To the contrary, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §906 provided that orders that affected substantial rights of a party were appealable. The juvenile court's order affected a substantial right of appellant--the termination of her parental rights. The court also stated that the appellate court's conclusion was not supported by any legislative intent or the statute's legislative history.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court that denied appellant mother an opportunity to review a juvenile court order that terminated reunification services and terminated her parental rights. Respondent Stanislaus County Department of Social Services was denied relief because a substantial right of appellant was affected and the legislative intent and history did not bar review.

People v. Brown


No. S010071.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


6 Cal. 4th 322; 862 P.2d 710; 24 Cal. Rptr. 2d 710; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 5814; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8907; 93 Daily Journal DAR 15196

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied February 2, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 444.

Stay granted by People v. Brown, 1994 Cal. LEXIS 2484 (Cal., May 11, 1994)
Writ of certiorari denied Brown v. California, 513 U.S. 845, 115 S. Ct. 138, 130 L. Ed. 2d 79, 1994 U.S. LEXIS 5946 (1994)
Writ of habeas corpus denied In re Brown, 1999 Cal. LEXIS 3768 (Cal., June 3, 1999)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Brown v. Ornoski, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 22310 (9th Cir. Cal., Sept. 19, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. CR18104, J. William Mortland, Judge.
People v. Brown, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 6378 (Cal., Dec. 2, 1993)
People v. Brown, 45 Cal. 3d 1247, 248 Cal. Rptr. 817, 756 P.2d 204, 1988 Cal. LEXIS 151 (1988)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of death reinstated by the Superior Court of Riverside County (California) based on his convictions of rape and first degree murder with special circumstances on the basis that he was denied due process because the judge who presided over the modification hearing under Cal. Penal Code § 190.4(e) was not impartial and thus should have been disqualified under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.1(a)(6)(C).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of rape and first degree murder with special circumstances and sentenced to death. The court affirmed the judgment of death reinstated by the trial court after the trial court denied appellant's application to modify the penalty verdict. The court rejected appellant's due process challenge to the trial court's judgment based on its ruling on his application because that judge's conduct during the modification hearing conducted pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §190.4(e) did not rise to a level of partiality that denied appellant due process or that required disqualification for cause under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.1(a)(6)(C). The court determined that § 170.3(d) did not bar appellate review of appellant's due process challenge because it did not apply to non-statutory claims on appeal from a final judgment that was allegedly constitutionally invalid due to judicial bias. The court determined that the trial court did not violate Cal. Penal Code § 190.4(3) because its review of a supplemental probation report did not cause prejudice to appellant in its ruling under § 190.4(e). The trial court correctly considering his age under Cal. Penal Code § 190.3(I).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's judgment of death based his convictions for rape and first degree murder with special circumstances that was reinstated by the trial court after denying his application to modify the penalty verdict because he was not denied due process when the judge who presided over the modification hearing was impartial and thus was correctly not disqualified, and appellant's challenge was not barred by statute.

People v. Garceau


No. S004776, Crim. No. 26410.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


6 Cal. 4th 140; 862 P.2d 664; 24 Cal. Rptr. 2d 664; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 5806; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8537; 93 Daily Journal DAR 14656



November 18, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 29601, Lewis E. King, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged, through automatic appeal, a judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County (California) convicting him on two counts of first degree murder, as well as the resulting death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and the jury fixed the penalty at death. The jury also found true the allegation of personal use of a knife in the commission of each offense and a multiple-murder special circumstance. On appeal, the court rejected defendant's argument that the prosecution improperly used peremptory challenges to racially discriminate jurors. The court held that defendant failed to establish a prima facie case of discrimination. The court also held that it was not error to reject defendant's motions to excuse three jurors for cause. The court reasoned that defendant failed to show that the denial affected his right to a fair jury. The court held that it was proper for the trial court to excuse a juror who demonstrated that it would be difficult for her to set aside her bias against the death penalty. The court also found certain testimony admissible for probative value under Cal. Evid. Code § 352 because it bore directly upon the issue of defendant's motive to kill the victims. As to the other alleged errors during the penalty phase, the court held that they were few and generally trivial and would not warrant a reversal based upon cumulative error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence, holding that the cumulative effect of the alleged jury-selection errors and trial phase errors did not warrant reversal. The court also rejected defendant's contentions that the cumulative impact of error at the guilt and penalty phases of his trial undermined the reliability of the sentencing process.

People v. Wash


No. S004780, Crim. No. 26414.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA

CASE SUMMARY:

The court also found that a prior burglary conviction was properly utilized because defendant knowingly and voluntarily pled guilty to said charges.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's death sentence and convictions of first degree murder, rape, robbery, and burglary because defendant's rights were not violated by police questioning or witness identification and the prosecutor's remarks referencing God's laws during penalty phase arguments did not affect the jury's verdict of the death sentence.

SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA

August 30, 1993, Decided

The penalty was fixed at death and a motion for modification of the penalty verdict was denied.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and rape and was sentenced to death after the trial court denied the motion for modification of the penalty. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and death sentence for first-degree murder and rape were affirmed. All alleged errors and potential reversible issues raised by defendant were found meritless. The motion for modification of the penalty verdict was properly denied. There was no reversible error in the admission of statements and other evidence, or in the prosecutor's argument.

In re Harris


No. S022130


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


5 Cal. 4th 813; 855 P.2d 391; 21 Cal. Rptr. 2d 373; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 3651; 93 Daily Journal DAR 9723; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5752



July 29, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified September 30, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A629873, Rosemary Dunbar, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed a writ of habeas corpus seeking review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that sentenced petitioner as an adult for criminal acts committed the day before petitioner's sixteenth birthday.


OVERVIEW: On the day before petitioner's sixteenth birthday, petitioner committed the criminal act of homicide. Petitioner was tried and sentenced as an adult. Petitioner filed a writ of habeas corpus. The court granted the writ. The court held that the writ was proper to consider because petitioner's allegations concerned an order that was administered in excess of the trial court's powers. The court first determined that the trial court had proper subject matter jurisdiction because whether a case should proceed in juvenile or adult court did not involve an issue of subject matter jurisdiction. The court then held that the adjudication of petitioner's guilt was proper, as petitioner made no allegations refuting the verdict. However, the court determined that the sentence imposed was improper because petitioner could only be subject to the maximum punishment available for a juvenile. The court held that the common law birthday rule applied to age determination under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 26. The court held that petitioner did not reach the age of 16 until the actual date of his birthday.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the adult criminal sentence imposed on petitioner and remanded because petitioner was a juvenile at the time the criminal acts were committed, as they occurred the day before petitioner's sixteenth birthday.

In re Clark


No. S022475


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


5 Cal. 4th 750; 855 P.2d 729; 21 Cal. Rptr. 2d 509; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 3652; 93 Daily Journal DAR 9761; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5736



July 29, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by In re Clark, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 4964 (Cal., Sept. 22, 1993)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A083960, Harry Mock, Jr., Judge
In re Clark, 1991 Cal. LEXIS 4668 (Cal., Oct. 16, 1991)



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed a second writ of habeas corpus after the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) entered an order sentencing petitioner to death based upon his convictions for murder, attempted murder, arson, and rape.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of first degree murder, two counts of attempted murder, arson, and rape. Petitioner was sentenced to death. Petitioner filed a writ of habeas corpus a year after the convictions, which was denied. Petitioner then filed a second writ. The court denied the second writ. The court found that the writ was untimely and repetitive of prior petitions. The court held that although the general rule was that, absent justification for the failure to present all known claims in a single, timely petition for writ of habeas corpus, successive and/or untimely petitions would be summarily denied, an exception existed for petitions which alleged facts which, if proven, would establish that a fundamental miscarriage of justice occurred as a result of the proceedings leading to conviction and/or sentence. However, the court held that petitioner failed to justify the untimeliness of his writ. The court also held that there was no fundamental miscarriage of justice in the imposition of petitioner's convictions or sentence.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's writ of habeas corpus because petitioner failed to justify the untimeliness of his writ and failed to establish a fundamental miscarriage of justice in the imposition of his convictions or sentence.

People v. Wader

188 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Williams v. Garcetti


No. S024925


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


5 Cal. 4th 561; 853 P.2d 507; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 341; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 3168; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5108; 93 Daily Journal DAR 8571



July 1, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C731376, Ronald M. Sohigian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed with directions to affirm the judgment of the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of the decision of a court of appeals (California), that ruled that an amendment to Cal. Penal Code § 272 was unconstitutional because it was vague and overbroad.


OVERVIEW: Appellants challenged an amendment to Cal. Penal Code § 272 that provided that a parent had a duty of reasonable care, supervision, protection, and control over their minor child arguing that it did not meet constitutional due process requirements because it was vague and overbroad. The court reversed finding that the amendment was neither vague nor overbroad. It concluded that because the language of the amendment incorporated the definitions and the limits of parental duties that had long been a part of California dependency law and tort law, it was not vague. It found that § 272 held parents liable only if they were criminally negligent in breaching their duty of supervision and control. It concluded that although the amendment contained no explicit description of the parental duty, because it incorporated a preexisting definition from tort law, it provided sufficient guidance to police and prosecutors charged with enforcing it, and thereby minimized the danger of arbitrary or discriminatory enforcement so as not to be overbroad.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment ruling the statute unconstitutional. The court held the amendment was not unconstitutionally vague because it provided adequate notice to parents with regard to potential criminal liability for failure to supervise and control their children nor was it overbroad because it was specific enough to allow parents to identify and avoid breaches of the duty of supervision and control for which they could be penalized.

 

189 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Saunders


No. S022903


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


5 Cal. 4th 580; 853 P.2d 1093; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 638; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 3169; 93 Daily Journal DAR 8578; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5101



July 1, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A989753, James B. Pierce, Commissioner.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for burglary ,which was entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), on the ground that the trial court erred when it discharged the jury before the jury had determined the truth of the defendant's alleged prior convictions and impaneled a second jury to make those determinations because doing so violated his right to be free from double jeopardy.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with attempted murder, burglary, and assault with a firearm. Prior to trial, the issue of his prior convictions was bifurcated. The jury found defendant guilty of burglary, and the trial court then discharged the jury. The truth of the alleged prior convictions was latter tried to a new jury, which found the allegations were true, and defendant was sentenced accordingly. Defendant appealed, contending that impaneling of the second jury to determine the truth of the prior conviction allegations violated state statutory law and unconstitutionally placed him twice in jeopardy. The court held that the trial court improperly discharged the jury before the jury had determined the truth of the alleged prior convictions but that defendant did not call this error to the trial court's attention by timely objection; thus, defendant waived any objection. The court concluded that because the proceedings relating to the alleged prior convictions had not yet transpired at the time the trial court discharged the jury, defendant was not twice placed in jeopardy; therefore, the conviction was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction because the bifurcated proceeding did not offend the double jeopardy clause because defendant waived any objection to the trial court's decision to impanel the second jury by failing to object at that time. Moreover, because the proceedings regarding the prior convictions had not begun when the first jury was discharged, defendant was not placed in jeopardy twice.

 

190 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Cahill


No. S020126


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


5 Cal. 4th 478; 853 P.2d 1037; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 582; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 3087; 93 Daily Journal DAR 8304; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4902



June 28, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County No. 76846, Rothwell B. Mason, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed insofar as it holds that the erroneous admission of defendant's involuntary confession automatically required reversal of his murder-related convictions, and the matter is remanded to the Court of Appeal for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeal (California), which reversed the murder-related convictions of defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's murder- related convictions were reversed when the appellate court found that the admission of an involuntary confession compelled the automatic reversal of his convictions. The state petitioned for review, requesting that the court review the issue of whether the state Constitution compelled automatic reversal where a trial court erroneously admitted defendant's coerced confession. During the appeal, the United States Supreme Court overruled a federal line of cases applying the reversible per se standard in involuntary confessions cases, and concluded that the appropriate standard was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. In light of this change by the Supreme Court, the court found that a rule of automatic reversal was not warranted under California law. Thus, the court reversed the judgment of the Court of Appeal insofar as it held that the erroneous admission of defendant's involuntary confession automatically required reversal of his murder-related convictions, and remanded the matter.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment with respect to its holding that the erroneous admission of defendant's involuntary confession automatically required reversal of his murder-related convictions, and remanded the case for further proceedings finding that the prejudicial effect of the erroneous admission of the involuntary confession should be determined under the reasonable-probability test embodied in the California Constitution.

People v. Mayfield


No. S004568, Crim. Nos. 23349, 25196


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


5 Cal. 4th 142; 852 P.2d 331; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 836; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 2494; 93 Daily Journal DAR 6638; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3873

DISPOSITION: One multiple-murder special-circumstance finding is vacated. The judgment otherwise is affirmed entirely and the petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which convicted defendant of two counts of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code §§187, 189. The jury returned a verdict of death and the trial court denied defendant's motion to modify the verdict. Defendant also filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: Defendant received a death sentence for his conviction on two counts of first degree murder, Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189. The court set aside one of the special circumstance findings, but otherwise affirmed the judgment entirely. The state improperly alleged twice the special circumstance of having been convicted of multiple murder in the same proceeding. The court concluded that the refusal to suppress the audio and video versions of defendant's confession was proper because defendant made a knowing and intelligent waiver of his Miranda rights. The court found no reasonable possibility that defendant would have been sentenced to life imprisonment instead of death had errors regarding the prosecutor's mistake in telling the jury to disregard sympathy, the jury's consideration of a duplicative special circumstance of multiple murder, and isolated misstatements of the law in discussing defendant's motion to modify the verdict not occurred. The court denied defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus because the court concluded with regard to both the guilt and penalty trials that defendant did not show prejudice, whatever his trial counsel's deficiencies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against defendant except to vacate one multiple-murder special-circumstance finding because the state improperly alleged the special circumstance twice. Defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus was denied because defendant did not show prejudice from his trial counsel's deficiencies.

 

192 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Johnson v. Calvert


No. S023721


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


5 Cal. 4th 84; 851 P.2d 776; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 494; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 2474; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3739; 93 Daily Journal DAR 6409



May 20, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, Nos. X633190 and AD 57638, Richard N. Parslow, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant surrogate sought review of an order from Court of Appeal for the Fourth District, Division Three (California), which, under the Uniform Parentage Act, Cal Civ. Code §§ 7000-721 (repealed 1994), affirmed the trial court's ruling that respondents, genetic parents, were the natural parents of a child born as a result of a surrogacy contract.


OVERVIEW: Appellant surrogate entered into a contract with respondents, genetic parents, whereby she would gestate a zygote conceived of respondents genetic material. Respondents filed a declaratory judgment action seeking a determination that they were the natural parents of the child. The appellate court affirmed the trial court's holding that they were. On further appeal, the court affirmed, holding that although the Uniform Parentage Act, Cal Civ. Code §§ 7000-721 (repealed 1994), recognized both genetic consanguinity and giving birth as means of establishing a mother and child relationship, when the two means did not coincide in one woman, she who intended to procreate the child and raise as her own, was the natural mother. The court held that the surrogacy contract was not barred by public policy. The court held that the determination that respondents were the natural parents of the child did not deprive appellant of her constitutional rightsas appellant was not exercising her own right to make procreative choices. Rather she agreed to provide a necessary and profoundly important service without any expectation that she would raise the child as her own.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that respondents, genetic parents, were the natural parents of a child gestated by appellant surrogate. Although the Uniform Parentage Act recognized both genetic consanguinity and giving birth as means of establishing a mother and child relationship, when the two means did not coincide in one woman, she who intended to procreate the child and raise as her own, was the natural mother.

 

193 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. King


No. S026827


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


5 Cal. 4th 59; 851 P.2d 27; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 233; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 2472; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3635; 93 Daily Journal DAR 6223



May 17, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. SCR 51040, Craig S. Kamansky, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed to the extent it holds that defendant is ineligible for a CYA commitment. In all other respects, it is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, San Bernardino County (California), convicting him of first degree murder, attempted premeditated murder, and second degree robbery and sentencing him to state prison for 25 years to life.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a minor, was tried as an adult and convicted of first degree murder, attempted premeditated murder, and second degree robbery. The trial court refused to sentence defendant to the state youth authority, under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 1731.5(a), and instead sentenced him to state prison for 25 years to life. Defendant appealed, arguing that the trial court erred in finding that he was ineligible for commitment to the youth authority. The court ruled that under § 1731.5(a), a person who was tried as an adult but was under the age of 21 at the time of apprehension could be committed to the youth authority, rather than sentenced to state prison, unless he was sentenced to death or imprisonment for life. Further, the court held that the punishment for first degree murder of 25 years to life was not a life sentence under § 1731.5(a). The court reversed the judgment to the extent that it held that defendant was ineligible for commitment to the youth authority, and in all other respects affirmed the judgment, concluding that a person convicted of attempted premeditated murder, like the person convicted of the successful crime, was eligible for youth authority commitment.


OUTCOME: Judgment convicting and sentencing defendant was reversed to the extent it held that defendant was ineligible for a youth authority commitment, and in all other respects, it was affirmed where defendant was eligible for youth authority commitment because the punishment for first degree murder of 25 years to life was not a life sentence.

 

194 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Alfredo A. v. Superior Court


No. S024618


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


5 Cal. 4th 1; 849 P.2d 1330; 18 Cal. Rptr. 2d 869; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 2184; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3276; 93 Daily Journal DAR 5644



May 4, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Granted July 15, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 3736. (See 6 Cal. 4th 1212)


PRIOR HISTORY: Ct. App. No. B060056. Super. Ct. No. WJ02036.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner juvenile appealed the order from the Court of Appeal for the Second Appellate District (California), which denied his petition for writ of habeas corpus, treating it as a writ of administrative mandate. Petitioner sought an order requiring respondent juvenile court to make judicial probable cause determinations within 48 hours of a warrantless arrest for an extended juvenile detention.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner juvenile filed a writ of habeas corpus in the appellate court, claiming that he was entitled to a judicial determination of probable cause within 48 hours of his warrantless arrest to support an extended pretrial detention under U.S. Const. amend. IV. Respondent juvenile court held that the 48-hour rule did not apply to juvenile proceedings. The issue was rendered moot as to petitioner, but ripe for determination because it was capable of repetition, yet evading review. The appellate court denied the writ. The court affirmed and held that the constitution only required that a probable cause hearing be held within 72 hours of a juvenile's warrantless arrest. There were fundamental differences between adult and juvenile proceedings, and the statutes governing juvenile detentions protected the minor's rights under the Fourth Amendment while balancing the need for flexibility. The formal detention hearing under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 632(a) could serve to meet the probable cause requirement when held within 72 hours of the arrest. If not, the constitution required a separate, timely, judicial determination of probable cause for an extended detention.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment finding that the statutes governing juvenile procedures withstood constitutional scrutiny. The court held that there were fundamental differences between adult and juvenile detentions, and the 48 hour rule did not apply to juvenile proceedings. The constitution only required that a probable cause hearing be held within 72 hours of a juvenile's warrantless arrest to support an extended detention.

People v. Cummings


No. S004699, Crim. No. 24863


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 1233; 850 P.2d 1; 18 Cal. Rptr. 2d 796; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 2015; 93 Daily Journal DAR 5512; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3146



April 29, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified June 23, 1993.
Rehearing denied by People v. Cummings, 5 Cal. 4th 362A, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 3206, 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4717, 93 D.A.R. 8107 (1993)
Writ of certiorari denied Gay v. California, 511 U.S. 1046, 114 S. Ct. 1576, 128 L. Ed. 2d 219, 1994 U.S. LEXIS 3106 (1994)
Writ of certiorari denied Cummings v. California, 511 U.S. 1046, 114 S. Ct. 1576, 128 L. Ed. 2d 219, 1994 U.S. LEXIS 3108 (1994)
Subsequent appeal at People v. Gay, 2003 Cal. LEXIS 6581 (Cal., Aug. 25, 2003)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Gay (Kenneth) on H.C., 2005 Cal. LEXIS 2304 (Cal., Feb. 15, 2005)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Gay v. Ayers, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 379 (9th Cir. Cal., Jan. 7, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A392702, Dana Senit Henry, Judge.
People v. Cummings, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 2435 (Cal., Apr. 29, 1993)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction of defendant Kenneth Earl Gay is reversed as to counts I, II, III, IV, V, VI, VII, VIII, IX, XII, XIII, XIV, and XVI. That judgment, including the sentence of death, is affirmed in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), convicting them of first degree murder, Cal. Penal Code §189; attempted robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 664/211; robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 211; and conspiracy to commit robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 182/211 and sentencing them to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of first degree murder, Cal. Penal Code § 189; attempted robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 664/211; robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 211; and conspiracy to commit robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 182/211, and they were sentenced to death. Defendants appealed, arguing that they were denied trial before a jury selected from a representative cross-section of the populace, that the court erred in denying their motions for severance and requiring them to stand trial together before separate juries on the murder count, and that their motions for change of venue were erroneously denied. The court ruled that the procedures employed by the jury commissioner and the bases for excusing certain group members, whose excusal was the probable cause of the disparity in the jury venire, were constitutionally permissible. Further, the court held that the use of dual juries was a permissible means to avoid the necessity for complete severance. The court reversed the robbery convictions of one defendant, and affirmed the judgment in all other respects, concluding that defendants failed to show a reasonable likelihood that a fair trial was not had as a result of the denial of a change of venue.


OUTCOME: Judgment convicting defendants was reversed as to the robbery convictions of one defendant and affirmed in all other respects where the prosecutor offered adequate justification for the exercise of peremptory challenges, the state's interest in joinder gave the trial court broad discretion in ruling on a motion for severance, and the order in which jury instructions were given was immaterial.

 

196 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Guiton


No. S026003


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 1116; 847 P.2d 45; 17 Cal. Rptr. 2d 365; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 1180; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1967; 93 Daily Journal DAR 3497



March 18, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Ct.App. No. E008383. Super. Ct. No. CR-34537. Riverside County. James T. Warren, Judge



DISPOSITION: That portion of the judgment of the Court of Appeal reversing the conviction on count one and remanding the matter for resentencing is reversed. In all other respects the judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from a decision of the Court of Appeal (California), which reversed defendant's conviction on a count charging defendant with selling or transporting cocaine and remanded the matter for resentencing but affirmed defendant's conviction on a count charging defendant with possession of cocaine for sale.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged in count one with selling or transporting cocaine and in count two with possession of cocaine for sale. A jury convicted defendant of both counts. The court below reversed the conviction on count one and remanded the matter for resentencing. On appeal, the court held that there existed sufficient evidence of guilt beyond a reasonable doubt on the independently valid ground of transporting cocaine. The court stated that if the inadequacy of proof was purely factual, of a kind the jury was fully equipped to detect, reversal was not required whenever a valid ground for the verdict remained. An appellate court should affirm the judgment unless a review of the entire record affirmatively demonstrated a reasonable probability that the jury in fact found the defendant guilty solely on the unsupported theory. Because the record did not affirmatively demonstrate a reasonable probability that the jury found the defendant guilty solely on the sale theory, the court held that the conviction should not have been reversed. The court reversed the portion of the judgment below reversing the conviction on count one and affirmed the judgment in all other respects.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that portion of the judgment below reversing defendant's conviction on count one and remanding the matter for resentencing. In all other respects the court affirmed the judgment below. Because the record did not affirmatively demonstrate a reasonable probability that the jury found defendant guilty solely on an unsupported theory, defendant's conviction for selling or transporting cocaine should not have been reversed.

People v. Stansbury


No. S004697, Crim. No. 24685


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 1017; 846 P.2d 756; 17 Cal. Rptr. 2d 174; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 869; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1717; 93 Daily Journal DAR 3164



March 8, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Concurring and Dissenting Opinion of March 11, 1993, Reported at:93 Daily Journal DAR 3164. As Modified May 26, 1993. Rehearing Denied May 26, 1993. Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 869. Reversed and remanded Stansbury v. California (1994) 62 U.S.L. Week 4279 [128 L.Ed.2d 293].


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A529247, James H. Piatt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of first degree murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187, lewd act on a child under the age of 14, Cal. Penal Code § 288(b), rape, Cal. Penal Code § 261(2), and kidnapping, Cal. Penal Code § 207. Defendant was sentenced to death after the jury found three special circumstance allegations to be true.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder, lewd act on a child under the age of 14, rape, and kidnapping. He was sentenced to death. On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not make defendant's right to self-representation a mere formality. Defendant personally and actively participated in his own defense, including the examination of witnesses, the decision to hire certain law clerks, the preparation of the jury questionnaire, examination of certain prospective jurors during voir dire, making the opening statement at trial, and submitting numerous pro se motions. The trial court allowed defendant to call his appointed co-counsel at every stage of the proceedings to use him as he saw fit. The trial court was not indifferent to conditions of defendant's confinement during trial, and it held hearings and at times intervened to ensure that conditions were such that he was able to do the work required to represent himself. A statement that defendant made to police that he had driven a turquoise car, which a witness had seen the victim thrown from, was not made while in custody. It was made during an investigation regarding a lead that did not focus suspicion on defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of first degree murder, lewd act on a child under the age of 14, rape, and kidnapping. The trial court did not interfere with defendant's right to represent himself. Further, statements that defendant made to the police before he was Mirandized were made before he was in custody; therefore they were properly used against him.

 

198 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Sherwin-Williams Co. v. City of Los Angeles


No. S026785


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 893; 844 P.2d 534; 16 Cal. Rptr. 2d 215; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 415; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 917



February 4, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BC017727, Ronald M. Sohigian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we reverse the judgment of the Court of Appeal with directions to remand the cause to the superior court with directions to enter judgment in favor of defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city challenged the decision of Division Three of the Court of Appeal for the Second Appellate District (California), which affirmed the issuance of a preliminary injunction prohibiting defendant from enforcing Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 47.11 against plaintiffs, manufacturers and retailers, to the extent that it regulated the retail display of aerosol paint in containers, due to preemption by Cal. Penal Code § 594.1.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, manufacturers and retailers, filed a complaint and petition for writ of mandate against defendant city, attacking Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 47.11. Plaintiffs claimed that the ordinance was preempted by Cal. Penal Code § 594.1, the lower court found preemption and issued a preliminary injunction prohibiting defendant from enforcing the ordinance to the extent that it regulated the retail display of aerosol paint. Defendant appealed, and the appellate court affirmed. On further review, the court reversed, finding that the statute mainly defined the lawful transfer and possession of aerosol paint and required retailers to post a warning against vandalism. By contrast, the ordinance simply regulated the retail display of aerosol paint and marker pens. The court held that the ordinance did not prohibit what the statute commanded or command what it prohibited. The court held that the statute did not exclusively cover the field of the prevention of graffiti, and its scope was therefore limited. The court also held that the statute did not expressly manifest an intent to fully occupy the area and preempt and therefore impliedly intended not to.


OUTCOME: The court reversed with directions to remand the cause to the superior court with directions to enter judgment in favor of defendant city. The court held that the local ordinance was not preempted because the statute did not exclusively cover the field of the prevention of graffiti by covering the field of the retail display of aerosol paint and broad-tipped marker pens in preclusive terms.

 

199 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Smith v. Regents of University of California


No. S006588


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 843; 844 P.2d 500; 16 Cal. Rptr. 2d 181; 1993 Cal. LEXIS 414; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 860



February 3, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified April 15, 1993. Rehearing Denied April 15, 1993, Reported at 1993 Cal. LEXIS 2080.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, Nos. 519977-2 and 533997-6, Charles Zook F. Sutton and Winton McKibben, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed insofar as it holds that the Regents of the University have power to impose and collect a mandatory student activities fee. In all other respects the judgment is reversed. The case is remanded to the Court of Appeal, which shall direct the superior court to conduct further proceedings, and to grant relief, in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff students appealed a decision from the Court of Appeals (California), affirming a decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County in favor of defendant Regents of the University of California and others, in an action challenging defendant's collection and use of a mandatory activities fee as violating U.S. Const. amend. I, Cal. Const. art. I, § 2(a), Cal. Const. art. I, § 3, and Cal. Const. art. IX, § 9(f).


OVERVIEW: The lower court affirmed a judgment of the trial court that collection of a mandatory activities fee by defendant Regents of the University of California was legal. On appeal, plaintiff students contended defendant did not have sufficient authority to collect the mandatory fee. The court rejected that contention because Cal. Const. art. IX, § 9(a) expressly vested defendant with full powers of organization and government over the university. Plaintiffs further contended that income from the mandatory fee was used in several ways that violated their rights to freedom of speech and association. The court held that defendant was required to set a rational limit on the use of mandatory fees. The rules permitted the use of the fees to fund activity that was indisputably political and even "partisan." To protect the rights of dissenting students, defendant was required to identify groups that were ineligible for mandatory funding and offer students the option of deducting a corresponding amount from the mandatory fee. Defendant could not collect from objectors that portion of the mandatory fee that represented the cost of lobbying governmental bodies. The court reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court insofar as it held that defendant Regents of the University of California had power to impose and collect a mandatory student activities fee. In all other respects the court reversed the judgment of the lower court. The court remanded the case to the lower court to direct the trial court to conduct further proceedings, and to grant relief, in accordance with the opinion.

People v. Alcala


No. S004724, Crim. No. 25693


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 742; 842 P.2d 1192; 15 Cal. Rptr. 2d 432; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6138; 93 Daily Journal DAR 119; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 55



December 31, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified March 10, 1993. Rehearing Denied March 10, 1993, Reported at 1993 Cal. LEXIS 1277. Mosk, J., is of the opinion the petition should be granted.
Rehearing denied by People v. Alcala, 4 Cal. 4th 1115A, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 1277, 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1798, 93 D.A.R. 3209 (1993)
Stay granted by People v. Alcala, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 3435 (Cal., June 30, 1993)
Writ of certiorari denied Alcala v. California, 510 U.S. 877, 114 S. Ct. 215, 126 L. Ed. 2d 171, 1993 U.S. LEXIS 5857 (1993)
Related proceeding at Alcala v. Superior Court, 2007 Cal. App. LEXIS 267 (Cal. App. 4th Dist., Feb. 27, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-42861, Donald A. McCartin, Judge.
People v. Alcala, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6434 (Cal., Dec. 31, 1992)



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant murderer sought review from his convictions and death sentence in the Superior Court for Orange County (California) for murder, kidnapping false imprisonment, use of a deadly weapon, and special circumstances in violation of Cal. Penal Code. § § 187,189, 207, 236, 190.2(a) (17) (ii), and, 12022(b) respectively. Defendant challenged his conviction primarily on evidentiary grounds and his death sentence on several different grounds.


OVERVIEW: Defendant murderer was tried twice for offenses related to kidnapping and killing a 12-year old girl who had left her home for a ballet lesson. Her body was discovered more than 40 miles away in a mountain ravine. Apparently, defendant had been hanging around the area trying to pick up other young girls by taking their photographs and then trying to persuade them to go for a ride and smoke some joints. Defendant was also observed in the area where the murder victim was found. Defendant alleged that a multitude of errors entitled him to a reversal of his convictions and, at a minimum, reversal of his death penalty sentence. The court reviewed each of his assertions and found most of them lacking in legal merit. The court did note that some minor errors had occurred during the guilt phase but found that these errors were harmless and had not prejudiced defendant's trial in any fashion. The court noted that none of defendant's assertions of error during the penalty phase had any merit. The court ultimately concluded that defendant had received a fair and untainted trial and that the constitution did not require more.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant murderer's convictions and death sentence for murder, kidnapping false imprisonment, use of a deadly weapon and special circumstances. The court concluded that defendant had received a fair and untainted trial and the constitution did not require more.
